Time saver. Expedite the task by following the recommendation being described.

Alerts reader that the information will help them solve a problem or better understand the subject being described.

1  Introduction
1.1  Preface
This document provides the solution design for Core42’s (C42) Signature Private Cloud Data Center environment. The solution design document describes overall Data Center Infrastructure design for realizing the requirements gathered and documented in Core42 DCN Private Cloud Solution Requirements Document (SRD) and using the reference architecture and high-level design illustrated in Core42 DCN Private Cloud High Level Design (HLD) Document.
1.2  Audience
This document is intended for the following teams:
•    Core42 DC Engineering Manager
•    Core42 Operations Team
•    Cisco CX Team
1.3  Scope
The scope of this document is limited to the following:
•    The list of devices in Private Cloud Environment BoM.
•    The C42 sites listed in the table below.

Table 1 Core42 Site Locations
No.    Site Name
1    Apollo DC: Fabric-AZ1, NWS-AZ1 & BMC Network
2    Iris DC: Fabric-AZ2, NWS-AZ2 & BMC Network
3    Hermes DC: Fabric-AZ3 & BMC Network
1.4  Assumptions
The following assumptions have been made during the development of this document.
Table 2 Assumptions
No.    Assumption    Validated
1    Rack space availability for all network equipment with its required power and cooling.

2  Design Overview
2.1  Solution Overview
Private Cloud environment, part of Signature Cloud project umbrella, is one of the Cloud environments that is aimed to enable multi-tenancy and reconfigurability for different Multi-Tenant workloads leveraging Red Hat OpenStack services on OpenShift (RHOSO) at its foundation. Private Cloud environment, cloud infrastructure platform (RHOSO) spans across 3 data centers (DC) located in UAE. Each DC is treated as Availability Zone (AZ). The 3 AZs (DCs) are named:
•    Apollo (Pod5 Hall A&B AZ1),
•    Iris (Pod9 Hall A&B AZ2)
•    Hermes (Pod5 Hall A&B AZ3)

A fiber ring interconnects these AZs (DCs) with following distance characteristics:
•    Apollo-Hermes is (Pri 76 Kms Sec 76 kms)
•    Apollo-Iris is (Pri 50 Kms Sec 102.5 kms)
•    Iris-Hermes is (Pri 27 Kms Sec 125 Kms)

Figure 1 Data Center Locations


Figure below depicts overall Solution architecture approach for the Private Cloud Environment representing various functional blocks of the overall architecture.

Figure 2 Private Cloud Environment Overall Solution Architecture



The overall architecture is aligned to the multi-tenancy solution hosted on RHOSO platform, where the Tenant workloads and associated storage solutions predominantly reside in Fabric-AZ while the Network-AZs provide controlled entry and exit points to the hosted environment. The Fabric solution provides architectural flexibility to extend networks (L2 or L3) within and across Fabrics. As depicted in figure above, Private Cloud Solution will consist of 5 VXLAN based Fabrics as follows.
Fabric-AZ
a)    Fabric-AZ1
b)    Fabric-AZ2
c)    Fabric-AZ3
NWS-AZ
d)    NWS-AZ1
e)    NWS-AZ2
Both Fabric-AZs and NWS-AZs will be provisioned and managed by Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller (NDFC) solution. NDFC solution consist of 3 x physical Nexus Dashboard (ND) nodes that are physically situated in Management (MGS) block.
2.1.1  Fabric AZ
Each Fabric-AZ (AZ1, AZ2 & AZ3) will form individual VxLAN fabric containing following functional blocks:
•    Management (MGS),
•    Compute (COM),
•    GPU
•    Block Storage (BLK)
•    Object storage (OBJ) & File Storage (FLS) combined

A block is just an abstract term in context to the Fabric hosting functional elements of the underlying platform which work together as pool of resources (Virtual Storage, Virtual Compute,  Virtual network). However, in terms of physical characteristic, each block includes dedicated Leaf and Spine switches.

Each VxLAN fabric will consist of three network layers:
•    Dedicated Leaf & Spine switches - per functional block uplinks to Super Spine / Core Switches
•    Super Spine/Core Switches - common to all blocks and uplinks to Data Center Interconnect (DCI) block consisting of DCI PE routers.
2.1.2  Network Services (NWS) AZ
Network Services (NWS-AZ), present only in AZ1 and AZ2, will form fabric #4 and fabric #5. These fabrics will be composed of Leaf and Spine layers.

The Network Spines will  directly uplink to DCI block over Layer 3. As the name indicates, the Network block serves network connectivity to facilitate communication or traffic from Fabric-AZ1/AZ2/AZ3 to external and various other networks part of the solution.
2.1.3  Out-of-Band (BMC) Block
Out-of-Band management block is a dedicated environment per DC outside of the VXLAN Fabric. The purpose of this block is to provide port-density to all the infrastructure elements (Network, Servers, Storage, Services appliances) with dedicated management/iLO/IPMI ports dedicated path for device access and management separated from control and data planes.

This design document is focused on Private Cloud environment and will expand on the detailed physical and logical design aspects of the Solution architecture.
 
2.2  Architecture Building Block
The high-level building blocks of the Private Cloud environment architecture is depicted in figure below.

Figure 3 Private Cloud Environment Building Blocks

2.3  Architecture Guiding Principles
Following are some key guiding principles governing the DC “Private Cloud” environment design.
•    Hierarchy – specific role of each layer of the network
•    Modular topology - building blocks are concise
•    Simple and scalable design in terms of operations and future expansions
•    Simple operations and maintenance (software upgrades)
•    Isolated fault domains and isolated service domains
•    Load sharing and redundancy on multiple levels
•    Fast convergence
•    Out-of-Band management
2.3.1  Core Layer
•    The Core layer is designed to provide high-speed, low-latency, and highly available connectivity between network blocks within an Availability Zone (AZ) and across AZs via the DCI network.
•    No end host devices are expected to be connected at Core layer.
•    Provides both VxLAN Multi-Site and vrf-lite extensions for a given fabric.
•    Built using 4 x Core switches (N9K-C9364D-GX2A) per AZ

2.3.2  Management Services (MGS) Block
•    Responsible for hosting cloud services (DNS, NTP, others), management and orchestration platforms, mainly the Control Plane functions of RedHat OpenStack Platform (RHOSP) which performs Orchestration of the cloud.
•    MGS block includes  four OpenShift clusters:
1.    Cloud Services - Tenant facing services (cloud console, API, dns, ntp)
2.    Openstack control plane
3.    OPS for infrastructure management
4.    SOC for security tools
•    Provides connectivity for OpenStack control plane/orchestration, storage management/orchestration etc.
•    Following end host devices are expected to connect to the Access Layer switches
•    Firewall Pair in Active/Standby modes
•    Compute / Server nodes
•    Hardware Security Modules (HSM) Thales node
•    Block Storage Nodes (serving Management Block Compute only)
•    NDR (Network Detection and Response) node
•    2 x Palo Alto 3420 Firewalls (Active/Standby) secure and controls traffic entering and exiting the VRFs inside management block.
•    Firewalls are physically connected to MGS Leaf switches and to the BMC Spine switches. Firewalls are placed (logically) in-between inside and outside VRFs within AZ Fabric, thereby providing inter-VRF routing and control, protecting Management block assets.
•    Each firewall will be configured in a single context with different policies controlling and securing traffic between VRFs. VRF instances in Management block will connect to the Firewall over separate logical sub-interfaces.
•    The BMC block will also connect to the management firewall, The BMC block connectivity is mainly to facilitate connectivity requirements (if any) between the iLO/IPMI ports in BMC and control plane segments in MGS block.
•    Built using 2 x Spine Switches (N9K-C9332D-GX2B) and 8 Leaf Switches (N9K-C93180YC-FX3B) per AZ.
2.3.3  Compute (COM) Block
•    Main purpose is to host tenant workloads running on Redhat OpenStack platform.
•    Spans over 20 Racks with total 40 Leaf Switches (N9K-93180YC-FX3B) and 4 Spines (N9K-C9364D-GX2A) for Compute.
•    Compute block consists of approximately 230+ server nodes
•    Each rack consists of 2 top-of-rack (TOR) switches and one BMC switch for devices OOB management
2.3.4  GPU Block
•    Provides accelerated compute resources to tenant workloads that require parallel processing, such as AI/ML, rendering, or HPC.
•    Spans over 12 Racks with total 24 Leaf Switches (N9K-93180YC-FX3B) and 4 Spines (N9K-C9364D-GX2A) for GPU nodes.
•    GPU block consists of approximately 90+ server nodes.
•    Each rack consists of 2 top-of-rack (TOR) switches and one BMC switch for devices OOB management
2.3.5  Block Storage (BLK)
•    Main purpose is to provide storage Volumes for VM workloads
•    This is part of RHOSP environment and consists of 8 Racks and 2 leaf switches per rack (per AZ).
•    Block Storage networks are L2 stretched within AZ.
•    Built using 4 x Spine Switches (N9K-C9332D-GX2B) and 16 Leaf Switches (N9K-C93180YC-FX3B) per AZ.
2.3.6  Object and File Storage (OBJ & FLS)
•    Provides Tenants with access to S3-compatible Object storage and NFS file storage.
•    OBJ networks are L2 stretched within and across AZs
•    Object Storage is external to RHOSP environment and consists of 10 rack per AZ
•    Built using 4 x Spine Switches (N9K-C9364D-GX2A) and 20 x Leaf Switches (N9K-93180YC-FX3B)
•    Out of 10 racks, one rack is dedicated for FLS services for the VM workloads. 2 leaf switches (Nexus 93180YC-FX3B) uplink to 4 x OBJ Spine switches
•    FLS Includes an pair of inter-connect switches (N9K-C9336C-FX2) for providing back-end connectivity to NetApp File storage appliances
2.3.7  Network Services (NWS) Block: (AZ1 and AZ2 Only)
•    Main purpose is to provide external connectivity (North-South) for following blocks/users
•    Compute Block Multi-Tenant VM workloads hosted on Compute/GPU block to external networks (Tenant Remote Office)
•    Object Storage block connectivity to external Tenant Remote Office (DCI)
•    Remote Offices/Customers to Compute Block hosting Tenant VMs (Direct Connect)
•    Platform console (Horizon Dashboard located in MGS block) connectivity from tenant remote office.
•    Each AZ consists of 2 Network Leaf switches (N9K-93180YC-FX3B), 2 Border Leaf/DCGW switches (N9K-93180YC-FX3B), 2 Network Spine switches (N9K-C9332D-GX2B) and 2 x NAT Gateway firewalls (Palo Alto) and 2 x Network WAN Switches (N9K-C93180YC-FX3).
•    NAT Gateway is used to resolve IP address overlapping scenarios such as Tenant users accessing Horizon Dashboard and OBJ from DCI and others.
•    Each NWS AZ includes 3 x network nodes for hosting Central Virtual Routers (CVR) part of the RHOSP solution
2.3.8  BMC Block
•    Provides network port-density for all devices / elements part of the solution with dedicated management ports, iLO/BMC ports that can be managed out-of-band.
•    Dedicated Spines/Aggregation switches (2 x C9500-48Y4C-A) are used for Compute, GPU and Storage block nodes.
•    The BMC access/TOR switches (56xC9300L-48T-4X-E) part of these blocks connect to the dedicated spine switches.
•    The BMC Core switches also provide aggregation functionality for NWS and MGS block BMC TOR switches.
•    ND Cluster nodes data interface (10G) in each AZ shall connect to the BMC Core switches
•    BMC Core switches uplinks to DCI PE routers over 4 x 10G uplinks (crisscross).
•    BMC Core switches also connect to MGS Firewalls physically. This is for facilitating connectivity requirements between Fabric segments and BMC/iLO ports of the server nodes.

2.5  L2 / L3 Extensions
The architecture approach provides capability to extend L2/L3 within and across AZs for the various function blocks of the solution. Refer to High-Level Design (HLD) document on the requirement evolution and context behind for more details.

Table below captures the L2/L3 requirement summary including Gateway placements for the various blocks part of the Fabric and NWS AZs.
Table 5 L2/L3 Extensions Requirement Summary
Block    L2 Extension
MGS (Management)    •    Yes (some subnets are extended across AZs)
Compute     •    Yes (Within AZ & across AZs)
GPU Block    •    Yes (Within AZ & across AZs)
Block Storage    •    Yes (Within AZ)
•    Management and Block Storage blocks within the AZ
Object Storage    •    Yes (Within & across AZs)
•    Compute to OBJ Block L2 extension for File Storage subnets across AZs
Network Servers    •    Yes (L2 extend tenant provider networks in NWS block across AZ1 and AZ2)
BMC    •    No (Local within AZ)

N9K-C9364D-GX2A
•    Cisco N9K-9364D-GX2A is a 2-rack-unit (2RU) switch
•    Supports 51.2 Tbps of bandwidth and 8.35 bpps across 64 fixed 400G QSFP-DD ports.
•    QSFP-DD ports also support native 200G (QSFP56), 100G (QSFP28), and 40G (QSFP+).
•    Each port can also support 4 x 10G, 4 x 25G, 4 x 50G, 4 x 100G, and 2 x 200G breakouts.
•    First 16 ports, marked in green, are capable of wire-rate MACsec encryption
•    Interface Numbering (Eth1/1 to 1/64) and 10G ports numbering (Eth1/65-66)
Info
The switches include port-side intake and rear-side exhaust airflow.

•    Cisco N9K-9364D-GX2A is a 2-rack-unit (2RU) switch
•    Supports 51.2 Tbps of bandwidth and 8.35 bpps across 64 fixed 400G QSFP-DD ports.
•    QSFP-DD ports also support native 200G (QSFP56), 100G (QSFP28), and 40G (QSFP+).

•    Cisco N9K-93180YC-FX3 Switch is a 1RU switch
•    Supports 3.6 Tbps of bandwidth and 1.2 Bpps.
•    48 x downlink ports can support 1-, 10-, or 25-Gbps Ethernet.
•    6 x uplink ports can be configured as 40 or 100-Gbps Ethernet.

Info
The switches include port-side exhaust and rear-side intake airflow.

Figure 13 Cisco N9K-93180YC-FX3 Switch

Interconnect Switches (N9K-C9336C-FX)- Non VXLAN
The FLS block includes a pair of FX2 switches that are not part of the Fabric and is used for the purpose of interconnecting NetApp File Storage appliances over Backend interfaces.
•    Cisco N9K-9336C-FX2 Switch is a 1RU switch
•    Supports 7.2 Tbps of bandwidth and over 2.4 bpps.
•    Switch can be configured to work as 1/10/25/40/50/100-Gbps offering flexible options in a compact form factor.
•    Breakout is supported on all ports.

Info
The switches include port-side exhaust and rear-side intake airflow.

Figure 14 Cisco N9K-9336C-FX2 Switch

3.3  Block Diagrams

Below zoom into each individual, functional block and provide more detailed information on the cabling, and interfaces used.
3.3.1  Compute
Figure below depicts the physical connectivity for Compute Block (AZ1), AZ2 and AZ3 follows the same connectivity design.
Figure 21 Logical - Compute


Figure 22 Physical - Compute


The Compute Block (COM) is running Tenant workloads composed of 20 compute racks. Moreover, Compute block consists of 2 Leaf switches per rack and one BMC switch per rack. In the Compute Block in every AZ, there are a total of 40x N9K-93180YC-FX3B leaf switches with 4x uplink to each of the 4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A spines using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM SFPs. The Compute Block spines use 4x uplinks to 4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A Core/Super Spine switches using QDD-400G-SR4.2-BD which are 400G MM SFPs. Each pair of Compute Leaf switches connect back-to-back using last 4 ports on switch E1/45-48 to accommodate for vPC Peer-links at a speed of 25G using SFP-H25G-CU1M DAC SFPs.
All Compute Block switches (spines and leaf switches) have dedicated management copper port (Mgmt 0) used to connect to BMC switch in respective rack.
3.3.2  GPU
The GPU Block is running GPU nodes composed of 12 GPU racks. GPU block consists of 2 Leaf switches per rack and one BMC switch per rack. In the GPU Block in every AZ, there are a total of 24x N9K-93180YC-FX3B leaf switches with 4x uplink to each of the 4x N9K-C9332D-GX2B spines using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM SFPs. The GPU Block spines use 4x uplinks to 4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A Core/Super Spine switches using QDD-400G-SR4.2-BD which are 400G MM SFPs. Each pair of GPU Leaf switches connect back-to-back using last 4 ports on switch E1/45-48 to accommodate for vPC Peer-links at a speed of 25G using SFP-H25G-CU1M DAC SFPs.

Figure 23 Logical - GPU


Figure 24 Physical - GPU


3.3.3  Block Storage
Figure below depicts the physical connectivity for Block storage (AZ1), AZ2 and AZ3 follows the same connectivity design.

Figure 25 Logical - Block Storage

Figure 26 Physical - Block Storage


Block storage (BLK) is used for providing storage Volumes for VM workloads. Block storage consists of 8 racks with 2 leaf switches per rack in every AZ. Block Storage block in each AZ has a total of 16x N9K-93180YC-FX3B leaf switches with 4x uplinks connecting to each of the 4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A spines using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM SFPs. Block storage spines use 4x 400Gig uplinks to 4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A Core/Super Spine switches using QDD-400G-SR4.2-BD which are 400G MM SFPs. Each pair of Block storage Leaf switches connect back-to-back using last 4 ports on switch E1/45-48 to accommodate for vPC Peer-links at a speed of 25G using SFP-H25G-CU1M DAC SFPs.

All Block storage switches (spines and leaf switches) have dedicated management copper port (Mgmt 0) used to connect to BMC switch in respective rack.

3.3.4  Object Storage
Figure below depicts the physical connectivity for Object Storage Block (AZ1), AZ2 and AZ3 follows the same connectivity design.

Figure 27 Logical - Object Storage


Figure 28 Physical - Object Storage


Object Storage Block consists of 9 racks and 2 leaf switches per rack in every AZ. In the Object Storage Block in every AZ, there are a total of 18x N9K-93180YC-FX3B leaf switches with 4x uplinks which connect to each of the 4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A OBJ spines using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM SFPs. Then, Object Storage Block spines use 4x uplinks to 4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A Core/Super Spine switches using QDD-400G-SR4.2-BD which are 400G MM SFPs. Each pair of Block storage Leaf switches connect back-to-back using last 4 ports on switch E1/45-48 to accommodate for vPC Peer-links at a speed of 25G using SFP-H25G-CU1M DAC SFPs.

All Object Storage Block switches (spines and leaf switches) have dedicated management copper port (Mgmt 0) used to connect to BMC switch in respective rack.

3.3.4.1  File Storage
File Storage is part of OBJ block. Figure below depicts the physical connectivity for File Storage appliances (AZ1), AZ2 and AZ3 follows the same connectivity design.

Figure 29 Logical - File Storage


The File Storage Block (FLS) includes inter-connect switches for providing back-end connectivity to NetApp storage appliances. It consists of two File Storage Leaf switches in each AZ that uplink to OBJ Spines in every AZ. OBJ block in each AZ consists of 2x N9K-93180YC-FX3B leaf switches with 4x uplinks connecting to 4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A OBJ Spines using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM SFPs. Moreover, the pair of File Storage Block Leaf switches connect back-to-back using last 4 ports on switch E1/45-48 to accommodate for vPC Peer-links at a speed of 25G using SFP-H25G-CU1M DAC SFPs. The File Storage Block includes 4x NetApp File AFF C800 hosts which use 16*100G, 32*25G (DAC) for external connectivity.

The File Storage Block switches (2x leaf switches) have dedicated management copper port (Mgmt0) used to connect to BMC switch in respective rack. The inter-connect switches are 2x N9K-C9336C-FX2 in every AZ which uplink directly to BMC in respective rack using dedicated management copper port (Mgmt0).

3.3.5  Network Services (NWS) Block (AZ1 & AZ2)
Figure below depicts the physical connectivity for NWS Block (AZ1) and AZ2 follows the same connectivity design.

Figure 30 Logical - Network Block


Figure 31 Physical - Network Block



The Network Block is responsible for providing external connectivity for Tenant VMs towards Remote office, via DCI links, as well as hosts network nodes (hosting Central Virtual Router) part of RHOSP solution. The Network Block consists of 2 racks hosting 2 Network leaf switches and 2 Border leaf switches per rack in AZ1 and AZ2 only. In the Network Block, there are a total of 2x N9K-93180YC-FX3B Network leaf switches (NWS Leaf) and 2x N9K-93180YC-FX3B Border leaf switches (DCGW). The leaf nodes have dual uplinks connecting to each of the 2x N9K-C9332D-GX2B Network spines using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM SFPs.

The DCGWs in network blocks are connected back-to-back across AZ1 and AZ2 using 4x100Gig links. The links physically connect to DCI routers within the DC and are logically Layer-2 extended across the DCs. Each DCGW provides 10Gig connectivity to the DCI layer for tenant VRF extensions via DCI if required.

A pair of Palo-Alto Firewalls are deployed in NWS block, serving as NAT Gateway. External traffic to Shared services passes through the NAT gateway firewalls, which connect to both the Network Leaf switches and DC Gateways using 4x10G MM fiber links.

The 3x NWS Nodes connect to Network leaf switches using 30x25G MM links, 10x25G for each NW Node. The Network Block spines utilize 4x uplinks to DCI switches using QSFP-100G-FR-S which are 100G SM SFPs. Each pair of Network Block Leaf switches connect back-to-back using last 4 ports on switch E1/45-48 to accommodate for vPC Peer-links at a speed of 25G, using 25G SFPs. All Network Block switches (spines and leaf switches) have dedicated management copper port (Mgmt 0) used to connect to BMC switch in respective rack.
3.3.6  Management (MGS) Block
Figure below depicts the physical connectivity for Management Services (MGS) Block AZ1, AZ2 and AZ3 follows the same connectivity design.

Figure 32 Logical - Management Block

Figure 33 Physical - Management Block


The Management Block hosts the management and orchestration platforms, mainly the Control Plane functions of RedHat OpenStack Platform (RHOSP) which performs Orchestration of the cloud. The Management Block consists of 4 racks with 2 leaf switches per rack in every AZ. In the Management Block in every AZ, there are a total of 8x N9K-93180YC-FX3B leaf switches with 4x uplinks to each of the 2x N9K-C9332D-GX2B MGS spines using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM SFPs., Management Block spines in turn using 4x uplinks to the Core/Super Spine switches (4x N9K-C9364D-GX2A ) using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM SFPs. Each pair of Management Block Leaf switches connect back-to-back using last 4 ports on switch E1/45-48 to accommodate for vPC Peer-links at a speed of 25G using SFP-H25G-CU1M DAC SFPs.

Management Block in each AZ includes a pair of Firewalls (A/S) which are connected to 2 different pairs of Management Block leaf switches using 10G MM links. All Management Block switches (spines and leaf switches) have dedicated management copper port (Mgmt 0) used to connect to BMC switch in respective rack.

Management block in each AZ also includes a 1 x TAP (Shunt) switch (N9K-C93180YC-FX3) installed in the same rack (M3-18) as MGS Leaf03 and Leaf04 switches. The switch connects into TAP Bridge hosted in racks (M3-18 and M4-05) over 8 x SFP-10G-LR-S (SMF). Shunt switch is used to tap traffic between firewall and Leaf. Copy of traffic is sent to shunt switch with 10G-SR SFP.
3.3.7  BMC Block
Figure below depicts the physical connectivity for BMC (OOB) Block. AZ1, AZ2 and AZ3 follows the same connectivity design.

Figure 34 Logical - BMC Block


Figure 35 Physical - BMC Block


The BMC Block provides out of band connectivity for all network devices and servers in DC. One BMC ToR is provisioned in each of the racks in every AZ. The BMC block consists of a total of 56x Catalyst C9300L-48T-4X-E leaf switches, 6x C9500-48Y4C-A spine switches (3 SVL pairs) and 2x C9500-48Y4C-A Core switch (1 SVL switch), in each DC. The leaf switches further grouped as MGS, NWS, GPU, COM, BLK and OBJ block switches. MGS and NWS leaf switches have uplinks connected to Core switches and for other leaf switches the uplinks are connected to spine switches. Each leaf has 2x uplinks which uses SFP-10G-SR-S, which are 10G MM SFPs. Each BMC spine pair connects to Core switch with 2x uplink using SFP-10G-LR-S, which is 10G MM SFPs. Additionally, the Core switch connect to 2x MGS Firewalls using 2x10G links. Each pair of BMC Block spines/core switches connect back-to-back using last 4 ports on switch to utilize 2 ports for SVL Peer-Links at the speed of 100G using QSFP-100G-SR1.2 which are 100G MM, and 2 ports for DAD Links at a speed of 10G using SFP-10G-SR-S which are 10G MM SFPs.
3.3.8  Physical Cabling & Power Considerations
All the fiber connectivity within the DCN layer in an AZ utilizes multi-mode fiber cables. Multi-mode fiber is deployed from each rack to the network racks where the spine layer of that block resides. The fiber connectivity between DCN & DCI layer utilizes single-mode fiber cables. Single mode fiber cables are deployed between DCN network racks and DCI racks for the same.

Each leaf switch is connected to every spine switches in the respective block in a crisscross manner, ensuring redundancy at every stage. Similarly, each spine switch has uplinks to all Core/Super Spine switches, maintaining connectivity even if one path fails (except Network Block Spines and BMC Block Spines which uplink to DCI switches).

Power source redundancy is an essential part of the design. All infrastructure devices are equipped with redundant power supplies, fed by dual power sources, ensuring that a failure at one source does not impact network availability.

Please refer Appendix A: Port-Connectivity section for the connectivity matrix of Private Cloud Fabrics

4  VLAN / IP / Naming Conventions
4.1  Naming Convention
A standardized infrastructure naming convention is paramount to efficient network operations.

Device naming convention should be defined in such a way that by seeing the label or the hostname of the device the network administrator should be able to understand its location & functionality of the device during troubleshooting of identifying a particular device.

For interface description as well, specific standard will be followed.

            All other P2P (Firewall etc) Including NWS    10.92.252.0/23    10.93.252.0/23    10.94.252.0/23
            Routing loopback (Lo0)- Manual    10.92.254.0/24    10.93.254.0/24    10.94.254.0/24

5  VXLAN Fabric Basics
Traditional network segmentation has been provided by VLANs that are standardized under the IEEE 802.1Q group. VLANs provide logical segmentation of Layer 2 boundaries or broadcast domains. However, due to the inefficient use of available network links because of a spanning-tree, coupled with a limited scalability to a 4094 VLANs maximum, it has become a limiting factor for organizations providing data center and cloud multi-tenant services.

As its name indicates, VXLAN is designed to provide the same Ethernet Layer 2 network services as VLAN does today, but with greater extensibility and flexibility. Compared to VLAN, VXLAN offers the following benefits:

•    Higher flexibility in the placement of multi-tenant segments throughout the data center: It provides a solution to extend Layer 2 segments over the underlying shared network infrastructure so that tenant workload can be placed across physical pods, anywhere in the datacenter.
•    Higher scalability to address more Layer 2 segments: VLANs use a 12-bit VLAN ID to address Layer 2 segments, which results in limiting scalability of only 4094 VLANs. VXLAN uses a 24-bit segment ID known as the VXLAN network identifier (VNID), which enables up to 16 million VXLAN segments to coexist in the same administrative domain.
•    Optimized Utilization of available network paths in the underlying infrastructure: VLAN uses the Spanning Tree Protocol for loop prevention, which ends up not using half of the network links in a network by blocking redundant paths. In contrast, VXLAN packets are transferred through the underlying network based on its Layer 3 header and can take complete advantage of Layer 3 routing, equal-cost multipath (ECMP) routing, and link aggregation protocols to use all available paths.

VXLAN operates as an overlay to encapsulate and transmit Layer 2 traffic across a Layer 3 Underlay. The underlay can be any Layer 3 routable network. The scope of the underlay network is only to serve as Layer 3 transport between the VTEP devices. In this case, the typical network design incorporates an underlay consisting of point-to-point Layer 3 connections between the spine and leaf layer utilizing IGP (e.g. OSPF) to distribute loopback IP information between all the leaf nodes. VXLAN utilizes loopback addresses to source and terminate all VXLAN tunnel traffic, which enables Layer 3 ECMP on the underlay to distribute traffic across all the spines. A Layer 3 underlay eliminates Layer 2 Spanning-Tree considerations from the Data Center aggregation.

Figure 37 VXLAN Fabric Basics

5.1  VXLAN Encapsulation and Packet Format
VXLAN is a Layer 2 overlay scheme over a Layer 3 network. It uses MAC Address-in-User Datagram Protocol (MAC-in-UDP) encapsulation to extend Layer 2 segments across the fabric or site. VXLAN supports a flexible, large-scale multitenant environment over a shared common physical infrastructure. The transport protocol over the physical Data Center network is IP plus UDP.

VXLAN defines a MAC-in-UDP encapsulation scheme where the original Layer 2 frame has a VXLAN header added and is then placed in a UDP-IP packet. With this MAC-in-UDP encapsulation, VXLAN tunnels Layer 2 network over Layer 3 network. The VXLAN packet format is shown in the figure below.
Figure 38 VXLAN Packet Format


As shown in the figure above, VXLAN introduces an 8-byte VXLAN header that consists of a 24-bit VNID and a few reserved bits. The VXLAN header together with the original Ethernet frame goes in the UDP payload. For Cisco Nexus NX-OS, the UDP port number is 4789. It complies with IETF standards and is not configurable. The 24-bit VNID is used to identify Layer 2 segments and to maintain Layer 2 isolation between the segments. With all 24 bits in VNID, VXLAN can support 16 million LAN segments.

The additional packet headers add a total of 50 bytes of overhead to each packet.  Based on the VXLAN standard, if a packet is received on an interface that will be encapsulated with VXLAN, the 802.1Q tag is removed from the L2 packet header prior to encapsulation. This mechanism allows for VLAN information to be mapped to a VXLAN VNID on the receiving device and mapped to the same or a different VLAN on the destination device.

The encapsulation header breakdown is presented in the table below.

Table 38 Encapsulation Header Breakdown
Header    Size
L2    14 bytes
VXLAN    8 bytes
UDP    8 bytes
Outer IP    20 bytes
Total Extra Headers    50 bytes

In order to accommodate the encapsulation overhead, the network that carries the VXLAN overlay must support the MTU used by the servers plus 50 bytes. Based on this, in order for servers to utilize a standard 1500 byte MTU, the underlying network must be configured with an MTU >= 1550 bytes. In order to support a server jumbo frame of 9000 bytes, the underlying network must be configured with an MTU >= 9050 bytes.
In Core42 environment, the servers/endpoints will be allowed to use the MTU of 9000 bytes. To accommodate that requirement, all underlay links will be configured with the MTU of 9100 bytes.

Figure 39 VXLAN Dataplane Packet Structure

5.2  VXLAN Packet Forwarding Flow
VXLAN uses stateless tunnels between VTEPs to transmit Layer 2 traffic on the overlay network across the Layer 3 transport underlay network.

Figure 40 VXLAN Packet Forwarding Flow


In the figure above, Host-A and Host-B in VXLAN segment 10 communicate with each other through the VXLAN tunnel between VTEP-1 and VTEP-2. This example assumes that address learning has been done on both sides, and corresponding MAC-to-VTEP mappings exist on both VTEPs.

When Host-A sends traffic to Host-B, it forms Ethernet frames with MAC-B address of Host-B as the destination MAC address and sends them out to VTEP-1. VTEP-1, with a mapping of MAC-B to VTEP-2 in its mapping table, performs VXLAN encapsulation on the packets by adding VXLAN, UDP, and outer IP address header to it.

In the outer IP address header, the source IP address is the IP address of VTEP-1, and the destination IP address is the IP address of VTEP-2. VTEP-1 then performs an IP address lookup for the IP address of VTEP-2 to resolve the next-hop in the transit network and subsequently uses the MAC address of the next-hop device to further encapsulate the packets in an Ethernet frame to send to the next-hop device.

The packets are routed toward VTEP-2 through the transport network based on their outer IP address header, which has the IP address of VTEP-2 as the destination address. After VTEP-2 receives the packets, it strips off the outer Ethernet, IP, UDP, and VXLAN headers, and forwards the packets to Host-B, based on the original destination MAC address in the Ethernet frame.

The traffic flows are thoroughly explained in the "Traffic Flow Types" chapter.

5.2.1  ECMP with VXLAN
5.2.1.1  Standard Hashing
Encapsulated VXLAN packets are forwarded between VTEPs based on the native forwarding decisions of the underlay transport network. A typical VXLAN transport network is a Layer 3 network that uses the standard IP ECMP to balance the traffic load among multiple best paths. To avoid out-of-sequence packet forwarding, flow-based ECMP is deployed. A flow is defined by the source and destination IP addresses and optionally the source and destination TCP or UDP ports in the IP packet header.

This hashing helps ensure that each VXLAN encapsulated flow can be uniquely identified by the five tuples in its outer IP header (source address and port, destination address and port, and protocol). This allows multiple traffic flows sent between the two VTEPs to utilize all available ECMP paths in the underlay network. Multiple flows can take the same path potentially over subscribing the path that is selected. Especially in scenarios where the number of flows is less. If a link fails, all flows are rehashed across the remaining working links which could result in some packets delivered out of order.
5.2.1.2  Resilient Hashing
With Resilient Hashing, if a physical link fails, the flows originally assigned to the failed link are redistributed uniformly among the remaining working links. The existing flows through the working links are not rehashed and hence are not impacted.

Note: At the time of this writing, ECMP resilient hashing is not supported for traffic flows over tunnel
5.2.1.3  Dynamic Load Balancing
Supported from latest 10.5.x release, DLB brings in support to consider the real-time link utilisation for selecting the path. Instead of using only flow hash to select the path, a link with least utilization is selected from the multiple available paths. Available modes are Flowlet and Per-Packet Load balancing.
•    Flowlets are bursts of packets from a flow identified by its 5-tuple (or selected fields from the packet), that are separated by large enough gaps such that they can be routed independently without causing reordering
•    This mode distributes traffic across the available links in a DLB ECMP and helps spread traffic out, reducing network congestion

A use-case where DLB topology is useful is in AI/ML training networks where the number of traffic flows (having unique 5-Tuple IP fields) are fewer and cause sub-optimal use redundant paths. However, for Private Cloud environment, this is not the case and large number of flows are expected considering multi-tenancy hosting environment. In addition, 10.4.5 is recommended release compared to the latest 10.5.x release where the DLB feature is introduced newly. Standard Hashing is considered for the deployment.
6  VXLAN BGP EVPN
6.1  Design overview

The VxLAN EVPN technology will be a fundamental block used to build the Private Cloud environment. The infrastructure will compose of three physical locations interconnected over Layer3 Data Center Interconnect network.

The use of the VxLAN in this design will bring multiple benefits including:
•    Multitenancy.
•    Flexibility to extend network segments between any ports of the environment.
•    Scalability, up to 16 million virtual network identifiers/segments.

The Private Cloud Environment described in this document will be built over 5 VxLAN fabrics interconnected with a VxLAN Multisite technology over DCI infrastructure.

The first three fabrics located respectively in AZ1, AZ2 and AZ3 will host required workloads segregated in the functional network blocks (Management, Compute, Block Storage, Object + File Storage). Remaining two fabrics located in AZ1 and AZ2 will host Network Services Block and will provide the external connectivity for the entire environment.

The logical solution architecture is depicted in the diagram below.

Figure 41 Solution architecture



6.2  Overlay Design
VXLAN was designed to solve specific problems with classic Ethernet by introducing a level of abstraction through encapsulation and providing a standards-based overlay with up to 16 million virtual network identifiers/segments.

The abstraction provided by using VXLAN overlay does not inherently change the flood and learn behaviour of classic Ethernet. In typical VXLAN deployments, BUM traffic is forwarded via layer 3 multicast or head-end replication (HER) in the underlay to facilitate the initial learning process so that subsequent traffic is not flooded.

In order to avoid the data-driven flood and learn behaviour for remote end host learning and VTEP discovery in a VXLAN fabric, a control plane is required to proactively distribute end-host information to participating VTEPs and to perform auto-discovery of VTEPs for a VXLAN virtual segment.

MP-BGP is used as the standards-based control plane for VXLAN overlays. The MP-BGP control plane provides protocol-based end-host reachability information distribution and automated VTEP peer discovery thereby allowing for a more scalable VXLAN overlay design. MP-BGP also introduces a set of features that reduces or eliminates traffic flooding in the overlay network and enables optimal forwarding for both east-west and south-north traffic.

Figure 42 Overlay Design


Above example figure with super-spine architecture reflects the Private Cloud Fabric-AZ environment. NWS Fabrics in AZ1 and AZ2 do not have super spines - the role of the Route Reflector and Rendezvous Point will be performed by the spine switches in these fabrics.

For Core42, each Fabric AZ will use MP-BGP EVPN as an overlay control plane. MP-BGP will be used for distributing end-host IP and MAC information between VTEPs participating in a virtual segment and also for automated VTEP discovery within the fabric.

Figure 43 NWS - Network AZ 1 and AZ2 Overlay Design


Core42 overlay configuration used in all five fabrics which are part of the Private Cloud environment will share common attributes/characteristics which are summarized in the table below:

Table 39 Overlay routing protocol details
Attribute    Value
Overlay routing protocol    iBGP
Address-family    L2VPN EVPN
BGP source/destination IP    Loopback0
Route Reflector location    For AZ Fabrics: RR will be located on four SuperSpines/BGWs
For Network Block Fabrics: RR will be located on two Spines/BGWs
AS number    AZ Fabric-1 :: 65101
AZ Fabric-2 :: 65201
AZ Fabric-3 :: 65301
Network Block Fabric AZ1 :: 65102
Network Block Fabric AZ2 :: 65202
Keepalive/Hold-time timers    60sec/180sec
BFD enabled    Yes
Authentication enabled    Yes
Authentication Key Encryption Type    3 :: 3DES
Authentication Key    <...>
6.3  MP-BGP/EVPN Control Plane
MP-BGP EVPN is a control protocol for VXLAN based on IETF RFC 7432. It provides control-plane and data-plane separation and a unified control plane for both Layer 2 and Layer 3 forwarding in a VXLAN overlay network.

The MP-BGP EVPN control plane offers the following benefits:

•    The MP-BGP EVPN protocol is an industry standard, allowing multivendor interoperability.
•    It enables control-plane learning of end-host Layer-2 and Layer-3 reachability information, enabling organizations to build more robust and scalable VXLAN overlay networks.
•    It uses the decades-old MP-BGP VPN technology to support scalable multitenant VXLAN overlay networks.
•    The EVPN address family carries both Layer-2 and Layer-3 reachability information, thus providing integrated bridging and routing in VXLAN overlay networks.
•    It minimizes network flooding through protocol-based host MAC/IP route distribution and Address Resolution Protocol (ARP) suppression on the local VTEPs.
•    It provides optimal forwarding for east-west and north-south traffic and supports workload mobility with the distributed Anycast function.
•    It provides VTEP peer discovery and authentication, mitigating the risk of rogue VTEPs in the VXLAN overlay network.
•    It provides mechanisms for building active-active multi-homing at Layer-2.

For the VXLAN EVPN Fabrics at Core42 DC, Nexus N9K-C9364D-GX2A super spine switches (cores) software will run MP-BGP EVPN and will be configured to be a part of the VXLAN EVPN control plane as MP-iBGP route-reflectors, distributing MP-BGP EVPN routes among the other MP-BGP EVPN peers/leaf switches (route reflector clients) using the standards-defined constructs. For the Fabrics in the Network Block of AZ1 and AZ2, which do not have super spines - the role of the Route Reflector will be performed by the spine switches.

The Nexus N9K-C9364D-GX2A spine switches in AZ fabrics (not Network Block fabrics) would perform data forwarding by routing VXLAN encapsulated packets based on the outer IP address in the VXLAN header. VXLAN encapsulation and decapsulation functions will not be performed by the spine.

The leaf switches in the VXLAN EVPN Fabric at Core42 DC will be the VTEPs running MP-BGP EVPN and will need to support both the control plane and the data plane functions. In the control-plane, they use MP-iBGP EVPN to advertise their local hosts. They receive MP-iBGP EVPN updates from their peers, i.e., other leaf VTEPs, via the route reflectors and install the EVPN routes in their forwarding tables. For data forwarding, they encapsulate user traffic in VXLAN and send it over the IP underlay network. In the reverse direction, they receive VXLAN encapsulated traffic from other VTEPs, decapsulate it, and forward the traffic with native Ethernet encapsulation toward the host.

6.3.1  MP-BGP EVPN NLRI and L2VPN EVPN Address Family
Like other network routing control protocols, MP-BGP EVPN is designed to distribute network layer reachability information, or NLRI, for the network. The NLRI specified in RFC 7432 can carry a variety of route types. Currently, 12 such types are defined for EVPN. Core42 design will use route types 2, 3, 4, and 5.

Figure 44 VXLAN Underlay


6.3.1.1  Route-Type 2
A unique feature of EVPN NLRI route-type 2 is that it includes both the Layer 2 and Layer 3 reachability information for end hosts that reside in the EVPN VXLAN overlay network. In other words, it advertises both the MAC and IP addresses of EVPN VXLAN end hosts. This capability forms the basis for VXLAN integrated routing and bridging support.

Layer 2 MAC addresses need to be distributed because VXLAN is a Layer 2 extension technology. Unlike a traditional VLAN, which is confined to a specific location in a network that remains within the Layer 2 and Layer 3 boundary, a VNI is a virtual Layer 2 segment in the overlay network. However, from the underlay network’s point of view, the VNI can span multiple non-contiguous sites, reaching beyond the Layer 2 and Layer 3 boundaries of the underlay infrastructure as shown in the figure below. Traffic between end hosts in the same VNI needs to be bridged in the overlay network. Therefore, VTEP devices in a given VNI need to know about other MAC addresses of end hosts in this VNI. Distribution of MAC addresses through BGP EVPN allows unknown unicast flooding in the VXLAN to be reduced.

Info
VXLAN with a MP-BGP EVPN control plane supports MAC address-only routes. The IP information is optional for route-type 2 (MAC and IP address advertisement) and can result in redundant MAC address route objects in the route information base (MAC address advertisement and MAC and IP address advertisement).

RFC 7342 defines a standards-based way of advertising Layer 3 host IP addresses through MP-BGP EVPN so that inter-VXLAN traffic can be routed to the destination end host through an optimal path. For inter-VXLAN traffic that needs to be routed to the destination end-host, host-based IP routing can provide the optimal forwarding path to the destination host. The following is an example of a MAC and IP address host advertisement in MP-BGP EVPN:

Figure 45 EVPN Type 2 NLRI Structure

Figure 46 Type 2 Route


6.3.1.2  Route-Type 5
MP-BGP EVPN can also advertise the IP subnet prefix routes of VNIs, these are type-5 routes. The prefix routes can be used to route traffic to the destination hosts when the host IP routes are missing for instance, when the host IP routes have yet to be learned by the VTEPs through MP-BGP. A VTEP can also advertise the prefix routes outside the VXLAN network if the subnets need to be routable and made known outside the VXLAN network.

Figure 47 EVPN Type 5 NLRI Structure


The EVPN NLRI is carried in BGP using the BGP multiprotocol extension with a new address family called Layer 2 VPN (L2VPN) EVPN. Similar to the VPNv4 address family in the BGP MPLS-based IP VPN (RFC 4364), the L2VPN EVPN address family for EVPN uses route distinguishers (RDs) to maintain uniqueness among identical routes in different VRF instances, and route targets (RTs) to define the policies that determine how routes are advertised and shared by different VRF instances.

A route distinguisher is an 8-bit octet number used to distinguish one set of routes (one VRF instance) from another. It is a unique number prepended to each route so that if the same route is used in several different VRF instances, BGP can treat them as distinct routes. The route distinguisher is transmitted along with the route through MP-BGP when EVPN routes are exchanged with MP-BGP peers.

Route targets can be applied to a VRF instance to control the import and export of routes between this instance and other VRF instances. The route-target attributes for a route are distributed in the form of a BGP extended community attribute, so the BGP configuration on the devices that run MP-BGP EVPN must be enabled to generate or process extended community attributes.

Cisco NX-OS has the flexibility of generating both RD and RT extended communities automatically. This relieves the administrator from the burden of maintaining a documentation list of VNI to RT/RD mapping. This automation also provides an added layer of protection against misconfiguration.

6.3.1.3  Route-Distinguisher Auto
Within Cisco NX-OS, the auto-derived RD is constructed with the IP address of the BGP Router ID as the 4-byte administrative field (RID) and the internal VRF identifier for the 2-byte numbering field (VRF ID).
•    The 2-byte numbering field is always derived from the VRF, but results in a different numbering scheme depending on its use for the IP-VRF or the MAC-VRF:
•    The 2-byte numbering field for the IP-VRF uses the internal VRF ID starting at 1 and increments. VRF IDs 1 and 2 are reserved for the default VRF and the management VRF respectively. The first custom-defined IP VRF uses VRF ID 3.
•    The 2-byte numbering field for the MAC-VRF uses the VLAN ID + 32767, which results in 32768 for VLAN ID 1 and incrementing.

Example auto-derived Route Distinguisher (RD)
•    IP-VRF with BGP Router ID 192.0.2.1 and VRF ID 6 - RD 192.0.2.1:6
•    MAC-VRF with BGP Router ID 192.0.2.1 and VLAN 20 - RD 192.0.2.1:32787
6.3.1.4  Route-Target Auto
Within Cisco NX-OS, the auto-derived Route-Target is constructed with the Autonomous System Number (ASN) as the 2-byte administrative field and the Service Identifier (VNI) for the 4-byte numbering field.

Examples of an auto-derived Route-Target (RT) with 2-byte ASN:
•    IP-VRF within ASN 65001 and L3VNI 50001 - Route-Target 65001:50001
•    MAC-VRF within ASN 65001 and L2VNI 30001 - Route-Target 65001:30001

Info
For IBGP deployment scenarios where are all VTEPs are part of the same BGP domain, the recommended approach is to use the auto-generated route distinguisher and a route target value.
Core42 implementation will use auto generated values of RDs and RTs.
6.3.2  Multi-Tenancy with MP-BGP EVPN
MP-BGP EVPN inherits the support for multi-tenancy with VPN using the VRF construct. Multiple tenants can co-exist and share a common IP transport network while having their own separate VPNs using VRFs in the VXLAN overlay network.

In the EVPN VXLAN overlay network, VXLAN Network Identifiers (VNIs) define the Layer-2 domains and enforce Layer-2 segmentation by confining the Layer-2 traffic to the VNI boundary. Similarly, Layer-3 segmentation among VXLAN tenants is achieved by applying Layer-3 VRF technology and enforcing routing isolation between the tenants using a separate Layer-3 VNI mapped to each VRF instance. Each tenant has its own VRF routing instance. IP subnets of the Layer-2 VNIs for a given tenant are in the same Layer-3 VRF instance that separates the Layer-3 routing domain from the other tenants.

Multi-tenancy is a built-in advantage of VXLAN EVPN and is not supported with VXLAN flood and learn or other Layer-2 extension technologies.

For the VXLAN EVPN Fabric at Core42 multiple VRFs will be used.

6.3.3  MP-BGP EVPN Route Advertisement and Learning
MP-BGP EVPN provides integrated routing and bridging by distributing the Layer 2 and Layer 3 reachability information for the end-host residing in the VXLAN overlay network. The VTEPs perform local learning to learn the MAC and IP information associated with locally attached hosts and then distributes this information through the MP-BGP control plane. This helps avoid network flooding for end-host learning and provides better control over end-host reachability information distribution.

Local learning can be performed by the VTEP for the locally attached hosts using standard data plane learning mechanisms which include learning the source MAC addresses from the incoming Ethernet frames and IP address learning when the hosts send Gratuitous ARP and Reverse ARP packets or ARP request for the gateway IP address on the VTEP.

As exhibited in the figure below, after learning the local MAC and IP information (step1), the VTEP (VTEP-1) then advertises the host information in MP-BGP to distribute it to other VTEPs participating in the VN segment (step2). The other VTEPs on learning the remote host information (step3), install the remote host information to the RIB and the FIB using VTEP-1 as the next-hop for H-MAC-1 and H-IP-1.
Figure 48 MP-BGP EVPN Route Advertisement and Learning


EVPN routes are advertised through the L2VPN EVPN address family. The L2VPN EVPN routes include the following information:
•    RD: Route Distinguisher
•    MAC address length: 6 bytes
•    MAC address: Host MAC address
•    IP address length: 32 or 128
•    IP address: Host IP address (IPV4 or IPV6)
•    L2VNI: VNI of the bridge domain to which the end host belongs
•    L3VNI: VNI associated with the tenant VRF routing instance

Info
The route-targets are advertised as extended community attributes attached to the EVPN route. The receiving EVPN VTEP device will compare the route-target attribute in the received route to its locally configured route-target import policy to decide whether to import or ignore the route.

For locally originated EVPN routes the VTEP uses its own VTEP address as the BGP next-hop for the route. It is apparent that the next-hop remains unchanged through the route-distribution across the fabric because the remote VTEP must learn the originating VTEP address as the next-hop for VXLAN encapsulation.
6.3.4  Symmetric IRB
With Symmetric IRB, the ingress and the egress VTEPs perform Layer 2 and Layer 3 lookup and the ingress VTEP does not need to know the destination VNI for inter-VNI routing. Symmetric IRB introduces the Layer-3 VNI and the VTEP router MAC address. Each tenant VRF is mapped to a Layer-3 VNI on all the VTEPs and this Layer-3 VNI is used to provide the VRF context information on the receiving VTEP for all inter-VXLAN routed traffic.

The VXLAN encapsulated packet for inter-VXLAN routing includes this Layer 3 VNI in the VXLAN header and the receiving VTEP uses this VNI to determine the VRF context in which the inner IP packets need to be forwarded. The VTEP router MAC address is used as the inner destination MAC address for the routed VXLAN packet.

As shown in the figure below, when a packet is to be routed from VNI A to VNI B, the ingress VTEP will first route the packet to the Layer 3 VNI. It will rewrite the ingress destination MAC address to the egress VTEP’s router MAC address and encodes the Layer 3 VNI in the VXLAN header.

At the egress VTEP the received encapsulated VXLAN packet is first decapsulated by removing the VXLAN header and since the destination MAC address in the inner packet header is its own MAC address, it performs a Layer 3 routing lookup for the destination IP address. The Layer 3 VNI in the VXLAN header provides the VRF context in which the routing lookup is performed.
Figure 49 Symmetric IRB


With symmetric IRB, VTEPs do not need to maintain and learn MAC address information for the remote host attached to the egress VNIs for which it does not have local hosts. This results in better utilization of the MAC address and the ARP adjacency table on a VTEP.

Prior to NXOS release 10.2(3)F configuration of each Layer-3 VNI required a consumption of one of the VLANs available on the switch. Starting from NXOS release 10.2(3)F a new Layer-3 VNI configuration mode was introduced which no longer consumes available VLANs.
Core42 implementation will use the new Layer-3 VNI configuration mode for all defined overlay VRFs saving VLAN pool resource for the other purpose.

Summary steps for configuring the new L3VNI mode on the switch:

configure terminal
 vrf context vrf-name
  vni number l3

Then under NVE:
interface nve1
 member vni vni_id associate-vrf

This is sufficient to enable L3 VNI for the given VRF.

Beginning with NDFC release 12.2.1, the Layer 3 VNI without VLAN feature is supported with Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller.

Following is the upper-level process to enable the Layer 3 VNI without VLAN feature in a fabric:
1)    When configuring a new fabric, the Enable L3VNI w/o VLAN field will be checked to enable the Layer 3 VNI without VLAN feature at the fabric level. The setting at this fabric-level field affects the related field at the VRF level, as described below.
2)    When creating or editing a VRF, the Enable L3VNI w/o VLAN field will be checked to enable the Layer 3 VNI without VLAN feature at the VRF level
6.3.5  VTEP Peer Discovery and Authentication in MP-BGP EVPN
Prior to MP-BGP EVPN, VXLAN did not have a control-protocol-based VTEP peer-discovery mechanism or a method for authenticating VTEP peers. These limitations present major security risks in real-world VXLAN deployments because they can easily allow the insertion of a rogue VTEP into a VNI segment. After a rogue VTEP has been inserted into the segment, it can send or receive VXLAN traffic.

With the MP-BGP EVPN control plane, a VTEP device first needs to establish BGP neighbour adjacency with other VTEPs or with iBGP route reflectors. In addition to the BGP updates for end-host NLRI, VTEPs exchange the following information about themselves through BGP:
•    Layer 3 VNI
•    VTEP address
•    Router MAC address

As soon as a VTEP receives BGP EVPN route updates from a remote VTEP BGP neighbour, it adds the VTEP address from that route advertisement to the VTEP peer list. This VTEP peer list then is used as a whitelist of valid VTEP peers. VTEPs that are not on this whitelist are considered invalid or unauthorized sources. VXLAN-encapsulated traffic from these invalid VTEPs will be discarded by other VTEPs.

For data-plane forwarding, a BGP EVPN VTEP accepts VXLAN-encapsulated packets only from VTEP peers that are on the whitelist. Thus, MP-BGP EVPN introduces protocol-based VTEP discovery and the capability to restrict VXLAN overlay traffic distribution to only BGP-learned VTEPs.

Along with the VTEP address that promotes VTEP peer learning, BGP EVPN routes carry VTEP router MAC addresses. Each VTEP has a router MAC address. After a VTEP’s router MAC address is distributed through MP-BGP and learned by other VTEPs, the other VTEPs use it as an attribute of the VTEP peer to encapsulate inter-VXLAN routed packets to that VTEP peer. The router MAC address is programmed as the inner destination MAC address for routed VXLAN.

For additional security, the existing BGP Message Digest 5 (MD5) authentication will be conveniently applied to the BGP neighbour sessions so that switches cannot become BGP neighbours to exchange MP-BGP EVPN routes until they successfully authenticate each other with a preconfigured MD5 Triple Data Encryption Standard (3DES) key. BGP neighbour authentication in MP-BGP EVPN is configured in the same way as previously supported in BGP.

An example is shown below.
Figure 50 Sample Configuration 1


Info
BGP sessions will be authenticated in Core42 environment. However, the sample configuration does not reflect Core42 configuration in any way.

The following example is a sample display of VNI peer status and information in Cisco NX-OS.

Figure 51 Sample Configuration 2


6.3.6  VXLAN EVPN Distributed Anycast Gateway
With MP-BGP EVPN, all VTEP’s in a VNI can be configured to be distributed anycast gateways for end hosts in the VNI IP subnet by supporting the same virtual gateway IP address and the virtual gateway MAC address. With the Anycast Gateway function, end hosts in a VNI can use their local VTEPs as the default gateway for northbound traffic outside of their IP network. This capability ensures optimal northbound packet forwarding from the end hosts in the VXLAN overlay network.

Distributed Anycast Gateways offer seamless mobility. When an end-host moves from one VTEP to another it does not need to send another ARP request to re-learn the gateway MAC address as the gateway IP and virtual MAC address are identically provisioned on all VTEPs within a VNI.

Figure 52 VXLAN EVPN Distributed Anycast Gateway


For the VXLAN EVPN Fabric at Core42 DC, all VNIs on all switches will use the same Anycast Gateway MAC address.

6.3.7  VXLAN EVPN ARP Suppression
ARP suppression in MP-BGP EVPN, reduces the network flooding caused by ARP broadcasts. With ARP suppression configured for a VNI, all the VTEPs in the VNI will maintain an ARP suppression cache with entries for known IP hosts (local and remote) and their corresponding MAC addresses in the VNI segment.

The local VTEP will intercept the ARP request from the end host in the VNI segment and check for the ARPed IP address in its ARP suppression cache table. If there is a match, the local VTEP responds with the cached MAC address for the ARPed IP address on behalf of the remote end host. The local host will learn the remote host MAC address in the ARP response.

If the ARPed IP address is missing in the suppression cache on the local VTEP, the local VTEP will then flood the ARP request to all the other VTEPs in the VNI using the multicast group associated with the VNI. This ARP flooding can occur for the initial ARP request to a silent host in the network.

The VTEPs in the network do not learn about the silent host until it replies to an ARP request with an ARP response. Once the local VTEP learns the MAC and the IP of the silent host, the information is distributed through MP-BGP EVPN control plane to all other VTEPs. The subsequent ARP requests for the silent host need not be flooded.

Since most end-hosts in a VNI segment will be sending out Gratuitous ARP or Reverse ARP requests to announce themselves to the network, the local VTEPs will have the opportunity to learn the local host IP and MAC addresses.

Once the local host IP and MAC addresses are known by the local VTEPs, the local VTEPs will distribute the local host information to all other VTEPs in the VNI segment using MP-BGP EVPN control plane. As a result, most IP hosts in the VNI segment should be learned by the VTEPs through local learning and control-plane based remote learning, reducing the network flooding caused by the host ARP learning behaviour.

Figure 53 VXLAN EVPN ARP Suppression


For the VXLAN EVPN Fabric at Core42 DC, all VNIs with default gateways on the VTEPs in the fabric will be configured with ARP suppression to reduce ARP based network flooding. It is strongly recommended that Core42 to enable ARP suppression wherever it is possible.

6.4  VXLAN Data Plane
6.4.1  Layer 2 and Layer 3 VNI
VRFs configured within the fabric consist of multiple Layer 2 segments, each with a corresponding VNI. These Layer 2 segments are virtual network segments or bridge-domains extended across the VXLAN fabric. The VNIs associated with these Layer 2 segments are referred to as the Layer 2(L2) VNIs. The L2 VNI is used to describe segments for which default gateway is hosted on the fabric leaf switches itself (Anycast Gateway IP) or without default gateway (gateway defined on devices outside of Fabric).

Each VRF within the fabric will also need a unique Layer 3 (L3) VNI defined for symmetric IRB. It is required to enable the routing between the Layer 2 segments within a particular VRF. An individual VTEP can have all or a subset of the Layer-2 VNIs configured but it requires the Layer 3 VNI for inter-VXLAN routing.
Figure 54 EVPN Layer 2 and Layer 3 VNI


For the VXLAN EVPN Fabric at Core42 DC, L3-VNI will be configured on each VTEP where the VRF is provisioned for inter-VNI routing. A subset of Layer-2 VNIs will be configured on each VTEP based on the servers and the applications attached to the VTEPs.

L2 VNI mapping convention used in Core42 will be VNI = 2XYYYY, where
•    X will help to differentiate between local and stretched networks:
o    0 – for stretched networks
o    1 – for AZ1 local networks
o    2 – for AZ2 local networks
o    3 – for AZ3 local networks
•    YYYY – for VLAN ID
L3 VNIs will be mapped 30000 + VRF_ID (in the documentation)

There will be one L3 VNI per VRF and many L2 - one per each VXLAN enabled VLAN.

6.5  Underlay Design
The underlay network provides IP reachability for all the VTEP addresses that are used to route the encapsulated VXLAN packets towards the egress VTEP through the underlay network. The network devices in the underlay network need to maintain routing information only for the VTEP addresses; moreover, they do not need to learn the EVPN routes.
6.5.1  Loopbacks
The underlay should contain several loopback interfaces. The following loopback interfaces will be configured on switches and will be part of the underlay network:
•    Loopback0 - interface used as Router-ID on both spines and leaf switches. Control plane iBGP sessions will be formed between these loopback interfaces.
•    Loopback1 – dedicated and bound to the NVE. The loopback address is assigned to the VTEP using the "source-interface config" command. A secondary address on these loopbacks will be used by VPC as shared VTEP IP.
•    Loopback99 - interface will be configured as the PIM anycast rendezvous point.
•    Loopback100 - interface used as Multi-Site virtual IP address (Multi-Site VIP).

Figure 55 IP Addressing Principles



Configuration Template 1 vPC – VTEP Loopback interface
interface loopback1
  description VTEP loopback interface
  ip address 10.92.255.51/32
  ip address 10.92.250.51/32 secondary

interface nve1
  no shutdown
  host-reachability protocol bgp
  advertise virtual-rmac
  source-interface loopback1

6.5.2  Underlay IGP
OSPF will be used as the underlay IGP to route VXLAN encapsulated packets between the VTEPs and to provide IP reachability between VTEP loopbacks used for MP-BGP adjacencies. IP reachability for the IP multicast function in the underlay network will also be provided using OSPF.

For the VXLAN EVPN Fabric at Core42 DC, all the switch roles in the fabric, i.e., Super Spines/BGW, Spine, Leaf, and Border Leaf, will be configured to be a part of a single OSPF instance (OSPF process UNDERLAY) for underlay routing. All the Layer 3 point-to-point links between the spine and the leaf, including the links to the border leaf switches will be configured in Area 0 (Backbone area) as OSPF network type "point-to-point". The VTEP loopbacks will also be configured to be a part of Area 0.

OSPF will be configured to use Equal Cost Multipath (ECMP) for all learned routes. ECMP helps to achieve load sharing by distributing traffic over all the router network ports that are at the same distance from the destination address. Load-sharing improves the utilization of the network segments, which in turn increases the effective network bandwidth.

Cisco NX-OS supports the Equal Cost Multipath feature with up to 64 equal-cost paths in the OSPF routing table and the unicast Routing Information Base (RIB). Layer 3 ECMP routing is accomplished in hardware in Cisco Nexus 9000 Switches, providing very fast failover and IP-based load-sharing over the equal cost path.

Cisco Nexus 9000 series switches form the source UDP port for a VXLAN traffic flow by hashing the original Layer 2 and Layer 3 packet header. This hashing helps ensure that each VXLAN encapsulated flow can be uniquely identified using the five tuples in its outer IP header. The hashed source UDP port introduces a desirable level of entropy for ECMP load sharing.

For the VXLAN EVPN Fabric at Core42 DC, each VTEP will have two or four (depending on the number of spines available in a given network block) equal-cost paths to the destination VTEP. The fabric will guarantee connectivity to the destination VTEP in the event of a single uplink or a single spine failure.

Figure 57 Underlay IGP



Core42 underlay routing protocols used in all five fabrics which are part of the Private Cloud environment will share common attributes/characteristics which are summarized in the table below:

6.5.3  Underlay Multicast (PIM-ASM and PIM Anycast RP)
MP-BGP EVPN reduces network flooding to a certain extent by using ARP suppression to suppress broadcast traffic from ARP request; however, there is still a requirement to flood and learn for initial ARP learning of silent hosts and a requirement for distribution of multicast, unknown unicast, and broadcast traffic inside a VNI segment. For these traffic types, IP multicast is used in the underlay to reduce the flooding scope to VTEPs and the end-hosts behind the VTEP participating in a VXLAN segment.

Each VXLAN segment is mapped to an IP multicast group in the transport IP network (underlay) and each VTEP participating in the VXLAN segment will independently join the multicast group as an IP host through IGMP (Internet Group Management Protocol). The IGMP joins trigger PIM joins and signalling through the transport network for the particular multicast group and thereby result in the formation of a multicast distribution tree.

Each VTEP device is both a receiver and a source in the multicast group it joins. If a VXLAN segment contains “n” number of VTEP devices, the multicast group for this VNI will have n*(S, G) states and one (*, G) state. The amount of multicast state grows with the number of VTEPs and the number of VXLAN segments in the underlay network. So a group shared by five VTEPs would have five (S, G) mroutes and one (*, G) mroutes. See the image below for reference. This can be reduced by using the same multicast group among VXLAN segments that exist on the same set of leaf switches and would therefore form the same shaped tree. If multiple virtual networks (say, VNIs 30000 and 30001) are assigned to the same group 239.1.1.1, and there are VTEPs that serve only end hosts of 30000 and not 30001, those VTEPs will still receive multicast traffic for 30001’s end-hosts, but drop those packets if the Layer 2 segment is not instantiated. This is because all VTEPs that subscribe to a multicast group need to receive traffic for that group.

You could also configure PIM (S, G) SPT (Shortest Path tree) suppression for multicast groups associated with the VXLAN segments.
Figure 59 PIM-ASM - S,G for 5 VTEP


As of this writing, the Nexus 9k scale guide states that 512 multicast groups are supported along with 8000 mroutes. The limitation is in the number of mroutes. We must carefully evaluate the number of VTEPs times multicast groups we are likely to need and decide on a multicast group design—1:1 Multicast-to-VNI mapping or 1:N Multicast-to-VNI mapping—so that the limitation on the number of mroutes is not exceeded for the life of the switches.

For the VXLAN EVPN Fabric at Core42 Data Center, the underlay fabric will be configured with PIM-ASM (Any Source Multicast) and "Anycast RP using PIM" RFC 4610 based implementation to support multicast routing for multicast groups associated with the VXLAN segments. You can use "PIM Anycast RP" to assign a group of routers, called the "Anycast RP set", to a single unicast RP address that is configured on multiple routers. This method is the only RP method that supports more than one RP per multicast group, which allows you to load balance across all RPs in the set. The "Anycast RP" supports all multicast groups.

PIM register messages from the VTEPs are sent to the closest RP, and PIM join-prune messages are sent in the direction of the closest RP as determined by the unicast routing protocol (OSPF) in the underlay. If one of the RPs goes down, unicast routing ensures these messages will be sent in the direction of the next-closest RP. You must configure PIM on the loopback interfaces that are used for "PIM Anycast RP".

The Nexus N9K-C9364D-GX2A Super-spine switches (or N9K-C9332D-GX2B spines in case of NWS fabrics)  will be configured to use this implementation of "Anycast RP" by adding both spine switches to the "Anycast RP set" for each multicast group and thereby allowing to load-balance across the four super-spines (two spines in case of NWS fabrics). The underlay fabric will be configured with PIM sparse mode on the Layer 3 point-to-point links between the leaf switches, including border leaf switches, and the spine switches. Static RP assignment will be configured on each VTEP with the Anycast RP address of the spine for the range of multicast group associated with the VXLAN segments.
Figure 60 Underlay Multicast

Info
Multiple VXLAN segments can share the same multicast group.
