6.6  VPCs
Core42 will accomplish Layer 2 multi-chassis link aggregation using VPCs. A pair of Virtual Port Channel (VPC) switches function as a logical VTEP device sharing an anycast VTEP address. For reference, see the figure below.
Figure 62 Shared Anycast VTEP in a VPC Pair


The VPC switches provide redundant host connectivity and redundant connectivity to routers, switches, and service chassis attached to the VPC pair using virtual port channels, while individually running Layer 3 protocol with the upstream devices in the underlay network.
6.6.1  System Infra VLAN
For VXLAN and VPC deployments, it is essential to maintain Layer 3 adjacency when a VXLAN VTEP (vPC switch) loses its underlay uplink connectivity. The command system nve infra-vlans <vlan-id> designates a VLAN as an infra-VLAN and is used to serve this purpose. This VLAN provides a local, un-stretched backup path between vPC peers, ensuring continued reachability to the spine and fabric. When the primary underlay uplinks to the spines are lost, traffic can still be forwarded correctly through this infra-VLAN over the peer-link.

system nve infra-vlans 900
!
interface Vlan900
  description VPC-Peer-Link SVI
  no shutdown
  mtu 9100
  no bfd echo
  no ip redirects
  ip address 10.92.243.204/31
  no ipv6 redirects
  ip ospf message-digest-key 10 md5 3 2dcdbac1880b74aa
  ip ospf network point-to-point
  ip router ospf UNDERLAY area 0.0.0.0
  ip pim sparse-mode
!
6.6.2  Secondary Address
VPC VTEP switches use a secondary IP address on the loopback interface associated with the VTEP NVE interface as the anycast VTEP address. The two VPC VTEP switches need to have the same secondary loopback address configured and advertised in the underlay network so that the upstream devices learn the /32 route from both the VPC VTEPs and load-share VXLAN encapsulated VXLAN traffic between them. For reference, see the figure below.
Figure 63 Sample Anycast Configuration for VPC Configuration


The other VTEPs in the network see the two switches as a single VTEP with the anycast VTEP address. When both the vPC VTEP switches are up and running, they load share in an active-active configuration. If one vPC switch goes down, the other switch takes over the entire traffic load so that the failure event doesn’t cause loss of connectivity for the devices connected to the vPC pair.

The MP-BGP EVPN control plane in Cisco NX-OS works transparently with vPC VTEP. With an MP-BGP EVPN control plane, vPC VTEPs continue to function as a single logical VTEP with the anycast VTEP address for VTEP functions, but they operate as two separate entities from the perspective of MP-BGP. They have different router IDs for BGP, form BGP neighbour adjacency with the BGP peers separately, and advertise EVPN routes independently. In the EVPN routes, they both use the anycast VTEP address as the next-hop so that the remote VTEPs can use the learned EVPN routes and encapsulate packets using the anycast VTEP address as the destination in the outer IP header of encapsulated packets.
Figure 64 Logical VPC Device on Separate VPC Peers


VPC peers must have consistent configurations, including a consistent VLAN to vn-segment mapping, the same NVE to loopback binding using the same secondary IP address on the loopback, and consistent vn-segment to group mapping. Like NX-OS VPC pairs, a peer-link is required. MACs and local ARPs are synchronized. However, routing tables are not synchronized.

For multicast, the VPC node that receives the (S, G) join from the RP (rendezvous point) becomes the DF (designated forwarder). On the DF node, encap routes are installed for multicast.

Decap routes are installed based on the election of a decapper from between the VPC primary node and the VPC secondary node. The winner of the decap election is the node with the least cost to the RP. However, if the cost to the RP is the same for both nodes, the VPC primary node is elected. The winner of the decap election has the decap mroute installed. The other node does not have a decap route installed.

On a VPC device, BUM traffic (broadcast, unknown-unicast, and multicast traffic) from hosts is replicated on the peer-link. A copy is made of every native packet and each native packet is sent across the peer-link to service orphan-ports connected to the peer VPC switch.

To prevent traffic loops in VXLAN networks, native packets ingressing the peer-link cannot be sent to an uplink. However, if the peer switch is the encapper, the copied packet traverses the peer-link and is sent to the uplink.

Info
With default configuration vlan tags of 4041 and 4046 allocated from the internal vlan range are used for BUM traffic replication within VPC Pair in VxLAN EVPN environments.

When the peer-link is shut, the loopback interface used by NVE on the VPC secondary is brought down and the status is Admin Shut. This is done so that the route to the loopback is withdrawn on the upstream and that the upstream can divert all traffic to the VPC primary.

Info
Orphans connected to the VPC secondary will experience loss of traffic for the period that the peer-link is shut. This is similar to Layer 2 orphans in a VPC secondary of a traditional VPC setup.

When the peer-link is no-shut, the NVE loopback address is brought up again and the route is advertised upstream, attracting traffic.

vPC within VxLAN EVPN environments support a vPC fabric peering configuration, which means the requirement to have a physical link providing connectivity between the two leaf switches, is no more required.
VxLAN implementation at Core42 will implement vPC in a traditional way with physical peer-link in place

The VPC peer-gateway feature must be enabled on both peers. For peer-gateway functionality, at least one backup routing SVI is required to be enabled across peer-link and also configured with PIM. This provides a backup routing path in the case when VTEP loses complete connectivity to the spines (all uplinks from a particular leaf go down). Remote peer reachability is re-routed over the peer-link in this case.

The following is an example of SVI with PIM enabled:
Configuration Template 2 SVI with PIM Enabled – Example
feature pim

interface Vlan900
  description VPC-Peer-Link SVI
  no shutdown
  mtu 9100
  no bfd echo
  no ip redirects
  ip address 10.92.243.204/31
  no ipv6 redirects
  ip ospf message-digest-key 10 md5 3 2dcdbac1880b74aa
  ip ospf network point-to-point
  ip router ospf UNDERLAY area 0.0.0.0
  ip pim sparse-mode

Info
The SVI must be configured on both VPC peers and requires PIM to be enabled.

As a best practice, use peer-switch, peer gateway, ip arp sync, ipv6 nd sync configurations for improved convergence in VPC topologies. In addition, If external L2 switches connects to the Leaf switches, then STP hello timer can be increased to 4 seconds to avoid unnecessary TCN generations when VPC role changes occur.

The following is an example (best practice) of a VPC configuration:
Configuration Template 3 VPC Configuration Example
feature vpc

vpc domain 6
  peer-switch
  peer-keepalive destination 10.92.208.52 source 10.92.208.51
  delay restore 250
  peer-gateway
  auto-recovery reload-delay 360
  ipv6 nd synchronize
  ip arp synchronize

On a VPC pair, shutting down NVE or NVE loopback on one of the VPC nodes is not a supported configuration. This means that traffic failover on one-side NVE shut or one-side loopback shut is not supported.

On a vPC enabled leaf or border leaf switch, by default all Layer 3 routes are advertised with the secondary IP address (VIP) of the leaf switch VTEP as the BGP next-hop IP address. Prefix routes and leaf switch generated routes are not synced between vPC leaf switches. Using the VIP as the BGP next-hop for these types of routes can cause traffic to be forwarded to the wrong vPC leaf or border leaf switch and black-holed. There is a provision to allow users to select the primary IP address (PIP) as the BGP next-hop when advertising these types of routes from VPC peers so that traffic will always be forwarded to the right vPC enabled leaf or border leaf switch.

Configuration Template 4 Advertise Private IP Address
interface nve1
  advertise virtual-rmac

router bgp 65101
  address-family l2vpn evpn
    advertise-pip

VMAC (virtual-mac) is used with VIP and system MAC is used with PIP when the VIP/PIP feature is enabled. With the "advertise-pip" and "advertise virtual-rmac" commands enabled, type 5 routes are advertised with PIP and type 2 routes are still advertised with VIP. In addition, VMAC will be used with VIP and system MAC will be used with PIP.

Core42 should enable the "advertise-pip" and "advertise virtual-rmac" commands.

The "advertise-pip" and "advertise-virtual-rmac" commands must be enabled and disabled together for this feature to work properly. If you enable or disable one and not the other, it is considered an invalid configuration.

Figure 65 Example Topology Configuration

      redistribute direct route-map fabric-rmap-redist-subnet
      maximum-paths 4
      maximum-paths ibgp 4

evpn
  vni 210205 l2
    rd auto
    route-target import auto
    route-target export auto

At Core42 fabrics "advertise-pip" and "advertise-virtual-rmac" commands will be configured.

Finally, in the vPC configurations, the delay restore timer should be set. After a vPC peer device reloads and comes back up, the routing protocol needs time to reconverge. The recovering vPCs leg may black-hole routed traffic from access to core until Layer 3 connectivity is re-established.

The vPC Delay Restore feature delays vPCs leg bring up on the recovering vPC peer device. vPC Delay Restore allows for Layer 3 routing protocols to converge before allowing any traffic on vPC leg. The result is a more graceful restoration and zero packet loss during the recovery phase (traffic still gets diverted on the alive vPC peer device).

This feature is enabled by default with a vPC restoration default timer of 30 seconds. The timer can be tuned according to a specific Layer 3 convergence baseline from 1 to 3600 seconds.

The following are the recommended configurations.

At Core42 the delay restore timer of 250sec was determined fit best the network specifics.

6.6.3  vPC – Primary Peer Switch
Configuration Template 7 vPC – Primary Peer Switch
vpc domain 10
  peer-switch
  peer-keepalive destination 10.92.208.52 source 10.92.208.51
  delay restore 250
  peer-gateway
  auto-recovery reload-delay 360
  ipv6 nd synchronize
  ip arp synchronize

6.6.4  vPC – Secondary Peer Switch

vpc domain 10
  peer-switch
  peer-keepalive destination 10.92.208.51 source 10.92.208.52

  peer-gateway

6.7  Firewall attachment to Fabric
There are several ways to attach external services to a VXLAN fabric.  In the case of Core42 environment we will be facing two scenarios:
•    NAT Gateway attachment to the fabric – in NWS fabrics, there will be transparent NAT Gateway (Virtual Wire Mode) between NWS-DCGW and NWS-LEAF devices.
•    MGS firewalls will be an A/S pair of firewalls; physically the devices are connected to two different VPC pairs. This will require eBGP between Active/Standby Firewalls Connected to Two Different VPC Pairs.

In this chapter we are going to focus on NATGW Layer 3 attachment scenarios. Please refer to Section 8.3 for the A/S pair of firewalls connected to two different VPC pairs scenario.
6.7.1  NAT Gateway attachment to the fabric
In this scenario, only shared service addresses are advertised outside – so chosen services can be reached out from Remote Office. Incoming traffic from Remote Office lands in NWS fabrics on DCGW (NWS-DCGW-01 / NWS-DCGW-02) devices which is then routed over eBGP towards NWS Leaf switches (NWS-LEAF-01/ NWS-LEAF-02).

In between DCGW and Leaf switches, there is NAT Gateway device which translates remote subnets to tenant specific NAT pool when accessing shared services. This Nat Gateway is transparent to the NWS Leaf switches and DCGW switches and eBGP is established between the DCGW and NWS-Leaf switches.

Figure 66 NWS Routing Design- Shared Services Access


Following approach will be applied for the traffic:
•    eBGP between Tenant VRF (DCGW side) and Infra VRF (NWS Leaf side) via NAT Gateway (virtual-wire mode)
•    NAT Pool advertised to Infra VRF from DCGW switches
•    Shared Service Subnets advertised to Tenant VRF from INFRA VRF
•    BGP Metric (AS-Path Prepend) changed so that INFRA VRF takes AZ1 NAT-GW to reach Shared Services
•    Source address gets NATed to AZ1 NAT pool as traffic traverses AZ1 NAT Gateway
•    Return traffic reaches AZ1 NAT GW from where AZ1 NAT Pool is advertised

Figure 67 NWS Routing Design- Shared Services Access


In this part of the document, configuration design choices of the showcased network is expanded and described in detail in section 8.3 of this document.

7  Multi-Site VxLAN EVPN
VXLAN EVPN Multi-Site architecture is a design for VXLAN BGP EVPN–based overlay networks allowing the interconnection of multiple distinct VXLAN BGP EVPN fabrics or overlay domains. And it allows new approaches to fabric scaling, compartmentalization, and DCI.

When building one large data center fabric per location, various challenges related to operation and failure containment exist. By building smaller compartments of fabrics, you improve the individual failure and operation domains. Nevertheless, the complexity of interconnecting these various compartments precludes the pervasive rollout of such concepts, specifically when Layer 2 and Layer 3 extension is required.

Private Cloud Environment leverages VXLAN EVPN MultiSite architecture allowing interconnection of multiple Fabrics or overlay domains. Fabric AZs in the architecture hosts the Multi-Tenant VM workloads while the Network Services (NWS) Fabrics provide resilient and controlled entry and exit points into the environment.

Figure 68 Private Cloud Environment Multisite (Compartmentalization)


VXLAN EVPN Multi-Site architecture provides integrated interconnectivity that doesn’t require additional technology for Layer 2 and Layer 3 extension. It thus offers the possibility of seamless extension between these 5 fabrics within Private Cloud environment. It also allows you to control what can be extended and what cannot. In addition to defining which VLAN or Virtual Routing and Forwarding (VRF) instance is extended, within the Layer 2 extensions, you can also control broadcast, unknown unicast, and multicast (BUM) traffic to limit the ripple effect of a failure in one data center fabric.

When you build networks using the scale-up model, one device or component typically reaches the scale limit before the overall network does. The scale-out approach offers an improvement for data center fabrics. Nevertheless, a single data center fabric also has scale limits.

In addition to the option to scale out within a single fabric, with EVPN Multi-Site architecture, you can scale out in the next level of the hierarchy. Similar to adding more leaf nodes for capacity within a data center fabric, with EVPN Multi-Site architecture, you add fabrics (sites) to horizontally scale the overall environment. With this scale-out approach in EVPN Multi-Site architecture, in addition to increasing the scale, you can contain the full-mesh adjacencies of VXLAN between the VXLAN tunnel endpoints (VTEPs) within a pod.
Figure 69 Scale Example

7.1  Border Gateway (BGW)
The main functional component of the EVPN Multi-Site architecture is the border gateway, or BGW. BGWs separate the fabric-side (site-internal fabric) from the network that interconnects the sites (site-external DCI) and mask the site-internal VTEPs.

Commonly, an EVPN Multi-Site deployment consists of two or more sites, which are interconnected through a VXLAN BGP EVPN Layer 2 and Layer 3 overlay. In this scenario, the BGW is connected to the site-internal VTEPs (usually through spine nodes) and to a site-external transport network that allows traffic to reach the BGWs at other, remote sites. The BGWs at the remote sites have site-internal VTEPs behind them. Only the underlay IP addresses of the BGWs are seen inside the transport network between the BGWs. The site-internal VTEPs are always masked behind the BGWs.

Figure 70 EVPN Multi-Site Deployment


From a BGW perspective, the role of the site-internal VTEPs is to share the common VXLAN and BGP-EVPN functions. To interoperate with a BGW, a site-internal node must support the following functions:
•    VXLAN with Protocol-Independent Multicast (PIM) Any-Source Multicast (ASM)
•    BGP EVPN Route Type 2 and Route Type 5 for the overlay control plane
•    Route reflector capable of exchanging BGP EVPN Route Type 4
•    VXLAN Operations, Administration, and Maintenance (OAM)–capable devices for end-to-end OAM support

From the point of view of the site-external network, no specific requirements are demanded apart from IP transport reachability between the BGWs and accommodation of an increased Maximum Transmission Unit (MTU) packet size. The BGWs always use Ingress Replication (IR) for Layer 2 BUM traffic between BGWs in different sites, but they can use PIM within a given site. This capability provides flexibility for existing deployments and transport independence for the site-external network.

Private Cloud environment will have the Super Spines carrying out the BGW function. The Fabric-AZ BGWs are connected to the spines just like any other VTEP in the fabric (site-internal VTEPs). BGWs are connected to the DCI PE routers (site-external interfaces). The NWS-AZs Spine switches performs the BGW role in addition to Spine and connect directly into the DCI PE Routers.

Figure 71 Multi-site Connectivity

7.1.1  Anycast Border Gateway
The Anycast BGW (A-BGW) performs the BGW function as described above. The A-BGW allows the scaling of the BGWs horizontally in a scale-out model and without the fate sharing of inter-device dependencies. As of Cisco NX-OS 7.0(3)I7(1), the A-BGW is available on the Cisco Nexus 9000 Series cloud-scale platforms (Cisco Nexus 9000 Series EX/FX/GX platforms), with up to six Anycast BGWs available per site.

Figure 72 Anycast Border Gateway


The name "A-BGW" refers to the sharing of a common Virtual IP (VIP) address or anycast IP address between the BGWs in a common site. This document uses the virtual IP address to refer also to the EVPN Multi-Site anycast IP address.

The virtual IP address on the BGW is used for all data-plane communication leaving the site and between Private Cloud Fabric sites the EVPN Multi-Site extension is used to reach a remote site. The single virtual IP address is used both within the site to reach an exit point and between the sites, with the BGWs always using the virtual IP address to communicate with each other. The virtual IP address is represented by a dedicated loopback interface associated with the Network Virtualization Endpoint (NVE) interface ("multisite border-gateway interface loopback100").

With this approach, and with the existence of an Equal-Cost Multipath (ECMP) network, all BGWs are always equally reachable and active for data-traffic forwarding. The underlay transport network within or between the sites is responsible for hashing the VXLAN traffic among the available equal-cost paths. This approach avoids polarization, given the entropy of VXLAN, and it increases resiliency. If one or more BGWs fail, the remaining BGWs still advertise the virtual IP address and hence are immediately available to take over all the data traffic. The use of anycast IP addresses or virtual IP addresses provides network-based resiliency, instead of resiliency that relies on device hellos or similar state protocols.

In addition to the virtual IP address or anycast IP address, every BGW has its own individual personality represented by the primary VTEP IP (PIP) address ("source-interface loopback1"). The PIP address is responsible in the BGW for handling BUM traffic. Every BGW uses its PIP address to perform BUM replication. Therefore, every BGW has an active role in BUM forwarding. Like the virtual IP address, the PIP address is advertised to the site-internal network as well as to the site-external network. The PIP address is used to handle BUM traffic between BGWs at different sites, because EVPN Multi-Site architecture always uses ingress replication for this process.

The PIP address is also used in two additional scenarios that are closely related. If the BGW is providing external connectivity with VRF-lite in addition to the EVPN Multi-Site deployment, routing prefixes that are learned from the external Layer 3 devices are advertised inside the VXLAN fabric with the PIP address as the next-hop address. From the BGW’s point of view, these externally learned IP prefixes are considered to originate locally from a BGW, using the BGP EVPN address family. This process creates an individual BGP EVPN Route Type 5 (IP prefix route) from every BGW that learned a relevant IP prefix externally. In the best case, your site-internal network has an ECMP route to reach non-EVPN Multi-Site networks.

Info
External learned IP prefixes will be redistributed to BGP EVPN from BGP IPv4 unicast and controlled using filtering. One of the use-case is the external connectivity from Fabric-AZ BGW to DCI PE OBS-BE VRF for the OBJ backend replication connectivity. This is detailed in the logical design section of this document.
7.1.2  RFC9014 compatibility
By default a multisite infrastructure deployed by Cisco is based on the RFC draft draft-sharma-bess-multi-site-evpn. It is a requirement from Core42 to ensure possible interoperability of the Border Gateway switches with a third party BGWs as described in RFC9014.

The configuration required to ensure that interoperability is ensured on the environment during the initial deployment of the multisite. The command required to ensure the RFC9014 interoperability is "dci-advertise-pip" and needs to be configured on the BGW switches. Additionally a default bgp behaviour of putting only best route into the routing table is modified to allow for ECMP.

The configuration of "dci-advertise-pip" changes the way in which L2VPN EVPN paths are advertised between BGWs.
•    With default configuration – without "dci-advertise-pip", the L2VPN EVPN paths are advertised towards remote BGWs using a shared multisite-anycast loopback IP as the next hop. This results with a single route available in L2VPN EVPN address-family and multiple ECMP paths available for next-hop address in the underlay network.
•    With "dci-advertise-pip" present in the configuration, the L2VPN EVPN paths are advertised towards remote BGWs using an individual PIP address of each BGW as the next hop. This results with a multiple ECMP routes available in L2VPN EVPN address-family (as many as there are BGWs in each site) and multiple ECMP paths available for next-hop address in the underlay network.
7.1.3  Designated Forwarder
Every A-BGW actively participates in the forwarding of BUM traffic. Specifically, the Designated-Forwarder (DF) function for BUM traffic is distributed on a per-Layer 2 VXLAN Network Identifier (VNI) basis. To synchronize the designated forwarders, BGP EVPN Route Type 4 (Ethernet segment route) updates are exchanged between the BGWs within the same site.

Figure 73 Designated Forwarder


To participate in the designated-forwarder election, the configuration of the same site ID is required. This ID is defined as part of the BGW configuration ("evpn multi-site border-gateway <site-id>"). In addition to the site ID, the use of the same Layer 2 VNI is needed to elect the designated forwarder from among the eligible BGWs.

The designated-forwarder assignment is performed on a per-Layer 2 VNI basis, using a round-robin process to distribute assignments equally. An ordinal list of PIP addresses is used, and based on all the Layer 2 VNI order of configuration or ordinal list, the designated-forwarder role is distributed in a round-robin fashion.

Info
Every BGW will have an active designated-forwarder role if the number of Layer 2 VNIs exceeds the number of BGWs.

Table below lists the Site-ID to be used for the Private Cloud Environment Fabrics leveraging MultiSite interconnection. Note that Multi-Site ID (ms-id) must be the same in all BGWs within the same fabric/site. The range of values for ms-id is 1 to 2,814,749,767,110,655.

Table 45 Site-IDs for Private Cloud Environment Fabrics
SL    DC Site    Fabric name    Site-ID
1    Apollo    aead3-a1-fab-01    101
2    Iris    aead5-a1-fab-02    102
3    Hermes    aead7-a1-fab-03    103
4    Apollo    aead3-a1-nws-01    111
5    Iris    aead5-a1-nws-02    112

To exchange the designated-forwarder election messages between the BGWs, BGP EVPN peering is required because the election messages consist of BGP EVPN Route Type 4 advertisements. Most naturally, the BGW would peer with a site-internal (fabric) route reflector, which also has all the endpoint information from within the site-internal VTEPs. BGWs in Private Cloud environment also performs the route-reflector functions.  With the route reflector already present in the fabric, and with all VTEPs, the exchange of designated-forwarder election messages is achieved.

Figure 74 Designated Forwarder Election Using Route Reflectors


The BGP EVPN Route Type 4 exchange should occur only through site-internal peering.
Figure 75 EVPN Type 4 NLRI Structure


In Private Cloud design, the following devices are used as BGWs.
•    Fabric-AZ: 4 x Super Spines / Core Switches: Nexus 9364D-GX2A
•    NWS-AZ: 2 x Spine switches: N9K-C9332D-GX2B

There will be a common VIP configured on interface loopback 100. The VIP is for communications between the Border Gateways and Leaf switches within a Site. There will be an individual Primary IP (PIP) per BGW configured on interface loopback 1 that will be used for communication between the sites. The PIP is also used for Broadcast, Unknown Unicast, and Multicast (BUM) replication.

Core42 will use the BGW-to-cloud topology model. The BGW-to-cloud model has a redundant Layer 3 core or DCI-PE routers between the different sites. In this deployment model, the Layer 3 DCI PE routers provides to each site redundant connectivity points to which the BGWs can connect.

The only specific requirements for the Layer 3 core are that it provide IP connectivity between the PIP addresses of the BGWs and accommodate the MTU for the VXLAN-encapsulated traffic across the cloud. Multiprotocol-BGP (MP-BGP) peering with VPN address families is supported only as part of the default VRF instances.

7.2  Underlay and Overlay
The main functional component of the EVPN Multi-Site architecture consists of the BGW devices. Their deployment affects the way that the overlay network performs its Layer 2 and Layer 3 services. Given that stability is of paramount importance for the overlay, proper design of the underlay network is critical.

For EVPN Multi-Site architecture, numerous best practices and recommendations have been established to successfully deploy the overall solution. Core42 will deploy the I-E-I model, which focuses on an Interior Gateway Protocol (IGP) and iBGP (IGP-iBGP)–based site-internal network (fabric) with eBGP-eBGP at the external site.

In addition to choosing the underlay routing protocols, you must separate the site-internal and site-external routing domains. In the case of I-E-I, the underlays will not likely be redistributed between the "I" (IGP) and the "E" (eBGP) domains.

The following sections present the main design principles for successfully deploying the EVPN Multi-Site architecture with the BGW-to-cloud topology.
7.2.1  Multi-Site Underlay (Site External)
EBGP is typically used to provide reachability between the BGWs of multiple sites, given its interdomain nature. For BUM replication between sites, EVPN Multi-Site architecture exclusively uses ingress replication to simplify the requirements of the site-external underlay network.

Figure 77 BGW with Site-External Topology


The sample configuration for a BGW with a site-external eBGP underlay is shown here using an example of aead3-a1-core-01. This configuration is just for understanding purpose, the IP address and interface names will not match the actual deployment.

7.2.2  Multi-Site Overlay (Site External)
The site-external overlay for VXLAN BGP EVPN must use eBGP, because the eBGP next-hop behaviour is used for VXLAN tunnel termination and origination.

In the case of EVPN Multi-Site architecture, a site-internal MAC address or IP prefix advertisement originates from the local BGWs VTEP IP address used as the next hop. Similarly, the BGWs of the local site receive a MAC address or IP prefix advertised from remote BGWs with their VTEP IPs as the next hop. This behaviour follows eBGP’s well-known and proven process of changing the next hop at the autonomous system boundary.

EVPN Multi-Site architecture uses eBGP not only for VXLAN tunnel origination and termination but also for its loop prevention mechanism offered through the as-path attribute. With this approach, on the control plane, prefixes originating at one site will never be imported back into the same site, thus preventing routing loops. On the data plane, designated-forwarder election and split-horizon rules complement the control-plane loop-prevention functions.

The configuration for a BGW with a site-external eBGP overlay is shown here using an example of aead3-a1-core-01.

Configuration Template 10 BGW with a Site-External eBGP Overlay
feature bgp     Enable feature bgp for underlay IPv4 unicast routing.
feature nv overlay
nv overlay evpn     Enable feature nv overlay for the VXLAN VTEP capability.
Extend VXLAN with EVPN (nv overlay evpn).
evpn multisite border-gateway 101
  dci-advertise-pip
  delay-restore time 300     Define the node as an EVPN Multi-Site BGW with the appropriate site ID.
Note: All BGWs at the same site must have the same site IDs (site ID 101 is shown here).
As a sub configuration of the BGW definition, a time-delayed restore operation for BGW virtual IP address advertisement can be set.
interface nve1
  host-reachability protocol bgp
  source-interface loopback1
  multisite border-gateway interface loopback100    Define the NVE interface (VTEP) and extend it with EVPN (host-reachability protocol bgp).
Define the loopback1 interface as the NVE source interface (PIP VTEP).
Define the loopback100 interface as the EVPN Multi-Site source interface (anycast and virtual IP VTEP).
router bgp 65101
  router-id 10.92.254.11

  neighbor 10.92.254.100
    remote-as 65000
    password 3 <hidden>
    update-source loopback0
    ebgp-multihop 5
    peer-type fabric-external
    address-family l2vpn evpn
      send-community
      send-community extended
      rewrite-evpn-rt-asn

  neighbor 10.93.254.100
    remote-as 65000
    password 3 <hidden>
    update-source loopback0
    ebgp-multihop 5
    peer-type fabric-external
    address-family l2vpn evpn
      send-community
      send-community extended
      rewrite-evpn-rt-asn

  neighbor 10.94.254.100
    remote-as 65000
    password 3 <hidden>
    update-source loopback0
    ebgp-multihop 5
    peer-type fabric-external
    address-family l2vpn evpn
      send-community
      send-community extended
      rewrite-evpn-rt-asn    Define the BGP routing instance with a site-specific autonomous system.
Note: The BGP router ID matches the loopback0 IP address.
Configure the neighbor with the EVPN address family (L2VPN EVPN) for the site-external overlay control plane facing the route server or remote BGW (peering to a pair of route servers is shown here as used in Core42 deployment.).
Configure the eBGP neighbor by specifying the source interface loopback0. This setting allows underlay ECMP reachability from BGW loopback0 to route-server loopback0.
Note: Site-external EVPN peering is always considered to use eBGP with the next-hop the remote site BGWs.
With the route server potentially multiple routing hops away, you must increase the BGP session Time-To-Live (TTL) setting to an appropriate value (ebgp-multihop).
In defining the site-external BGP peering session (peer-type fabric external), rewrite and reorigination are enabled.
The autonomous system portion of the automated route target (ASN:VNI) will be rewritten upon receipt from the site-external network (rewrite-evpn-rt-asn) without modification of any configurations on the site-internal VTEPs. The route-target rewrite will help ensure that the ASN portion of the automated route target matches the destination autonomous system.
7.2.3  Route-Server
There are several options for connecting border gateway devices, the most popular ones being back-to-back and via backbone route server. Two sites can be connected either way but for more than two sites route server is required to avoid full mesh of BGWs.

Note
Route-Server option is decided for Private Cloud Environment Multi-Site connectivity between BGWs.

This option provides following advantages over the full-mesh option.
•    Lower number of eBGP peering to maintain
•    Easy addition to new sites if setup expands
•    Central point for easy application of EVPN route filtering between sites if needed

The autonomous system portion of the automated route target (ASN:VNI) will be rewritten upon receipt from the site-external network (rewrite-evpn-rt-asn) without modification of any configuration on the site-internal VTEPs. If a route server stands in between the BGWs of the individual sites, an additional rewrite to the destination autonomous system is performed. The route-target rewrite helps ensure that the ASN portion of the automated route target matches the destination autonomous system.
7.2.3.2  Route-Target Rewrite
In VXLAN EVPN, Cisco NX-OS uses an automated route-target derivation in which a prefix is followed by a 2-byte Autonomous System Number (ASN). The suffix of the route target is populated with the VNI, which has a total size of 4 bytes. The prefix portion with the ASN is derived from the BGP instance that is locally configured on the respective node, and the VNI is derived from either the Layer 2 or Layer 3 configuration and its use depends on whether a MAC or IP address import must be performed.

7.2.3.3  Sample Route-Target Prefix and Suffix
When the MP-BGP and VPN address families are used, the route-target defines what is imported into a given VRF instance. The route-target is defined based on the export configuration of the VRF instance in which the prefix was learned. The route-target is attached to the BGP advertisement as an extended community to the prefix itself. At the remote site, the import configuration of the VRF instance defines the route-target extended community that is matched and the information that is imported.

Table 49 Sample Route-Target Prefix and Suffix
Prefix    Suffix
2-byte ASN    4-byte VNI
65501    50000

In EVPN Multi-Site architecture, each site is defined as an individual BGP autonomous system. Thus, with the use of automated route targets, the configurations of the VRF instance and the route-target extended community potentially diverge. For instance, if the local site uses ASN 65501 and the remote site uses ASN 65520, the route targets will be misaligned, and no prefixes learned from the control plane will be imported.

To allow the site-internal configuration to use the automated route-target and require no change to any VTEP, the rewriting of the autonomous system portion on the route-target must be possible because the export route-target at the local site must match the import route-target at the remote site. In EVPN Multi-Site architecture, the route target can be rewritten during ingress at the remote site.

The autonomous system portion of the route-target will be rewritten with the ASN specified in the BGP peering configuration. This action allows, for example, route-target 65501:50000 at the local site to be rewritten as 65520:50000 upon receipt of the BGP advertisements at the BGW of the remote site. If a route server is between the BGWs, an additional route-target rewrite must be performed on the route server. In this case, for example, route-target 65501:50000 at the local site can be rewritten as 65036:50000 on the route server and then as 65520:50000 at the remote site. This example assumes a symmetric VNI deployment (the same VNI across sites).

This approach enables successful export and import route-target matching by using automated route-target derivation with route-target rewrite. Neither the existing VTEP configuration or the static route-target configuration needs to be changed.

The route-target rewrite function is performed on the EVPN Multi-Site BGW facing the site-external overlay peering.

As of Cisco NX-OS 7.0(3)I7(1), automated route-target derivation and route-target rewrite are limited to a 2-byte ASN. This limitation is a result of the route-target format (ASN:VNI) used, which allows space for a 2-byte prefix (ASN) with a 4-byte suffix (VNI). In cases in which a 4-byte ASN is required, you can use common route targets across sites.
7.2.4  Site-Internal Underlay (Fabric)
OSPF is used to provide reachability between the intra-site VTEP (leaf), the spine, and the BGWs.
For BUM replication, multicast (PIM ASM) is used.

7.3  Per-Tenant Configuration
Previous sections discussed EVPN Multi-Site design scenarios and underlay and overlay configurations. This section explores the configurations needed for the VNIs, for either Layer 2 or Layer 3 extension. This section also discusses how to limit the extension, from either the control plane (selective advertisement) or the data plane (BUM enforcement).

This section begins by exploring the name-space mapping for VNIs and the use of VNIs across multiple sites with EVPN Multi-Site architecture.
7.3.1  Symmetric VNI
EVPN Multi-Site architecture allows the extension of Layer 2 and Layer 3 segments beyond a single site. Using EVPN Multi-Site architecture, you can extend Layer 2 VNIs to enable seamless endpoint mobility and address other use cases that require communication bridged beyond a single site. Use cases involving Layer 3 extension beyond a single site primarily require multitenant awareness or VPN services. With the multitenant capability in BGP EVPN and specifically in EVPN Multi-Site architecture, multiple VRF instances or tenants can be extended beyond a single site using a single control plane (BGP EVPN) and a single data plane (VXLAN).

All the use cases for EVPN Multi-Site architecture have the name space provided by VXLAN—the VXLAN network identifier, or VNI—as a central feature. This 24-bit name space, with about 16 million potential identifiers, is an integral part of VXLAN and is used by VXLAN BGP EVPN and EVPN Multi-Site architecture.

As of Cisco NX-OS 7.0(3)I7(1) for the Cisco Nexus 9000 Series EX- and FX-platform switches, all deployed sites must follow a consistent assignment of VNIs for either Layer 2 or Layer 3 extension. Therefore, a VLAN or VRF instance at the local site must be mapped to the same VNI that is used at the remote site. This consistent mapping is called symmetric VNI assignment. Subsequent releases will expand this capability to enable asymmetric VNI assignment, in which different VNIs can be stitched together at the BGW level.
7.3.2  Selective Advertisement
With the use of Layer 2 and Layer 3 extension to facilitate endpoint mobility, the boundaries of hierarchical addressing are non-existent. Thus, an individual endpoint’s MAC address and host IP address must be seen within a site or across sites whenever bridging communication is required. The host IP address is not especially important for the bridging itself, but it is needed to provide optimal routing between endpoints. To help ensure that endpoints in different IP subnets can communicate without hair-pinning through a remote site, knowledge of the /32 and /128 host routes is crucial.

EVPN Multi-Site architecture not only facilitates these Layer 2 and Layer 3 extension use cases, but it also provides ways to optimize such environments, building hierarchical networks even when Layer 2 extension is needed. EVPN Multi-Site selective advertisement limits the control-plane advertisements on the BGW depending on the presence of per-tenant configurations. If a VRF instance is configured on the BGW to allow a multitenant-aware Layer 3 extension, the data plane is configured, and control-plane advertisement in BGP EVPN is enabled. With this approach, only after the VRF instance is configured and associated with the VTEP is the relevant IP host and IP subnet prefix information advertised to the site-external network. The same approach is followed for Layer 2 extension and MAC address advertisement, with advertisements sent to the site-external network only after the Layer 2 segment has been configured and associated with the VTEP.

These advertisement control functions are provided simply to keep the site-external network manageable and to prevent saturation of the control-plane tables with unnecessary entries. In addition, if VRF route-target imports are configured unintentionally, the selective advertisement approach helps preserve hardware table space on the BGW and even on the VTEPs beyond it.

Selective advertisement is implicitly enabled. Control-plane advertisements are limited based on the local VRF and VNI configurations on the BGWs.
7.3.3  Layer 3 Extension
The configuration to enable Layer 3 extension through an EVPN Multi-Site BGW closely follows the configuration for a normal VTEP. However, for an EVPN Multi-Site BGW, no endpoint-facing Layer 2 or Layer 3 configuration is defined. All the per-tenant configuration settings for Layer 3 are provided solely to allow VXLAN traffic termination and re-encapsulation for transit through the BGW. The configuration used for the BGW transit functions also facilitates the selective advertisement control explained in the previous section.

Info
All BGWs at a given site must have the same configurations for Layer 3 extensions.

Core42 setup will utilize a new L3VNI configuration mode. In this mode there is no need to use one of the available vlans for each L3VNI. All available vlans can now be used to provide L2 segments.

Configuration Template 17  Layer 3 Extension
vrf context Core42
vni 50001 l3
rd auto
address-family ipv4 unicast
route-target both auto
route-target both auto evpn
address-family ipv6 unicast
route-target both auto
route-target both auto evpn     Define a VRF context (IP VRF) with the appropriate instance name.
The Layer 3 VNI chosen refers to the vn-segment ID that will be used to provide routing capability within a vrf.
The route distinguisher for the IP VRF instance can be derived automatically by using the router ID followed by the internal VRF ID (RID:VRF-ID). Similarly, the route target can be derived automatically by using the BGP autonomous system followed by the VNI defined as part of the VRF instance (ASN:VNI). The route targets must be enabled for the IPv4/IPv6 address family and specifically for EVPN.
info
The use of the automated route distinguisher and route target is optional, but it is a best practice.

interface Vni5000     (optional) Define a Layer 3 interface if any specific configuration is required (eg. PBR). There are no plans to use this configuration at Core42 at this point in time.
interface nve1
member vni 50001 associate-vrf     Associate the Layer 3 VNI with the NVE interface (VTEP) and associate it with the VRF type.

In addition to configuring the Layer 3 extension, you may need to add the VRF information in the configuration of the BGP instance. This step is mandatory if external connectivity for locally connected devices is required. This functionality will be required at Core42 due to the fact that BGWs will not only provide multisite extension, but also vrf-lite connectivity towards the DCI.
7.3.4  Layer 2 Extension
As with Layer 3 extension, the configuration to enable Layer 2 extension through an EVPN Multi-Site BGW is similar to the configuration used for a normal VTEP. However, for an EVPN Multi-Site BGW, no endpoint-facing Layer 2 or Layer 3 configuration is defined (that is, no distributed IP Anycast Gateway). All the Layer 2 configuration settings are provided solely to help ensure VXLAN traffic termination and re-encapsulation for transit through the BGW only. The configuration for Layer 2 extension also promotes selective advertisement beyond the BGW.

All BGWs for a given site must have the same configuration for Layer 2 extensions.
Configuration Template 18  Layer 2 Extension
vlan 10
vn-segment 30010     Define the Layer 2 VNI and attach it to a BGW local VLAN.
Note: The VLAN ID has no significance for any endpoint-facing function. It is a resource allocation setting only.

member vni 30010
multisite ingress-replication
[ingress-replication protocol bgp]
[mcast-group 239.1.1.0]     Associate the Layer 2 VNI with the NVE interface (VTEP) and configure the relevant site-internal and site-external BUM replication modes (dual mode).

route-target import auto

The Layer 2 VNI chosen refers to the vn-segment ID chosen in the previous step.
The route distinguisher of the MAC VRF instance can be derived automatically by using the router ID followed by the internal VRF ID (RID:VRF-ID). Similarly, the route target can be derived automatically by using the BGP autonomous system followed by the VNI defined as part of the VRF instance (ASN:VNI). The route targets must be enabled for the IPv4/IPv6 address family and specifically for EVPN.
Note: The use of an automated route distinguisher and route target is optional, but it is a best practice.

Info
For all of the Cisco Nexus 9000 Series switches which support BGW functionality, local endpoint connectivity is not supported on an EVPN Multi-Site Anycast BGW.
7.3.5  BUM Traffic Control
Layer 2 extension is a common use case. It is also a scenario in which failure replication is largely exposed. To provide a safer approach for Layer 2 extension, EVPN Multi-Site architecture allows you to control Layer 2 BUM traffic leaving the local site. EVPN Multi-Site architecture uses separate flood domains for site-internal and site-external traffic. This approach allows you to filter traffic between the flood domains. It also introduces split-horizon rules to help ensure that traffic entering the BGW from one flood domain does not return to the same flood domain. If BUM traffic reaches the BGW from the site-internal network, forwarding is allowed only to the site-external network, and if BUM traffic reaches the BGW from the site-external network, forwarding is allowed only to the site-internal network.

EVPN Multi-Site architecture allows selective rate limiting for BUM traffic classes that are known to saturate network infrastructure during broadcast storms, loops, and other traffic-generating failure scenarios. The BGW provides the capability to enforce these traffic classes individually through a rate limiter. Only traffic leaving the local site following termination and re-origination within the BGW will be enforced. The BUM enforcement takes place before the traffic is re-originated on the BGW for transmission to a remote site.

On the Cisco Nexus 9300 Series switches, the classification and rate limiting are applied globally to each BGW. The configured rate-limiting level represents the amount of BUM traffic allowed from each interface that faces the site-external network.
Configuration Template 19  BUM Traffic - rate limit
evpn storm-control broadcast level 10
evpn storm-control multicast level 10
evpn storm-control unicast level 10     Define storm control for EVPN Multi-Site Layer 2 extension. The percentage can be adjusted from 0% (block all classified traffic) to 100% (allow all classified traffic).
Info
The classification and use of storm control for EVPN Multi-Site architecture is comparable to that for storm control on a physical Layer 2 interface.

For Private Cloud environment, most of the traffic flows between the 5 x Fabrics is expected to take the VXLAN Multi-Site Tunnel as depicted in figure below. There are certain use-cases as below which will take VRF-Lite path instead of Multi-Site.
•    OBJ Backend (BE) traffic (VRF-Lite Path)
Figure 81 VXLAN Multi-Site Tunnel Traffic flows


Note
The VRF-Lite flows are detailed in the next chapter.

8  Logical Design: External Connectivity
This section will cover the logical design aspects involving external connectivity over VRF-Lite in Private Cloud Fabric design. The sub-sections cover two main topics which are VRF design and routing design for external connectivity over VRF-Lite in Fabric-AZs and NWS-AZs. The routing design involved for each use-case is detailed separately covering routing, control plane advertisement and data-plane traffic flow.

Following topics are detailed in the sub-sections
•    VRF Design
•    External Connectivity Use-cases
o    Border Gateway to DCI over VRF-Lite
o    Tenant Specific (VPN) peering in NWS-AZ
o    MGS Firewall Routing
8.1  VRF Design
Virtual Routing and Forwarding (VRF) is a network technology that allows for multiple instances of a routing table to coexist within the same router, enabling the segmentation of network paths without requiring multiple physical devices.

VRF Lite, on the other hand, is a simplified version of VRF that provides similar route isolation features without the need for Multiprotocol Label Switching (MPLS), making it suitable for smaller-scale deployments where full MPLS functionality is unnecessary.

The MP-BGP EVPN has built-in multi-tenancy, Layer 3 subnets in the VXLAN overlay network are in a tenant VRF routing instance. Different tenants can maintain their separate Layer 3 routing instances by default. Therefore, external routing for different tenants needs to be provided separately. The border leaf needs to have a Layer 3 interface to the outside for each tenant VRF instance for which it runs external routing.

Figure 82 VRF-Lite


The routing protocol used between each individual VRF on the border leaf (or BGW) and the external router can be static route, IGP, or BGP. MP-BGP EVPN allows for automatic import of the BGP routes learned from these VRF-lite IPv4 unicast address family into the L2VPN EVPN address family. With other routing protocols, redistribution of routes is required to ensure routes are exchanged between the VXLAN Fabric and the external router.

Note
Private Cloud Fabric design involves eBGP for peering with external devices.

When the border node learns the external routes from the edge router, it advertises the prefixes inside the VXLAN Fabric domain as EVPN Type-5 routes. This information is distributed to the other VTEP nodes. At the same time, the border node is configured to send EVPN routes learned from the L2VPN EVPN address family to the IPv4 or IPv6 unicast address family and advertises them to the external edge router or devices.
8.1.1  Private Cloud VRF Design
Private Cloud Fabric solution includes multi-VRF design for providing segregation and control for various traffic types. Figure below depicts the VRFs involved in the design and the types of external peering (VRF-Lite and MultiSite). While at this point, it might have been clear that the PC_DEFAULT VRF is designated to carry all multi-site traffic including various VRFs that are configured to be carried over MultiSite path.

The  focus of this section and diagram is specifically the VRFs that peers externally over VRF-Lite for satisfying certain solution requirements.

Figure 83 VRFs in design and types of external peering


In the designed network, C42 will implement multiple Virtual Routing and Forwarding (VRF) instances to achieve network isolation and ensure secure, independent routing domains.

The OBJ Backend (obs-be) leverages VRF-Lite to allow OBJ storage to storage backend replication traffic hosted in Fabric-AZ1-AZ3 to utilize VRF-Lite path instead of Multisite. This would allow the replication traffic to be prioritized and routed over dedicated Dark Fiber links within DCI network.

The following list specifies the VRFs that will be utilized within the network:
Table 50 List of VRFs
VRF    Corresponding VRF in DCI    Presence    Notes
default (OOB)    PC_OOB    All Private Cloud OOB Catalysts
default (Fabric)    PC_DEFAULT    All Nexus Switches in Private Cloud environment    For fabric Underlay
mgs-in    NA    MGS leaf switches    MGS subnets behind firewall
        AZ1-3 BGWs (for msite extension)
infra    NA    All Leaf switches & BGWs in Private Cloud environment    Main production vrf
shared-mgs    NA    MGS Leaf switches
AZ1-3 BGWs    Shared Services subnets behind mgs firewall
soc-services    NA    MGS Leaf switches
AZ1-3 BGWs     Soc Services subnets behind mgs firewall
obs-be    PC_OBS-BE    OBJ Leaf Switches, MGS Leaf Switches, AZ1-3 BGWs (for vrf-lite handoff)    Object storage replication
vpn01-vpn64    PC_DCGW_CLZ1 - PC_DCGW_CLZ2    Network Block    Tenant specific vrfs. May not be needed on the DCI if links directly terminate on DCGW.

8.1.1.1  VRF default (Private Cloud BMC)
The "default" VRF in Private Cloud BMC network is designated for out-of-band (OOB/BMC) networks. OOB/BMC network in the design provides access port-density for the management interfaces (iLO/IPMI/Mgmt) of infrastructure devices such as servers, switches storage appliances, firewalls etc. The management interfaces are dedicated for the BMC management and administration of devices and systems. It operates independently from the fabric in-band data traffic, providing a secure and reliable channel for network administrators to perform device configurations, updates, and monitoring without impacting production traffic.

The networks under the "default" (Private Cloud BMC) VRF are connected to Data Center Interconnect (DCI) Provider Edge (PE) routers on PC_OOB VRF. This setup facilitates seamless connectivity to shared services networks, enabling critical day-to-day operations and management tasks. In addition, the VRF connects to the MGS Firewalls within the AZ, to facilitate any communication requirements between iLO/IPMI segment to the control-plane segments in MGS block.
Key Features:
Default VRF: Primary routing instance for Private Cloud out-of-band (OOB/BMC) networks.
Isolation: Keeps management traffic separate from production data for enhanced security.
Shared Services Access: Connects to DNS, NTP, AAA services essential for network management.
External Connectivity: Linked to Data Center Interconnect (DCI) Provider Edge routers for routing to the PC_OOB VRF and MGS Firewall for connectivity requirements to MGS block control-plane segments.
Operational Role: Supports day-to-day management tasks like configuration, monitoring, and troubleshooting.

Figure 84 VRF Default (Private Cloud BMC)

8.1.1.2  VRF default (Private Cloud AZ)
The networks placed in the VRF are VXLAN fabric underlay networks. The networks should be placed in dedicated VRFs to ensure isolation, enhance security, and provide precise control over routing policies, thereby optimizing performance and simplifying management. This VRF will be used to build the underlay topology for individual VxLAN fabrics and carry Multi-Site Overlay traffic (L2VNIs and L3VNIs) between the Fabrics.
8.1.1.3  VRF mgs-in (Private Cloud AZ)
The MGS (Management Services) Block consists of dedicated networks grouped within the MGS Inside (mgs-in) VRF or multiple VRFs, specifically allocated for the Red Hat OpenStack Platform (RHOSP) Control Plane. These networks are secured by the MGS firewall, which has its "inside" interface connected to the MGS networks and its "outside" interface linked to the "infra" VRF. This configuration provides essential external connectivity while maintaining robust security for the control plane operations. MGS Block is expected to have multiple VRFs firewall which will be terminated on the MGS Firewalls. The MGS Firewalls shall provide inter-VRF routing between the VRFs.

Figure 85 VRF MGS-IN (Private Cloud AZ)

Key Features:
MGS Firewall Integration: The firewall's outside interface is connected to the infra VRF within the Private Cloud Availability Zone (AZ), providing controlled external access.
Control Plane Hosting: These VRFs host the control plane networks for Red Hat OpenStack on OpenShift (RHOSO), ensuring efficient management and operation of cloud services.
Multi-Site VXLAN Extension: The VRF is extended to other AZs using Multisite VXLAN, leveraging the "PC_DEFAULT" VRF in DCI PE routers for seamless interconnectivity across locations.
8.1.1.4  VRF infra (Private Cloud AZ)
The "infra" VRF is designated to host infrastructure-related networks for the RHOSO cluster and Block Storage cluster setup and management as well as service-related networks for the VM workloads onboarded. It also includes the OBS-Front End networks as part of shared services. This VRF is extended across other Availability Zones via Multisite VXLAN using the "PC_DEFAULT" VRF in DCI PE routers, facilitating seamless interconnectivity.
Key Features:
•    Infrastructure Networks
•    OBS-Front End Networks
•    Multi-Site VXLAN Connectivity
8.1.1.5  VRF shared-mgs (Private Cloud AZ)

The shared-mgs VRF is specific designed to host the shared services servers/devices. These networks are secured by the MGS firewall, which has its "inside" interface connected to the shared services networks and its "outside" interface linked to the "infra" VRF. The MGS Firewalls shall provide inter-VRF routing between the VRFs.

8.1.1.6  VRF soc-services (Private Cloud AZ)

Similar to shared-mgs VRF, soc-services VRF is specific designed to host the SOC services servers/devices. These networks are secured by the MGS firewall, which has its "inside" interface connected to the soc services networks and its "outside" interface linked to the "infra" VRF. The MGS Firewalls shall provide inter-VRF routing between the VRFs.

8.1.1.7  VRF obs-be (Private Cloud AZ)
The obs-be VRF is specifically designed to handle network traffic related to storage replication of volumes between OBJ servers. This dedicated VRF ensures that replication processes are isolated from other network activities, providing optimized performance and security for storage operations. The VRF will be connected to the PC_OBS-BE VRF in DCI PE routers using VRF-Lite, to provide reachability across Private Cloud Availability Zones (AZs). This configuration ensures efficient routing of storage replication traffic between AZs without relying on the VXLAN multisite network.

“obs-be” VRF will be extended to MGS block by peering with MGS Firewall. This peering allows obs-be segments to access shared-services and mgs-in segments.

8.1.1.8  VRF vpn01-vpn64
These represent the various tenants who will hosts the Tenant specific networks and VM workloads and are isolated from each other. These VRFs will mainly remain in NWS-AZs which will serve as an entry or landing point for a Tenant user from remote office. Once landed, a Tenant user can access destinations in infra VRF such as OBJ shared services via NAT Gateway firewall and Tenant VMs using Centralized Virtual Router (CVR) hosted in Network Node.

8.1.2  VRF Naming and L3 VNI Numbering
This VRF will be associated with a VNI number. This VNI number will be used for routing between the VLANs that are a part of the same Availability Zone.

Table 51 VRF and L3 VNI Numbering
VRF Name    VNI ID
infra    30101
mgs-in    30102
shared-mgs    30103
obs-be    30104
soc-services    30105
vpn01 – vpn64    30201 - 30264
8.2  Border Gateways
External connectivity over VRF-Lite on BGWs of Private Cloud Fabrics will be provided for the following VRFs
•    obs-be VRF (Fabric-AZs only)

As depicted in figure below, each BGW in Private Cloud Fabric will have VRF-Lite peering with DCI PE routers in addition to the MultiSite peering (PC_DEFAULT).

Figure 86 BGW External Peering


Infra VRF in both Fabric-AZs and NWS-AZs will host infrastructure networks (eg: Host operating system)

“obs-be” VRF presence is expected in Fabric-AZs only that hosts the Object Storage (OBJ) backend network. The VRF is used for segregating the replication traffic requirements between OBJ across the 3 AZs over backend network. The replication traffic will be prioritized within DCI to be carried over dedicated path and treated accordingly.

Routing Protocol
Private Cloud Environment will utilize eBGP to exchange routes between the VXLAN fabric and the DCI-PE routers for peering over VRF-Lite. Equal cost multi-pathing (ECMP) allows a router to load balance or distribute traffic over all available network ports with the same distance to the destination address. ECMP increases the usage of network segments, which increases effective network bandwidth. Cisco NX-OS supports the ECMP feature with up to 64 equal-cost paths in the eBGP route table and the unicast Routing Information Base.

The DCI-PE routers will be configured and managed independently and will not be configured by NDFC. Table below lists the BGP parameters used for building this connectivity for obs-be VRF.

Route-Advertisements
When the BGW node learns the external routes from the DCI-PE router, it advertises the prefixes inside the VXLAN Fabric domain as EVPN Type-5 routes. This information is distributed to the other VTEP nodes. At the same time, the BGW is configured to send EVPN routes learned from the L2 VPN EVPN address family to the IPv4 unicast address family and advertises them to the DCI-PE routers. Filters can be applied to send and receive only required prefixes into the VRFs.

Table below lists the prefixes expected to be advertised and received in and out of these VRFs.

Table 54 Route Advertisements
Fabric    VRF Name    Prefix Advertised    Prefix Received
Fabric-AZ    obs-be    <local-obs-be-prefixes>    <remote-obs-be-prefixes>

Configuration Template 20 eBGP-1
interface port-channel101.701
  mtu 9000
  encapsulation dot1q 701
  vrf member obs-be
  ip address 10.92.252.57/30
  no shutdown

interface port-channel102.701
  mtu 9000
  encapsulation dot1q 701
  vrf member obs-be
  ip address 10.92.252.61/30
  no shutdown

interface Ethernet1/63
  description to_ad-dc01-pe02_fh0/0/0/28
  mtu 9100
  channel-group 102 mode active
  no shutdown

interface Ethernet1/64
  description to_ad-dc01-pe01_fh0/0/0/28
  mtu 9100
  channel-group 101 mode active
  no shutdown

router bgp 65101
  router-id 10.92.254.11
  cluster-id 65101
  vrf obs-be
    log-neighbor-changes
    address-family ipv4 unicast
      advertise l2vpn evpn
      redistribute direct route-map fabric-rmap-redist-subnet
      maximum-paths 4
      maximum-paths ibgp 4
    address-family ipv6 unicast
      advertise l2vpn evpn
      redistribute direct route-map fabric-rmap-redist-subnet
      maximum-paths 4
      maximum-paths ibgp 4
    neighbor 10.92.252.58
      remote-as 64642
      password 6 <password>
      address-family ipv4 unicast
        send-community
        send-community extended
        route-map rmap-obsbe-to-dci-out out
    neighbor 10.92.252.62
      remote-as 64642
      password 6 <password>
      address-family ipv4 unicast
        send-community
        send-community extended
        route-map rmap-obsbe-to-dci-out out

ip prefix-list pl-host-routes seq 5 permit 0.0.0.0/0 eq 32

route-map rmap-obsbe-to-dci-out deny 10
  match ip address prefix-list pl-host-routes
route-map rmap-obsbe-to-dci-out permit 1000


8.3  Tenant VPN
The NWS-AZ networks are integrated within a range of VRFs: vpn01 - vpn64. This subchapter details the routing design for these networks, outlining how traffic is managed and routed across the various VRFs to ensure efficient and secure connectivity.
Figure 88 Routing Design for Tenant VRFs


8.3.1  Routing Design
The vpn01-64 networks (as highlighted in green box in figure above), also referred to as Tenant VRFs, are remote user networks that are isolated from each other and reside within different tenants. Tenant users can access destinations in Infra VRF (via a NAT Gateway) for accessing shared services such as OBJ Storage.

Figure 89 Tenant VRFs Routing Architecture


In the routing architecture on the picture above, following eBGP sessions are expected
•    Tenant VRFs establish eBGP sessions with WAN switches for Tenant remote office access.
•    Tenant VRFs on DCGW switches form eBGP sessions with the infra VRF on NWS Leaf switches, with a pair of NAT Gateway situated (L2) between the VRFs logically. The NAT Gateway operates transparently (L2 virtual wire mode) for Layer 3 traffic. The DCGW switches advertise NAT pools to the INFRA VRF in network leaf in respective AZ. Shared Service Subnets are advertised to the Tenant VRF from the infra VRF.
•    DCGW in NWS-AZ1 establish BGP sessions with DCGW in NWS-AZ2. This peering provides redundant path for single-homed Tenant users and adds resiliency to the design during failure scenarios such as NAT Gateways or NWS Spine failures.

The design provides active/active access to shared services from tenant private link. If customer / Tenant has private line in AZ1 then traffic will be forwarded to AZ1 NATGW and similarly for AZ2. There can be scenarios where a Tenant is dual-homed to both NWS-AZs. Shared services routes will be advertised from both NWS-AZs infra VRF towards vpn0x VRFs.

Please note, the configuration details for the NAT pool and rules are not covered in this chapter.
Routing Interface
To establish eBGP neighborship between infra and vpnxx VRFs on DCGW switches, VLAN SVI  interfaces are used. Each leaf switch pair will utilize a VLAN SVI interface per VRF instance. The table below lists VLAN SVI interfaces required for those connections.

Table 55 INFRA to VPNx VRF Routing Interfaces (AZ1)
Device A    VRF A    VLAN SVI #    VRF B    Device B
aead3b-a1-nws-leaf-01    infra    501    vpn01    aead3b-a1-nws-dcgw-01
aead3b-a1-nws-leaf-02    infra     501    vpn01    aead3b-a1-nws-dcgw-02
aead3b-a1-nws-leaf-01    infra    502    vpn02    aead3b-a1-nws-dcgw-01
aead3b-a1-nws-leaf-02    infra     502    vpn02    aead3b-a1-nws-dcgw-02

Routing Protocol
Tenant VRFs will utilize eBGP to exchange routes between the NWS Leaf switches and the DCGW switches. Each VPN network has two eBGP sessions: one to MPLS networks and another one to infra VRF. Table below lists the BGP parameters used for building this connectivity for infra and VPN VRFs.

Note that “local-as” (with the “no-prepend” and “replace-as” options) must be used for adjacencies with this back-to-back peering established from infra and vpnxx VRFs so as to allow the route advertisement between the VRFs (by default the Leaf would not be allowed the prefixes toward the same BGP ASN from where they were advertised).

Table 56 INFRA to VPNx VRF BGP parameters (AZ1)
Leaf Switch    VRF Name    Local ASN    Local IP    Remote ASN    BGP Peer IP    Remote Device Name
aead3b-a1-nws-leaf-01    infra    65141    169.254.10.1    65142    169.254.10.5    aead3-a1-nws-dcgw-01
aead3b-a1-nws-leaf-02    infra     65141    169.254.10.2    65142    169.254.10.6    aead3-a1-nws-dcgw-02
aead3b-a1-nws-leaf-01    infra    65141    169.254.10.9    65142    169.254.10.13    aead3-a1-nws-dcgw-01
aead3b-a1-nws-leaf-02    infra     65141    169.254.10.10    65142    169.254.10.14    aead3-a1-nws-dcgw-02

8.3.1.1  NAT Gateway attachment to the fabric
In this scenario, only shared services pool of IP addresses is advertised– so chosen services can be reached out from Remote Offices. Incoming traffic from Remote Office user lands in NWS fabrics on DCGW (NWS-DCGW-01 / NWS-DCGW-02) devices which is then routed over eBGP towards NWS Leaf switches (NWS-LEAF-01/ NWS-LEAF-02).

Figure 90 NWS Routing Design- Shared Services Access


Following approach will be applied for the traffic:
•    eBGP between Tenant VRF (DCGW side) and Infra VRF (NWS Leaf side) via NAT Gateway
•    NAT Pool advertised to Infra VRF from respective AZ
•    Shared Service Subnets advertised to Tenant VRF from INFRA VRF
•    Source address gets NATed to AZ1 NAT pool as traffic traverses AZ1 NAT Gateway
•    Return traffic reaches AZ1 NAT GW from where AZ1 NAT Pool is advertised

8.3.1.2  VLAN Pools and interfaces
There are two separate VLAN pools planned:
•    VLANs 500-599 – this is a pool reserved in NWS-LEAF and NWS-DCGW devices for the tenant traffic (VPN 1-64)
•    VLANs 600-699 - this is a pool reserved in NWS-DCGW devices for back-to-back connection across AZs.
vlan 501
  name infra-natgw-vpn01
vlan 502
  name infra-natgw-vpn02
!

Note that, to allow the traffic over the VPC peer-link for non-vxlan extended VLANs , the system nve infra-vlans 500-699 command will be used.

In Nexus devices all the L3 interfaces share the same mac by default. You can assign a static MAC address to a Layer 3 interface. The default MAC address for a Layer 3 interface is the MAC address of the virtual device context (VDC) that is associated with it.
!
interface Vlan501
  no shutdown
  vrf member infra
  no ip redirects
  ip address 169.254.10.1/29
  no ipv6 redirects
!
interface Vlan502
  no shutdown
  vrf member infra
  no ip redirects
  ip address 169.254.10.9/29
  no ipv6 redirects




8.3.1.3  Tenant route sharing
The Private Cloud infrastructure is prepared to host 64 tenants – each time a new tenant will need to be configured, a new set of routes needs to be shared and redistributed. All static routes will have a tag so that they can be easily redistributed to BGP, but those static routes, that need to be sent between NWS-LEAF Infra and DCGW VRFs have a specific tag that allows them to be distinguished from the rest.
8.3.1.3.1  From NWS-LEAF perspective
Reference configuration NWS-LEAF-1:
vlan 501
  name infra-natgw-vpn01
vlan 502
  name infra-natgw-vpn02
!
ip prefix-list pl-shared-subnets seq 5 permit 100.126.0.0/16 le 32
!
route-map rmap-infra-to-vpn-out permit 10
  match ip address prefix-list pl-shared-subnets
!
router bgp 65102
  template peer ptmp-dcgw-vpn-peer
    bfd
    remote-as 65142
    local-as 65141 no-prepend replace-as
    ebgp-multihop 5
    address-family ipv4 unicast
      route-map rmap-infra-to-vpn-out out
  vrf infra
    address-family ipv4 unicast
      redistribute static route-map rmap-infra-redst-static
    neighbor 169.254.10.5        // vlan 501 VPN01
      inherit peer ptmp-dcgw-vpn-peer
    neighbor 169.254.10.13        // vlan 502 VPN02
      inherit peer ptmp-dcgw-vpn-peer

All the needed static routes will be redistributed into vrf infra bgp neighbors via route-map rmap-infra-redst-static, and then filtered towards specific neighbors by the usage of another route-maps, prefix-lists and community-lists.
•    VPN neighbors: NWS-Leaf-1 connects only to DCGW-1 and NWS-Leaf-2 connects only to DCGW-2 BGP on VPN1-64 VLAN interfaces. VPN ‘Shared services subnets’ will be advertised towards VPN neighbors so Tenant Remote office users can reach the networks by the usage of route-map: rmap-infra-to-vpn-out and prefix list: pl-shared-subnets
ip prefix-list pl-shared-subnets seq 5 permit 100.126.0.0/16 le 32
!
route-map rmap-infra-to-vpn-out permit 10
  match ip address prefix-list pl-shared-subnets
From NWS-LEAF, only an new VLAN and new BGP neighbor needs to be configured for onboarding a new Tenant/VPNX. The usage of BGP peer template, simplifies the onboarding process and enables fast and consistent configuration.

8.3.1.3.2  From DC_GW perspective:
NWS-DCGW-1 reference configuration:

vlan 501
  name infra-natgw-vpn01
vlan 502
  name infra-natgw-vpn02
vlan 601
  name vpn1-nws1-nws2-b2b
vlan 602
  name vpn2-nws1-nws2-b2b
!
vrf context vpn01
  ip route 100.127.16.0/30 Null0 tag 23456
!
vrf context vpn02
  ip route 100.127.16.4/30 Null0 tag 23456
!
ip community-list standard com_natpool seq 5 permit 222:222
!
route-map rmap-vpn-redst-static permit 10
  match tag 23456
  set community 222:222
route-map rmap-vpn-redst-static permit 20
  match tag 34567
!
route-map rmap-vpn-to-infra-out permit 10
  match community com_natpool
!

[…]

  template peer ptmp-nwsleaf-infra-peer
    bfd
    remote-as 65141
    local-as 65142 no-prepend replace-as
    ebgp-multihop 5

      route-map rmap-vpn-to-infra-out out
  template peer ptmp-nws-b2b-peer
    remote-as 65202

  vrf vpn01
    address-family ipv4 unicast
      redistribute static route-map rmap-vpn-redst-static
    neighbor 169.254.10.1

    neighbor 169.254.4.5

    neighbor 169.254.4.6
      inherit peer ptmp-nws-b2b-peer
    neighbor 169.254.3.38
      remote-as 65050
      password 6 <encrypted pass>
      address-family ipv4 unicast
        send-community
        send-community extended
        route-map rmap-vpn-to-dci-out out
  vrf vpn02
    address-family ipv4 unicast
      redistribute static route-map rmap-vpn-redst-static
    neighbor 169.254.10.9
      inherit peer ptmp-nwsleaf-infra-peer
    neighbor 169.254.4.5
      inherit peer ptmp-nws-b2b-peer
    neighbor 169.254.4.6
      inherit peer ptmp-nws-b2b-peer

Each VPN client will be in their own VRF on DCGW switches.

Each Tenant / VPN will have following two types of connection:
•    Towards NWS-LEAF infra VRF peer – this will be aided with ptmp-nwsleaf-infra-peer bgp peer template which will redistribute only prefixes from rmap-vpn-to-infra-out – this route map is fed with prefixes from community list com_natpool that catches only routes that are tagged with 222:222 community
  template peer ptmp-nwsleaf-infra-peer
    bfd
    remote-as 65141
    local-as 65142 no-prepend replace-as
    ebgp-multihop 5
    address-family ipv4 unicast
      route-map rmap-vpn-to-infra-out out
•    Towards the other Network AZ back-to-back connections – this will be aided with ptmp-nws-b2b-peer bgp peer template – no route filtering will be used here all routes from IPv4 unicast AF (rmap-vpn-redst-static) will be fed to these neighbors.
  template peer ptmp-nws-b2b-peer
    remote-as 65202
    address-family ipv4 unicast

All the needed static routes will be advertised in ipv4 AF via rmap-vpn-redst-static and then filtered out towards selected neighbors.

  vrf vpn01
    address-family ipv4 unicast
      redistribute static route-map rmap-vpn-redst-static
    neighbor 169.254.10.1
      inherit peer ptmp-nwsleaf-infra-peer
    neighbor 169.254.4.5
      inherit peer ptmp-nws-b2b-peer
    neighbor 169.254.4.6
      inherit peer ptmp-nws-b2b-peer
8.3.1.4  eBGP between Tenant VRF and Infra VRF via NAT Gateway
It is to be noted that NWS-LEAF and DCGW switches are under the same Availability Zone and share same Autonomous System Number – for AZ1: 65102. To establish the eBGP session between them, there is a need to present them as if they were in different autonomous system. ASNs from NWS Fabric Local-AS range will be used for this purpose.

Availability Zone    ASN range
AZ1    65141 – 65149
AZ2    65241 – 65249

In AZ1 NWS-LEAF will use 65241 and DCGW will use 6542. Configuration snippet from NWS-LEAF-1:

template peer ptmp-dcgw-vpn-peer
    bfd
    remote-as 65142
    local-as 65141 no-prepend replace-as
    address-family ipv4 unicast
      route-map rmap-infra-to-vpn-out out

Control Plane Advertisements (Ingress)
For the ingress traffic, the shared services segment in Fabric-AZs is advertised into NWS-AZ1 and AZ2 over Multisite via the Router-Servers. The routes are received in infra VRF as depicted in figure below.

The infra VRFs in NWS-AZs advertise the routes towards vpn01 VRF. Figure below depicts the AS-Path details for the routes received in vpn01 VRF from infra VRF.

Figure 92 AS-Path details for Routes Received


During normal scenario, the ingress traffic from Tenant X Remote office can reach NWS-AZ1 or NWS-AZ2 (if dual-homed) and reaches the shared services via any one of the NWS-AZs.

Considering a failure scenario where both Active/Standby NAT GWs fail in NWS-AZ1, the traffic takes the NWS-AZ2 path to reach the shared services destination.

Control Plane Advertisements (Egress)
NWS-AZ VPN1 VRF has two peering, one externally with DCI MPLS or direct connection towards VPN1 or Tenant X Remote office and the second is back-to-back peering between BLs of NWS-AZ1 and NWS-AZ2.

The figure below assumes a single-homed Tenant use-case where Tenant X is connected on to NWS-AZ1. The learned prefixes from remote office are advertised into MultiSite via the Site Local Border Gateway (BGW) as well as direct eBGP peering between NWS-AZ1 and AZ2. For MultiSite path, the Route-Server in the design reflects the routes to NWS-AZ2.

As depicted, each NWS-AZ2 VPN1 VRF BGP table includes both eBGP VRF-Lite path (AS path highlighted in green) as well as Multisite path (AS Path highlighted in blue).
Figure 94 Control Plane Advertisements (Egress)


VPN01 peers with infra VRF over eBGP with NAT-GW in virtual wire mode (L2). A Static Null0 route that is configured for AZ1 NAT pool on NWS-AZ1 BL is redistributed into eBGP. Similarly, Null0 route is defined in AZ2 for the AZ2 NAT pool. As depicted in figure below, the route is received in infra VRF in NWS-AZ1. Similarly an Static Null0 route that is configured for AZ2 NAT pool on NWS-AZ2 BL is redistributed into eBGP. These routes are advertised into Fabric-AZs over Multisite via Router-Servers.

Figure 95 Routes Advertised over Multi-Site via Route-Servers

During normal scenario, the return traffic from Shared Services in Fabric-AZs can reach the Tenant X Remote Office NAT prefix via NWS-AZ1. This is considering that the Tenant traffic got SNAT from AZ1 NAT GW during ingress.
Figure 96 Egress Traffic Flow (Normal Scenario)

Egress Traffic Flow (Failure Scenario)
Consider a failure scenario, where both Active/Standby NAT GWs fail in NWS-AZ1. In this case, the traffic from shared services in Fabric-AZs takes the NWS-AZ2 path to reach the external destination. This is considering that the Tenant traffic got SNAT from AZ2 NAT GW during ingress.
