8.4  MGS Firewall
The MGS block is responsible for hosting the control plane networks for Red Hat OpenStack on OpenShift (RHOSO). These networks are designed with robust routing and security measures to ensure efficient and secure operations. Multiple VRFs are expected behind MGS Firewalls on the inside leg with Infra VRF on the outside leg. Inter-VRF routing will be done by MGS Firewall for the multiple inside MGS VRFs.
8.4.1  Routing Design
Figure below depicts the routing design towards MGS Firewalls. In the design, MGS Firewall cluster (Active/Standby) are physically connected to two different MGS Leaf switch pairs over VPC and offer rack-level redundancy. For easier illustration of inside and outside logical connectivity from Firewalls, the Leaf switches are shown twice (Infra VRF and mgs-in VRF). Physically they are the same switches and VPC links towards the firewalls carrying the both Infra and mgs-in VLANs for the L3 peering.

Figure 98 MGS Firewall Design


Key Routing Design components:
•    eBGP Multi-Hop Neighborship: MGS-Leaf switches Loopback interfaces within the mgs-in VRF establish eBGP multi-hop connections with the dedicated MGS Firewall's "inside" interface, providing reliable routing paths. Similarly, the Leaf switches infra VRF establish multi-hop eBGP connections with the MGS Firewalls “outside” interface.
•    Commands “local-as” (with the “no-prepend” and “replace-as” options) will be used for the adjacency with the firewall established from both the MGS-IN and outside INFRA VRFs so that the firewall node is able to propagate routing advertisement between them. By default, it would not be allowed to advertise prefixes toward the same BGP ASN from where they were received.
•    BFD: Bidirectional Forwarding Detection (BFD) will be disabled for this implementation due to certain limitations on Firewall end.
•    Commands "maximum-path" and "export-gateway-ip" will be enabled as part of best practices. This is required when active and standby firewall nodes are connected to different leaf pairs in same fabric. "export-gateway-ip" command ensures that in both the mgs-in and infra VRFs, traffic originated from any leaf node and destined northbound (or southbound) is only optimally encapsulated to the MGS leaf nodes connected to the active firewall node.
•    Inter AZ Communication: Intra-VLAN and Inter-VLAN communications across AZ1/AZ2/AZ3 happens within mgs-in VRF over Multi-Site. Similar is the case for INFRA VRF segments

Note
The Firewall must have static routes for the Loopback IP addresses with a next-hop of the Anycast Gateway IP for IP connectivity. eBGP multi-hop metric needs to be set to an appropriate value.

Note
Disabling BFD on the firewalls will lead to longer convergence time in the event of failure conditions.

It is important to note that the active firewall node should establish multi-hop EBGP sessions with all four-Leaf switches where active and standby firewalls are connected. This is important to minimize the traffic outage during a firewall switchover event.

A firewall failover event would cause the standby firewall to become active. The standby firewall must be configured with BGP Graceful Restart, a feature that enables BGP sessions to be restarted without causing a disruption in the network. It works by allowing the devices to maintain their established routes even after a routing peering session reset or restart, and because the routing table of the newly activated firewall is fully synchronized with the previously active firewall node, it can continue to forward traffic toward the fabric based on previously programmed information.

8.4.1.1  A/S pair firewall where the devices are physically connected to two different VPC pairs
In this scenario, the active/standby firewalls are connected to two different vPC pairs of leaf switches. Each firewall peers with four loopback interfaces, two from each vPC pair. This setup provides redundancy and ensures that the firewall can maintain connectivity even if one of the vPC pairs becomes unavailable.
•    Each firewall establishes eBGP sessions with the loopback interfaces of both vPC pairs.
•    The use of loopback interfaces across multiple vPC pairs ensures that the BGP sessions are resilient to failures in any single vPC pair.
•    The VXLAN extended VLANs are used to provide the necessary Layer 2 connectivity for the BGP peering across the different vPC pairs (leveraging AGW).
This configuration provides high availability, as the firewalls can maintain BGP sessions with the remaining vPC pair if one pair fails.
•    The topology allows for graceful FW failover. After the failover the FW doesn’t need to re-establish the eBGP adjacencies with the switches.
•    To ensure optimal traffic flows towards the FW, the functionality of “export-gateway-ip” is enabled.
Figure 99 A/S pair firewall where the devices are physically connected to two different VPC pairs


The purpose of the above diagram is to showcase A/S pair firewall integration, in the scenario where the devices are physically connected to two different VPC pairs – the presented data like IP addresses, ASN/VLAN/Loopback/Segment numbers etc. are just example and do not reflect Core42 final configuration.

Routing Considerations (Traffic Symmetry)
Layer-2 stretched VLANs with independent firewalls across multiple sites can lead to asymmetric paths, potentially causing traffic drops at the firewall. To maintain traffic symmetry, only the locally learnt /32 host routes (Eg: AZ1) will be advertised towards the AZ1 firewall inside interface. Remotely learnt /32 routes (Eg:AZ2/3) via MultiSite for the stretched segment will not be advertised towards the AZ1 firewall.   This ensures that traffic for these route ingresses and egresses through the same AZ firewall (AZ-FW). In the event of a firewall failure in one Availability Zone, BGP Route-Maps will be used to steer traffic to a specific site, maintaining uninterrupted service and ensuring resilience.

Figure 100 Local /32 Host Prefix Advertisement


Following are key characteristics of the BGP Route-Maps to achieve traffic symmetry and is illustrated in Figure below.
•    Stretched Subnets:
o    /32 routes will be advertised for local endpoints only
o    /32 routes will NOT be advertised (denied) for remote endpoints
•    Prefix routes for Stretch Subnets:
o    advertised with metric (AS-Path) manipulated so that traffic prefers AZ1 followed by AZ2 (prepend x4) and AZ3 (prepend x8)
•    Site-Local Subnets:
o    Local Prefix routes (only) will be advertised as is.
o    Remote Prefix routes (received via EVPN) will be summarized and advertised (summary-only) with worst metric (AS-Path prepend).
o    Each AZ will have its primary and secondary backup path (Site Failure cases)
    For AZ1 – AZ2 will be primary backup and AZ3 secondary backup
    For AZ2 – AZ1 will be primary backup and AZ3 secondary backup
    For AZ3 – AZ1 will be primary backup and AZ2 secondary backup
•    Default route: Local Preference will be added in MGS Leaf switches mgs-in VRF for incoming prefix (default route) so that local firewall is preferred for all outgoing traffic. On MGS Leaf infra VRF, advertisement will be as follows towards MGS Firewall outside interface.
o    AZ1: Advertise Default route
o    AZ2: Advertise Default route with AS-Path prepend x3
o    AZ3: Advertise Default route with AS-Path prepend x6

Figure 101 Multisite MGS Routing

Route-Advertisements
In the network design, the MGS advertise default route to the mgs-in VRF BGP routers, ensuring that all connected devices have a consistent path for routing external traffic.

Conversely, the mgs-in BGP routers advertise specific routes, including local /32 routes for stretched segments, to the Infra VRF via the MGS Firewall.

Table below lists the prefixes expected to be advertised and received in and out of these VRFs.
Table 60 Prefixes Advertised and Received
Leaf Switch    VRF Name    Prefix Advertised    Prefix Received    Comments
MGS-LEAF{3-4,7-8}    mgs-in        0.0.0.0/0    MGS F/W advertises default route towards MGS_IN VRF
MGS-LEAF{3-4,7-8}    mgs-in    {specific routes-local}
{specific routes-remote – summary-only}
{local /32 for stretched segments}        MGS_IN VRF advertises specific routes (including local /32 for stretched segments) towards Firewall and Infra-VRF
MGS-LEAF{3-4,7-8}    Infra        {specific MGS_IN routes}
{local MGS_IN /32 for stretched segments}
{specific BMC routes}

Configuration Template 21 eBGP-3
ip prefix-list pl-default seq 5 permit 0.0.0.0/0
ip prefix-list pl-host-routes seq 5 permit 0.0.0.0/0 eq 32
ip prefix-list pl-l2ext-host-routes seq 5 permit 10.95.0.0/16 eq 32

ip as-path access-list asp-local seq 5 permit "^$"
ip as-path access-list asp-mgsfw-in-local seq 5 permit "_65106$"

route-map rmap-all-mgsfw-out permit 10
  match as-path asp-local
  match ip address prefix-list pl-l2ext-host-routes
route-map rmap-all-mgsfw-out deny 20
  match ip address prefix-list pl-host-routes
route-map rmap-all-mgsfw-out permit 30
  match as-path asp-local
route-map rmap-all-mgsfw-out permit 40
  match as-path asp-mgsfw-in-local

route-map rmap-all-mgsfw-in permit 10
  match ip address prefix-list pl-default
  set local-preference 200

route-map rmap-infra-mgsfw-in permit 10

route-map rmap-infra-mgsfw-out permit 10
  match as-path pl-default

router bgp 65101

        route-map rmap-infra-mgsfw-out out

Control Plane Advertisements
mgs-in VRF will includes both Site-Local and Stretched segments. As depicted in figure below, each Fabric-AZ learns /32 host routes from stretched segments and Site local /24 Prefixes.

The learned prefixes are advertised into Multisite via the Site Local Border Gateway (BGW). Route-Server in the design reflects the routes to the other Fabric-AZs. Since the Route server is not a VTEP or BGW or in other words not in the data path, it does not have the next hop pointing to itself when reflecting the routes to other AZs. The route server reflects the EVPN routes received from Fabric BFW to other AZs even if all tenant VRFs are not created on the route-server. This is achieved using the command retain “route-target all” under BGP l2vpn address-family configuration on Router-Servers.

As depicted, each Fabric-AZ mgs-in VRF BGP table includes local routes as well as routes received from remote Fabric-AZs. The Router-Server ASN is added to the path for such routes received over Multisite path.

Figure 102 mgs-in VRF Control Plane Advertisements


The deployment of independent firewall services (Active/Standby) in each fabric, brings up the critical requirement to avoid the creation of asymmetric traffic path via firewalls in different sites for both North-South and East-West (inter-tenant) communication. This is because there is no state synchronization happening across sites between the firewall devices, nor any clustering mechanism allowing to redirect traffic.

The /32 prefixes for the stretched segment are advertised into the Infra VRF on the outside leg of firewall. This may cause the creation of asymmetric traffic during corner case failure scenarios such as both Firewalls (active/standby) fails. This is illustrated in subsequent section.

Asymmetric Issue
Assuming the path manipulation (using AS-Path prepend) is not implemented, certain scenarios can cause connections to break due to stateful firewall in the picture. Figure below illustrates the ingress failure scenario in Fabric-AZ1 where both Firewalls have failed.

In this case, traffic from external network to the Fabric-AZ1 /32 Host Routes (10.10.100.10/32) have ECMP routes via both Fabric-AZ2 and Fabric-AZ3. This may cause the creation of asymmetric traffic, if an inbound flow is steered through AZ2 and corresponding egress / return traffic exits AZ3 causing the traffic to be dropped by firewall.
Figure 103 Ingress Failure Scenario (Firewall Failure in Fabric-AZ1)


Consider both active & standby firewall failure in Fabric-AZ1 as depicted in figure below. In this case, traffic from stretched Host (10.10.10.10) to the external network have ECMP routes via both Fabric-AZ2 and Fabric-AZ3. This may cause the creation of asymmetric traffic, if the outbound flow is steered through AZ2 and corresponding return traffic enters AZ3 causing the traffic to be dropped by firewall.
Figure 104 Asymmetric Traffic Issue during Failure Scenario

Path Manipulation
Typical solution to this problem is to ensure predictive path behaviour by manipulating path for the prefixes. This was covered in earlier sections of this document. This will ensure deterministic path during normal operations and failure scenarios. This recommendation is driven by the requirements that, at steady state, connectivity toward a site-local subnet should always traverse the local firewall in that site.

Figure 105 Path Manipulation in Ingress Traffic


Control Plane Advertisement (Ingress)
Figure below illustrates the control plane advertisement from the mgs-in VRF towards infra VRF post path manipulation. For easier illustration, only stretch subnet use-case is shown. As depicted, each Fabric-AZ receives /32 and Prefix routes from its local MGS-IN VRF via MGS FW.
Figure 106 Control Plane Advertisement from MGS_IN VRF to Infra VRF

The received routes are then advertised into infra VRF over Multisite via Route-Server. Figure below illustrates the control plane advertisement.
Figure 107 Routes Advertised into Infra VRF over Multi-Site via Route-Server

Ingress Traffic Flow (Normal Scenario)
Figure below illustrates the data plane traffic flow on the ingress direction. During Normal scenario, the local /32 hosts can be reached via the respective parent site or AZ.
Figure 108 Data Plane Traffic Flow (Ingress Direction in Normal Scenario)


Ingress Traffic Flow (AZ1 Failure Scenario)
Considering Fabric AZ1 ingress Flow and assuming that both Active & Standby firewalls fail in Fabric AZ1, traffic from source in external networks prefer Fabric AZ2 path (AS-Path prepend x3) for 10.10.100.0/24 prefix compared to Fabric-AZ3 which has longer prefix.
Figure 109 Data Plane Traffic flow (Ingress Direction in AZ1 Failure Scenario)

Ingress Traffic Flow (AZ1 & AZ2 Failure Scenarios)
Assuming that subsequently both Active & Standby firewalls fail in Fabric AZ2 in addition to AZ1, traffic from source in external networks has the Fabric AZ3 path for 10.10.100.0/24 prefix.
Figure 110 Data Plane Traffic flow (Ingress Direction in AZ1 + AZ2 Failure Scenario)


Control Plane Advertisement (Egress)
Like ingress path manipulation, on the egress side, similar approach is needed to ensure path symmetry during failure scenarios. Default route originated in Infra VRF is advertised to the mgs-in VRF for egress flow. AZ2 prepends the path (x3 times) while AZ3 prepends (x6 times). During normal operations, to maintain path symmetry for egress flow, Local Preference is set in mgs-in VRF inbound routes so that the local site is always preferred as exit point.
Figure 111 Path Manipulation in Egress Traffic


Egress Traffic Flow (Normal Scenario)
Figure below depicts the flow during normal scenario. The traffic exits the site local firewall due to the local preference set.
Figure 112 Egress Traffic Flow (Normal Scenario)



Egress Traffic Flow (AZ1 Failure Scenario)
Considering Fabric AZ1 egress Flow and if both Active & Standby firewalls fail in Fabric AZ1, traffic from source part of stretch subnet prefer Fabric AZ2 path (AS-Path prepend x3) for 0.0.0.0/0 route over the Fabric-AZ3 which has longer AS-Path prepend.
Figure 113 Egress Traffic Flow (AZ1 Failure Scenario)


Egress Traffic Flow (AZ1 & AZ2 Failure Scenario)
If subsequently both Active & Standby firewalls fail in Fabric AZ2 as well in addition to AZ1, traffic from source path of stretch subnet in AZ1 takes the Fabric AZ3 path (0. 0. 0.0/0).
Figure 114 Egress Traffic Flow (AZ1 + AZ2 Failure Scenario)

9  Fabric Traffic Flows
This section covers in detail the traffic flows expected in Private Cloud environment for various traffic types. The traffic flows are grouped into high-level categories as listed in table below
9.1  Traffic Flows (L2)
9.1.1  Intra AZ
Figure below depicts the traffic between endpoints or end-hosts within same VLAN / L2 VNI within same AZ / DC.

From control-plane perspective, Host information (mac / IP) learned by the local Leaf switches are advertised to the BGWs acting as Route-Reflector (RRs)/ RR ensures the host info are advertised (EVPN Type 2) to other Leaf switches in the Fabric.

Assuming Host A wants to communicate with Host B in MGS Block.
1.    Traffic from Host A reaches connected Leaf
2.    Leaf does a Layer-2 lookup on destination mac address. The lookup results in destination reachable via Leaf 3 VTEP. Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards destination Leaf VTEP.
3.    Packet gets routed in underlay, based on destination VTEP address, and reaches destination Leaf.
4.    Destination Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Host B based on destination lookup on original packet.

Similarly Host B (MGS-Block) to Host C (Compute Block) traffic flow follows similar approach. The only difference is the traffic gets routed in the underlay via Super Spines or Core as this block interconnects MGS and Compute block spines.
Figure 115 Intra VLAN/AZ Traffic Flows

9.1.2  Inter-AZ
Figure below depicts the traffic flow between endpoints within same VLAN / L2 VNI but across AZ or Data Centres.

Host information (mac / IP) learned by the local sites are advertised by local-site BGWs to the remote site BGWs via Route-Server (RS). The RS ensures the host info are advertised (EVPN Type 2) to Remote Site BGWs in MultiSite deployment.

Assuming Host D in AZ1 wants to communicate with Host E in AZ2.
1.    Traffic from Host D reaches directly connected Leaf
2.    Source Leaf does a Layer-2 lookup on destination mac address. The lookup results in destination reachable via AZ1 BGWs Anycast TEP. Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards destination BGW.
3.    BGW  decapsulates the packet and identifies the destination points to remote-site BGW. It re-encapsulates the packet and forwards to AZ2 BGW.
4.    BGW re-encapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Leaf in AZ2 after lookup.
5.    Destination Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Host E based on destination lookup on original packet. The return traffic follows the similar path in the opposite direction.

Figure 116 Intra VLAN – Inter AZ Traffic Flows


9.2  Traffic Flow (L3)
9.2.1  Intra-AZ
Figure below depicts the L3 traffic flow between endpoints in different VLANs / L2 VNI within a Fabric AZ. From control-plane perspective, internal and external subnet prefixes are advertised as EVPN Type 5 routes via RR within a Fabric.

Assuming Host B in L2 VNI A (MGS Block) wants to communicate with Host D in L2 VNI B (Compute Block).
1.    Traffic from Host B reaches directly connected Leaf. Source Leaf identifies the packet as L3 as its destined to the anycast gateway mac address. A lookup returns as destination reachable via Compute Leaf VTEP.
2.    Source Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards destination VTEP. Ingress VTEP routes packets from Source VNI onto the Layer-3 VNI. D-MAC in the inner header is the egress VTEP router MAC.
3.    Packet gets routed in underlay based on destination VTEP address reaches destination Leaf.
4.    Egress VTEP routes packets from L3 VNI to the destination VNI/VLAN. Destination Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Host D based on destination lookup on original packet.

Figure 117 Traffic Flow: Intra-AZ (L3)

9.2.2  Inter-AZ
Figure below depicts the L3 traffic flow between endpoints in different Fabric-AZs and different VLANs / L2 VNI. From control-plane perspective, internal and external subnet prefixes are generally advertised as EVPN Type 5 routes via Router-Server across Fabric-AZs.

Assuming Host A in AZ1 wants to communicate with Host C in AZ2.
1.    Traffic from Host A reaches MGS Leaf. Leaf identifies the packet as L3 as its destined to it anycast gateway mac address. A lookup points destination reachable via AZ1 BGW.
2.    Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards BGW VTEP address. Ingress VTEP routes packets from Source VNI onto the Layer-3 VNI.
3.    BGW  decapsulates the packet and identifies the destination points to remote-site BGW. It re-encapsulates the packet and forwards to AZ2 BGW.
4.    AZ2 BGW decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Leaf in AZ2 after lookup.
5.    Destination Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Host C based on destination lookup on original packet. The return traffic follows the similar path in the opposite direction.

Figure 118 Inter VLAN/AZ Traffic Flows

 
9.3  Traffic Flows (Fabric-AZ)
9.3.1  Tenant VM to VM
Figure below depicts high-level VXLAN Traffic flows between Fabric-AZs (AZ1, AZ2 and AZ3).
For simpler reference only AZ1 and AZ2 are shown, however the same are applicable for AZ3 as well.
VM to VM traffic flow over Geneve Tunnel between the compute nodes. The Geneve Tunnel is built between the Compute nodes hosting VMs and is carried over Tenant VLAN part of infra VRF (RHOSO underlay). Within Compute hypervisor, VM Tap ports for each project network are placed in dedicated logical OVN switch. Each OVN switch is associated with VNID which is unique per project network and the traffic is carried over Geneve tunnels between the compute nodes.

Assuming Tenant VM in AZ1 compute block wants to communicate with Tenant VM in AZ2. Geneve Traffic from VM reaches Compute Leaf.
1.    A lookup on the leaf points the destination (compute node in AZ2) via AZ1 BGW.
2.    AZ1 Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards AZ1 BGW VTEP address.
3.    AZ1 BGW decapsulates the packet and identifies the destination points to remote-site AZ2 BGW. It re-encapsulates the packet and forwards to AZ2 BGW.
4.    AZ2 BGW decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Leaf in AZ2 after lookup.
5.    Destination Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination compute node based on destination lookup on original packet. The return traffic follows the similar path in the opposite direction.

Figure 119 Multi-Site VXLAN Traffic flows between Fabric-AZs


9.3.2  Compute to Block Storage (BLK)
This Flow is expected to be local within AZ during normal operations. Compute block and Block Storage nodes are in different L2 VNIs within infra VRF and the traffic is routed between them. As depicted in figure below.
1.    Traffic from Compute reaches directly connected Leaf switch.
2.    Source Leaf identifies the packet as L3 and lookup points to BLK Leaf VTEP. Source Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards destination VTEP.
3.    Packet gets routed in underlay based on destination VTEP address reaches destination Leaf.
4.    Destination Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination BLK node based on destination lookup on original packet.

Figure 120 Traffic Flow: Compute to Block storage



9.3.3  Tenant VM to Shared Services (OBJ/NTP/DNS)
Tenant (X) will have its specific OBJ Distributed Virtual Router (DVR) that has a leg in infra VRF.
1.    Tenant VMs when accessing shared services such as OBJ, NTP/DNS etc, will use a Floating IP (FIP) from the shared external network (L2 VNI).
2.    This flow is securely routed in infra VRF using VLAN ACLs (VACLs) and is expected to be within AZ during normal operations. Traffic is permitted from source (FIP segment) to specific destinations (OBJ/NTP/DNS). If permitted Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards destination VTEP.
3.    The packet gets decapsulated on OBJ Leaf and forwarded to OBJ storage nodes
4.    For NTP/DNS, the traffic is further routed towards MGS-Firewalls if permitted by VACLs.
5.    MGS Firewalls routes the packet (if allowed) towards the NTP/DNS servers in MGS-IN VRF.

Figure 121 Traffic Flow: Tenant VMs to shared services


9.3.4  Tenant VM to FLS
Each Tenant (X) will have specific FLS DVR for accessing the File storage services. Both FLS and VMs requiring access will be part of same L2 VNI.

1.    VMs accessing FLS service will use a Floating IP (FIP) from the Tenant specific L2 VNI.
2.    This flow is routed in default VRF and is expected to be within L2 VNI (L2) during normal operations. Source Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards destination VTEP.
3.    The packet gets decapsulated on FLS Leaf and forwarded to FLS storage appliances

Figure 122 Traffic Flow: Tenant VM to File Storage



9.4  Traffic Flow (NWS to Fabric-AZ)
9.4.1  Tenant Remote Office to OBJ & Horizon
A Tenant user from Remote Office can access the Horizon Dashboard in MGS Block and OBJ shared services in OBJ Block.
1.    This Flow is expected to enter NWS-AZ1/2 and land on Tenant specific VRF (vpn01 for example) from Tenant Remote Office. The traffic gets SNAT (via NAT GW) and routed to the infra VRF as the destination (Horizon and OBJ) routes are learned from this VRF.
2.    Network Leaf switch encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards NWS-AZ BGW TEP address
3.    NWS-AZ BGW decapsulates the packet and identifies the destination points to remote-site BGW in Fabric-AZs. It re-encapsulates the packet and forwards to Fabric-AZ BGW.
4.    Fabric-AZ BGW decapsulates the packet and after lookup re-encapsulates and forwards to destination OBJ-Leaf.
5.    OBJ-Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination OBJ node based on destination lookup on original packet. The return traffic follows the similar path in the opposite direction.
6.    If the packet is destined to Horizon Dashboard, the Fabric-AZ BGW forwards to destination MGS-Leaf infra VRF.
7.    MGS-Leaf routes the packet to the MGS-Firewalls from where the Horizon Dashboard segment route is learnt. The Firewall routes the packet towards mgs-in VRF where the Horizon Dashboard segment is hosted.

Figure 123 Traffic Flow: Tenant Remote Office to OBJ & Horizon Dashboard

 
9.4.2  Tenant Remote Office to VM
1.    Traffic from source Tenant Remote offices (vpn01 for example) lands on the Border Leaf or DCGW switches in NWS-AZ. The Traffic is destined towards the actual Tenant VM project network prefixes.
2.    Lookup on DCGW points the destination prefix reachable via NWS-Leaf switches (static route with HMM tracking learnt via BGP on DCGW) where the Tenant CVR instance is active. Traffic is encapsulated in VXLAN and sent towards the NWS-Leaf switches.
3.    NWS-Leaf switch decapsulates the packet and since NWS-Leaf switch has static route configuration, it routes the packet towards CVR hosted on Network nodes.
4.    CVR Router routes the traffic towards the Compute node over Geneve Tunnel. Geneve Traffic from VM reaches NWS-Leaf switch in infra VRF.
5.    NWS-Leaf switch encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards NWS-AZ BGW TEP address.
6.    NWS-AZ BGW decapsulates the packet and identifies the destination points to remote-site BGW. It re-encapsulates the packet and forwards to Fabric-AZ BGW.
7.    Fabric-AZ BGW decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Compute Leaf in infra VRF.
8.    Compute Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination Tenant VM based on destination lookup on original packet. The return traffic follows the similar path in the opposite direction.

Figure 124 Traffic Flow: Tenant Remote Office to VM





9.5  Traffic Flows (VRF-Lite)
9.5.1  OBJ-Back-End (BE)
OBJ Storage nodes has a leg in obs-be VRF in addition to infra VRF. This backend leg is used for carrying replication traffic between the OBJ Storage nodes across Fabric-AZs. The backend traffic is carried over VRF-Lite instead of VXLAN MultiSite. The packet flow is as follows.

1.    Traffic from OBJ node backend interface in AZ1 destined to OBJ node in AZ2 reaches OBJ Leaf. A lookup in obs-be VRF on the leaf points the destination reachable via AZ1 BGW.
2.    Source Leaf encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and sends towards AZ1 BGW TEP address.
3.    AZ1 BGW decapsulates the packet and identifies the destination prefix is learned from DCI-PE routers (obs-be VRF) over VRF-Lite peering. The packet is forwarded to DCI-PE routers where it is routed towards AZ2 BGW.
4.    AZ2 BGW receives the packet and based on the lookup encapsulates the packet in VXLAN and forwards to destination OBJ-Leaf in AZ2.
5.    OBJ-Leaf decapsulates the packet and forwards to destination OBJ node backend interface. The return traffic follows the similar path in the opposite direction.

Figure 125 Traffic Flow: OBJ Backend Flow


  
9.6  Failure Scenarios (Fabric)
9.6.1  Link & Spine Failures
Figure below illustrates various link failures and Spine failures in the fabric.
1)    Considering a link between LF1 and SPN1 fails in Compute Block, following are the sequence of events that proceeds.
a)    Link Down is detected by BFD and subsequently OSPF peering is brought down
b)    Underlay failure does not impact VXLAN Overlay Control Plane as traffic ECMPed to remaining link towards RR (SS)
c)    This is same case if Spine fails. Spine is acting as intermediate node doing just underlay routing. Failure in this layer does not impact VXLAN overlay control plane.
d)    Traffic from other block is attracted to the Leaf via remaining links
e)    Sub-Second Convergence in underlay
2)    If LF2 to Server Link fails in Block Storage Block for example:
a)    Traffic from Server shifts traffic to other available link in port-channel (Leaf 2) on the egress side.
b)    Return can come in on LF1 or LF2. If LF2, then the traffic can take VPC Peer-lInk to reach the compute node.

Figure 126 Link & Spine Failure in Fabric


 
9.6.2  Fabric Leaf Failures
Figure below illustrates Leaf failure use-case in the fabric.

1)    One of the Leaf in VPC Peer fails
a)    OSPF brought down on Spines towards the Leaf switch.
b)    In the rest of the Fabric, VPC VTEP IP of the failed Leaf switch is still learned from its peer Leaf2.
c)    Traffic from other Leaf switches in the Fabric send traffic to Leaf switch 2 only on the ingress side
d)    Traffic from server shifts traffic to other available link on the egress side.

Figure 127 Fabric Leaf Failure


9.6.3  Core / BGW Failures
Figure below illustrates BGW internal and external link failure use-cases in the fabric.

1)    BGW Internal Interface failure
a)    Internal interface failures tracked using fabric-tracking function (evpn multisite fabric-tracking)
b)    As long as any interface is available, BGW can extend Layer 2 and Layer 3 traffic to remote sites
c)    If all interfaces fail on internal, BGW is isolated from VTEP perspective
d)    The isolated BGW withdraws all its advertised routes (Type2,3,4,5)
e)    Traffic is rerouted to the remaining 3 x BGWs and have it active
f)    For L2 VNIs, Designated-forwarder role for the VNIs previously “owned” by the isolated BGW is now renegotiated between the remaining BGWs
Figure 128 Core/BGW Failure


2)    BGW External interface failure
a)    External interface failures tracked using dci-tracking function (evpn multisite dci-tracking).
b)    As long as one interface is available BGW can extend Layer 2 and Layer 3 traffic to remote sites
c)    If all interfaces fail on external, the isolated BGW stops advertising the loopback to the site-internal underlay network
d)    It withdraws all BGP EVPN Route Type 4 (Ethernet segment) route advertisement.
e)    all traffic is rerouted to the remaining BGWs loopback addresses
f)    The advertisements to participate in designated-forwarder election are removed from the DCI-isolated BGW

 
9.7  Failure Scenarios
9.7.1  MGS Firewall
Normal Scenario
Figure below illustrates the traffic flow involving MGS firewalls. MGS block involves both site-local and stretch segments. During normal operations, traffic from both site local is expected to ingress and egress the local site. However, for stretched subnets, to maintain traffic symmetry is achieved by advertising site-local /32 host routes will be advertised towards F/W inside interfaces. This will ensure, the traffic for /32 host routes ingress and egress the AZ-FW.

Figure 129 MGS Firewall Traffic Flow (Normal Scenario)


BGP Attributes are used on prefix routes to steer traffic to specific site in case of firewall failure in one AZ. BGP Route-Maps used on Firewall Inside peering to achieve the following
•    Stretched Subnets:
o    /32 routes will be advertised for local endpoints only
o    /32 routes will NOT be advertised (denied) for remote endpoints
•    Prefix routes for Stretch Subnets:
o    advertised with metric (AS-Path) manipulated so that traffic prefers AZ1 followed by AZ2 (prepend x4) and AZ3 (prepend x8)
•    Site-Local Subnets:
o    Local Prefix routes (only) will be advertised as is.
o    Remote Prefix routes (received via EVPN) will be summarized and advertised (summary-only) with worst metric (AS-Path prepend).
o    Each AZ will have its primary and secondary backup path (Site Failure cases)
    For AZ1 – AZ2 will be primary backup and AZ3 secondary backup
    For AZ2 – AZ1 will be primary backup and AZ3 secondary backup
    For AZ3 – AZ1 will be primary backup and AZ3 secondary backup
•    Default route: Local Preference will be added in MGS Leaf switches MGS-IN VRF for incoming prefix (default route) so that local firewall is preferred for all outgoing traffic. On MGS Leaf INFRA VRF, advertisement will be as follows towards MGS Firewall outside interface.
o    AZ1: Advertise Default route
o    AZ2: Advertise Default route with AS-Path prepend x3
o    AZ3: Advertise Default route with AS-Path prepend x6

AZ1 Firewalls Failure
This use-case assumes both firewalls fail in Site-1. Hosts in AZ2 and AZ3 site-local and stretched subnet continue to communicate using site local Firewalls as depicted in figure below.
Figure 130 AZ1 Firewall Failure


Hosts connected to Site-Local and Stretched subnet in AZ1 will take the Multisite path towards AZ2 because the Leaf will find the default route received from Site-2 with AS Prepend x3 as preferred path and communicate outside. Traffic from hosts outside can communicate with hosts connected to site-1 local and stretched segments via Site-2 since it will have the preferred route with AS Prepend x3 compared to site-3 routes with AS prepend x6.

AZ1 & AZ2 Firewalls Failure
This use-case assumes both firewalls fail in Site-2 as well in addition to Site-1. Hosts in AZ3 site-local and stretched subnet continue to communicate using site local Firewalls as depicted in figure below.
Figure 131 AZ1 & AZ2 Firewall Failure


Hosts connected to Site-Local and Stretched subnet in AZ1 and AZ2 will take the Multisite path towards AZ3 because the Leaf will find the default route received from Site-3 with AS Prepend x6 and communicate outside. Traffic from hosts outside can communicate with hosts connected to site-1 and site-2 local and stretched segments via Site-3 since it will be only route available with AS Prepend x6.

9.7.2  NAT Gateway
Normal Scenario
Traffic flows from Tenant Remote Office involving NAT Gateway will tend to use AZ specific NAT pool to reach the shared services segments. This will also depend on if the Tenant is connected to single NWS-AZ (single-homed) or both AZs (dual-homed). Assuming here that the Tenants are single homed, the traffic from Tenant (Green) will enter NWS-AZ1 and get Source NAT from AZ1 pool to reach the Shared Services segment in Fabric-AZs. The Return traffic reaches AZ1 NAT GW from where AZ1 NAT Pool is advertised.

Figure 132 NAT Gateway Flow (Normal Scenario)

AZ1 NAT-GWs Failure
Considering failure use-case where both NAT-Gateways in NWS-AZ1 fail in AZ1, the traffic from Remote user (tenant green) will enter the DCGW in AZ1 and since the DCGWs are interconnected between AZ1 and AZ2, the traffic will take the DCGW-to-DCGW links to reach AZ2. The traffic gets SNAT from AZ2 pool in order to the reach the shared services segment in Fabric-AZs. The Return traffic reaches AZ2 NAT GW since the incoming traffic from Tenant user got SNAT from AZ2 pool.

Figure 133 NAT Gateway Flow (Failure Scenario)

10.1.3  Dell Block
The bond details for Dell Block storage devices are listed in the below table.

Dell Block storage devices are equipped with Network Interface Cards (NICs) or integrated network adapters that provide 25Gbps speed ports connectivity to the uplink leaf switch to handle the server-to-switch traffic. The redundant uplink paths using VPC and link aggregation (LACP) technology to provide increased throughputs, redundancy and high availability.

There are multiple types of Dell Block storage devices such as block-00, block-01, and block-05, block-20 each have dedicated Network Interface Cards (NICs). 4 x 25G forms two bundles(bond0 and bond1) for carrying production traffic and 1 x 1G as the Management(iDRAC) connectivity for each type of Dell Block storage devices.

10.1.4  NetApp (FLS)
NetApp (FLS) provides the File Share services in a cloud environment. Each NetApp (FLS) have dedicated Network Interface Cards (NICs) with 25G speed for carrying production and 1G speed for the Management(iDRAC) connectivity.

Each NetApp (FLS) has 8 x 25G NIC forming a bundle using VPC with link aggregation (LACP) technology towards the uplink leaf switches to provide increased throughputs, redundancy and high availability.

10.1.6  Red Hat
Red Hat deployment includes multiple clusters / nodes / servers configured for different roles such as, Master, Worker, KVM Bootstrap etc. Each server node includes dedicated Network Interface Cards (NICs) with 25G speed for carrying production and 1G speed for the Management(iDRAC) connectivity. There are two bundles(bond0 and bond1) for the nodes using VPC and link aggregation (LACP) technology towards the uplink leaf switches to provide increased throughputs, redundancy and high availability.

10.2  OpenShift eBGP Peering
OpenShift nodes need to establish L3 peering with the fabric over vPC. In Fabric environment, Leaf Switches establish eBGP peering with OpenShift Control Plane and Worker nodes. In VXLAN Fabric, IPv4 Layer 3 peering between leaf nodes and Hosts are supported with the loopback IP in Tenant VRF. Static routes on OpenShift Platform for loopback IPs must point to the Anycast Gateway address on Fabric Leaf switches. This peering will be a Multi-Hop eBGP peering between OpenShift Nodes and MGS Leaf and Multi-Hop BFD will be enabled for faster convergence.

Figure 134 BGP Peering with OpenShift nodes

10.2.1  OPS Cluster (CP Peering)
Figure below depicts the Peering with the OPS Cluster Control Plane nodes from Fabrics in all 3 x AZs. Each AZ includes a Control plane node with which the MGS Leaf switches does eBGP peering over multi-hop.

Figure 135 BGP Peering with OPS Cluster Control Plane nodes


As depicted in figure above, the Control Plane nodes peers with the Private Cloud MGS Leaf switches SVI interface. The nodes use static routes towards the SVI interface IP Address as destination and next-hop as the anycast gateway address (VLAN 1000). The VLAN 1000 for control plane network is L2 Stretched across all 3 x AZs.

Table below lists the BGP peering details for this L3 connectivity.

        aead3b-a1-mgs-leaf-02        10.64.191.18
AZ2    shared-mgs    aead5b-a1-mgs-leaf-01    65201    10.65.191.17    65004    10.95.64.3
        aead5b-a1-mgs-leaf-02        10.65.191.18

Table 74 Prefixes Advertised and Received (per AZ)

10.2.1.3  Config-Template

Template below includes reference configuration for AZ1.
system nve infra-vlans 901
!
vlan 901
  name ocp-peering-anycast
!
interface Vlan901
  description ocp-peering-anycast-svi
  no shutdown
  no autostate
  vrf member shared-mgs
  no ip redirects
  ip address 10.92.191.250/31
  no ipv6 redirects
!
interface Vlan1000
  description ops-cp
  no shutdown
  mtu 9000
  vrf member shared-mgs
  no ip redirects
  ip address 10.95.64.1/26 tag 12345
  no ipv6 redirects
  fabric forwarding mode anycast-gateway
!
ip prefix-list pl-all-routes seq 5 permit 0.0.0.0/0 le 32

ip prefix-list pl-ocp-ops-ip-pool seq 5 permit 10.95.64.0/26 eq 32
ip prefix-list pl-ocp-ops-ip-pool seq 10 permit 10.95.65.0/24 eq 32

route-map rmap-ocp-all-out deny 10
  match ip address prefix-list pl-all-routes

route-map rmap-ocp-ops-in permit 10
  match ip address prefix-list pl-ocp-ops-ip-pool

router bgp 65101
  vrf shared-mgs
    neighbor 10.95.64.2
      bfd multihop
      remote-as 65004
      description openshift-ops-cp1-az1
      password 6 JDYkvsngUiQreJIh4ynymj3gvCTplWfwDdvAkKBJqg4zpGVWJNK6sErNPH00Qhf5HJpKPxYCRcArAA==
      update-source Vlan901
      ebgp-multihop 10
      address-family ipv4 unicast
        route-map rmap-ocp-ops-in in
        route-map rmap-ocp-all-out out

10.3  Multi-Tenant Security Consideration
Multi-Tenant VM workloads share external Network for accessing shared services like Object Storage (OBJ) and NTP/DNS. Tenant VMs will use the dynamically assigned Floating IP (FIP) from shared External Network in order to access the shared services. One of the key considerations is to deny FIP to FIP communications between Tenants and only permit towards OBJ/NTP/DNS networks. FIP assignment is dynamic via DHCP (OSP) and cannot be controlled using ranges.

Figure below illustrates this consideration and requirements.

Figure 140 multi-Tenant security consideration


In order to address the requirement, VLAN Access Control List (ACLs) will be leveraged in Private Cloud Fabric environment. VLAN ACLs will be defined permitting specific flows from External Network segments to the shared services segments and applied to the specific VLANs (External Network). The rest of the flows will be denied including intra segment (External network) flows.

TCAM needs to be carved prior configuring VACLs on Compute Leaf switches. This step will require reboot of the switches for the config to take effect. Following config template includes sample configuration for TCAM carving and VLAN ACLs.

hardware access-list tcam region ing-racl 2048
hardware access-list tcam region egr-racl 1536
hardware access-list tcam region vacl 256
!
ip access-list permit-fls
  10 permit ip any 60.1.1.0/8
  20 permit ip 60.1.1.0/8 any
vlan access-map fls 10
  match ip address permit-fls
  action forward
vlan filter fls vlan-list 500
!

 
10.4  Static Route with HMM Tracking
Tenants from remote office can access the VM workloads  directly for the purpose of management and other administrative purpose. This traffic from remote office is achieved via Central Virtual Router (CVR) hosted in Network nodes or servers in NWS-AZs. Tenant specific CVR is hosted on the network nodes to route traffic from Tenant remote office as depicted in figure below. Note that a Tenant could be single-homed to one of the NWS-AZ or dual-homed to both NWS-AZs.

In NWS-AZ specifically, the CVR hosted on Network nodes have two legs logically connected to infra VRF and Tenant specific VRF (VPN01 for example). The CVR active is spawned on one of the NWS-AZs dynamically. The active instance could either get spined on an network node in AZ1 or AZ2. A static route is configured on Network TOR switches in Tenant specific VRF (VPN1) with Tenant VM project network as destination. The next-hop for this router is the IP address of CVR interface. The static route is redistributed into eBGP and advertised externally to Tenant specific remote office.

Figure 141 Routing - Remote Office to VM Access


Given the design that the CVR active instance can reside only in one of the NWS-AZs, static route with tracking feature will be leveraged in order to maintain traffic symmetry and path optimization for this traffic flow. As depicted in figure below, Static routes for Tenant specific project network are applied under the Tenant VRF (VPN01) on Network Leaf Nodes (only) on both NWS-AZ1 and A2Z2 pointing to CVR IP. The Static route will track the Host Mobility manager (HMM) route for CVR. Only the site Network TORs switches where the CVR is active will originates prefix routes.

The redistributed route into Fabric ensures that the site where CVR is active is preferred due to the better metric  (AS-Path). Therefore, traffic from remote user can directly land on the NWS-AZ1 that has the active CVR instance instead of sub-optimal path via AZ2 in this example. Figure below illustrates the static route with tracking feature leveraged for traffic symmetry and optimized routing.

Figure 142 Static Routing - Remote Office to VM Access


11  Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller (NDFC)
The Cisco Nexus Dashboard runs on UCS C-series SFF Rack Server. The Cisco UCS C225 M6 Rack Server is among the most versatile general-purpose enterprise infrastructure and application servers in the industry. It is a high-density 2-socket rack server that delivers industry-leading performance and efficiency for a wide range of workloads, including virtualization, collaboration, and bare-metal applications. The Cisco UCS C225 M6 server extends the capabilities of the Cisco UCS portfolio in a 1-Rack-Unit (1RU) form factor. The UCS C225 that will be used for Nexus Dashboard comes with additional VIC mLOM card with four 10/25Gbit Ethernet interfaces.

Figure 143 Cisco UCS C220 M6 Rack Server Front View


Figure 144 UCS C220 Rear View


11.1  Licensing Information
Cisco Nexus Dashboard offers flexible options with a choice of three licensing tiers. It is offered as a part of Cisco Data Center Networking (DCN) Advantage, Cisco DCN Premier tier licenses, and Cisco DCN Day-2 Ops Add-on License for customers.

Nexus switches utilize Cisco Smart Licensing model. In this model, the license instance is not locked to a particular product instance. Instead a device which requires a specific license requests it from the pool of available licenses at Smart Licensing Portal.

In Core42 Private Cloud Environment, the Nexus devices being part of the fabrics will require following licenses:
-    Switches acting as BorderGateways (SuperSpines at fab-01/02/03 and Spines at nws-01/02) will require Advantage License package,
-    Route-Servers will require Essentials License package.
-    Remaining switches in all EVPN fabrics will require Essentials License package,
11.2  Scaling and Compatibility References
The 1RU UCS server used for Nexus Dashboard comes with redundant power supplies, presents the following network and console connections:
•    Data Connection: Virtual Interface Card (VIC) with two 10/25GE interfaces.
•    Management connection: 1-Gb Ethernet ports.
•    CIMC (Cisco Integrated Management Controller) to be connected to a dedicated network providing an ILO for the server: 1-Gigabit Ethernet port
•    Console connection: serial port (RJ-45 connector)

11.3  Cluster Overview
Nexus Dashboard is offered as a cluster of specialized Cisco UCS servers (Nexus Dashboard platform) with the software framework (Nexus Dashboard) preinstalled on it. The Cisco Nexus Dashboard software stack can be decoupled from the hardware and deployed in a number of virtual form factors.

Each Nexus Dashboard cluster typically consists of 3 master nodes. In addition, you can provision a number of worker nodes to enable horizontal scaling and standby nodes for easy cluster recovery in case of a master node failure. For cluster sizing information and recommended number of nodes based on specific use cases. See the Cisco Nexus Dashboard Capacity Planning tool.
11.3.1  Nexus Dashboard Services
Cisco Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller, Cisco Nexus Dashboard Orchestrator and Cisco Nexus Dashboard Insights services are being integrated into the Cisco Nexus Dashboard as native services as part of simplifying the overall consumption experience.

Cisco Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller (NDFC) consolidates management for multiple NX-OS-based switches, bringing automation and monitoring for LAN, EVPN VXLAN, and SAN fabrics. Simply choose an operational mode (LAN, SAN), discover new and/or existing switches, and leverage the benefits of Zero-Touch Provisioning (ZTP), fabric monitoring, backup, faster provisioning and upgrades, and much more. NDFC supports Cisco (including Nexus, Catalyst® and ASR routers) and third-party devices.

Cisco Nexus Dashboard Orchestrator (NDO) is a centralized management solution designed to streamline the deployment, operations, and policy management of multi-site data center environments. It provides a unified interface for configuring and monitoring Cisco Application Centric Infrastructure (ACI) fabrics across different locations, enhancing operational efficiency and consistency.

Cisco Nexus Dashboard Insights (NDI) allows operators to minimize downtime by turning hardware and software telemetry (including anomalies and advisories) into insights to identify potential issues and recommendations to fix them, gathering years of experience under a single network-operations platform. It can also take advantage of its analytics to learn more about sustainability, compliance, changes, and traffic behavior (including flow records, drop, congestion, latency, AI/ML RoCEv2, and more).

Core42 ND Cluster for Private Cloud environment will host NDFC service only. Overview on NDO and NDI are provided as an reference to the capability ND as an platform offers to host and manage multiple services from a single-pane.

11.3.2  Nexus Dashboard Node Roles
Nexus Dashboard supports three roles for cluster member nodes:

Master Node– Capable of creating, receiving or transmitting information over VXLAN fabric, they manage the state of a cluster. New master nodes can be added without service disruption or taken out of service for graceful maintenance. Nexus Dashboard platform requires 3 master nodes to maintain a healthy status. It can operate with degraded performance when 1 of 3 nodes fails. When two nodes will fail, the cluster will need to be manually restored via CLI. Nexus Dashboard GUI is inaccessible in that state and applications are not in operation.

Worker Node– Additional service nodes to support master nodes executing the application workloads. Up to four worker nodes can be added to a Nexus Dashboard cluster.

Standby Master Node– The node remaining on standby, does not support running ND cluster in execution of workloads, unlike the worker node. A failed master node can be manually replaced with standby node for cluster recovery.
11.3.3  Cluster Sizing
Nexus Dashboard cluster is first deployed using 1 or 3 master nodes. Depending on the type and number of services, may be required to deploy additional worker nodes in the cluster after the initial deployment.
•    Single-node clusters are supported for a limited number of services and cannot be extended to a 3-node cluster after the initial deployment.
•    Only 3-node clusters support additional worker.
•    Deploy a single-node cluster and want to extend it to a 3-node cluster or add worker nodes, need to redeploy it as a base 3-node cluster.
•    For 3-node clusters, at least 2 master nodes are required for the cluster to remain operational. If 2 master nodes fail, the cluster will go offline and cannot be used until you recover it.

•    Core42 will deploy a cluster of 3 physical Nexus Dashboard nodes.
•    The cluster will only host a Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller service and will be used to manage VxLAN fabrics in Private Cloud environment.
•    To ensure that ND cluster communication is not dependent on the fabric managed by the cluster itself, a decision was made to connect the member nodes to the out-of-band (BMC) network.
•    To provide required level of redundancy and resiliency Core42 team decided to put each of the ND nodes in a different availability zone AZ.

11.3.4  Cluster Connectivity
Each Nexus Dashboard node is connected to two networks: Data network and Management network.
Management network at Core42 is used for:
•    Accessing Nexus Dashboard GUI
•    Accessing Nexus Dashboard CLI via SSH
•    DNS and NTP communication
•    Nexus Dashboard firmware upload

Data network at Core42 is used for:
•    Nexus Dashboard node clustering
•    Service to service communication
•    Nexus Dashboard nodes to switches communication (to access mgmt0 interface of the managed switches)

These interfaces are configured as Linux bonds (Active/standby teaming). Interfaces on the connected switch need to be set up as individual host interfaces as “switchport mode access” if VLAN is not configured on Nexus Dashboard interface. Otherwise, the interfaces will need to be set up as trunk.

Below are the requirements for ND cluster interfaces considering specific service which will be running on Core42 ND cluster:

Table 84 ND Service-Specific Network Requirements
Nexus Dashboard Service    Management Interface    Data Interface
NDFC    Layer 2 or Layer 3 adjacent    Layer 2 or Layer 3 adjacent

Core42 BMC subnets are local to a particular Availability Zone, thus no layer-2 segment is stretched between BMC blocks in all three AZs. This enforces the ND nodes to be layer-3 adjacent - which means that data interfaces (as well as management interfaces) of all nodes will belong to different subnets and will be located multiple IP hops away from each other.

Reference:
•    https://www.cisco.com/c/dam/en/us/td/docs/dcn/tools/nd-sizing/index.html
•    https://www.cisco.com/c/en/us/td/docs/dcn/ndfc/1222/verified-scalability/cisco-ndfc-verified-scalability-1222.html

When operating in Layer 3 mode with LAN deployment type (the mode will be applied at Core42)
•    LAN Device Management Connectivity must be set to Data
•    2 IPs for SNMP/Syslog and SCP services
•    Since Endpoint-Locator (EPL) is enabled, 1 additional IP in the data network for each fabric.
•    All persistent IPs must be part of a separate pool that must not overlap with the management or data subnets
•    For more information about Layer 3 mode for persistent IPs, see the Persistent IP section in this document.

Nexus Dashboard Internal Networks:
•    The Data network interface is connected to out-of-band Management network and uses 10/25Gb Ethernet ports. In Core42 implementation Data Interface will be connected to BMC Spine 5/6 Switches (also acts as BMC Aggregation/Spine in BMC block)
•    The Management network interface is connected to out-of-band Management network and uses 1/10Gb Ethernet ports. In Core42 implementation Management Interface will be connected to out-of-band leaf switches.

For communication between the containers used by the Nexus Dashboard, the following two internal networks are required:
•    Application overlay– Used for applications internally within Nexus Dashboard. It must be a /16 network, and a default value are prepopulated during deployment.
•    Service overlay– Used internally by the Nexus Dashboard. It must be a /16 network, and a default value are prepopulated during deployment

Application and Service Overlay network addresses are never exposed outside and any traffic on these networks is routed internally. Communications between containers deployed in different Nexus Dashboard nodes are VXLAN-encapsulated and use the data interfaces' IP addresses as source and destination.

The following table gives the Round-Trip Time requirements:
Table 85 ND RTT Requirements
Application    Connectivity    Maximum RTT
Nexus Dashboard Cluster    Between Nodes    150 ms
Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller    Between Nodes    50 ms
    To Switches    200 ms

11.4  Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller (NDFC) Overview
Cisco Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller (NDFC) is the next generation of Cisco Data Center Network Manager (DCNM). NDFC is the comprehensive management and automation solution for all Cisco Nexus and Cisco Multilayer Distributed Switching (MDS) platforms powered by Cisco NX-OS. NDFC provides management, automation, control, monitoring, and integration for deployments spanning LAN, SAN, and IP Fabric For Media (IPFM) fabrics.
•    Management: NDFC provides fabric-oriented configuration and operations management. It is optimized for large deployments with little overhead, but traditional deployments are supported and can be customized by the user to meet business needs. NDFC also provides representational state transfer (RESTful) APIs to allow easy integration from Cisco or third-party overlay managers and enable the automation to meet customer’s needs.
•    Automation: NDFC brings an easy-to-understand and simple deployment approach to bootstrapping new fabrics. Cisco’s best practices are built into the fabric builder policy templates, and automatic bootstrap occurs with the click of a button, reducing provisioning times and simplifying deployments.
•    Monitoring and visualization: NDFC maintains the active topology monitoring views per fabric into the new NDFC UI. When combined with Cisco’s Nexus Dashboard Insights (NDI), customers can complement their solution with advanced support for day-2 operations.
11.4.1  NDFC Scalability
This chapter presents verified scalability metrics for various deployment types of the NDFC, Release 12.2.2. The values listed below have been validated through rigorous testing on dedicated testbeds. It is advised not to reach the maximum scalability limits to ensure optimal performance and reliability.

11.4.2  NDFC Management Connectivity
In deploying the Cisco Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller (NDFC), Core42 has opted for Layer 3 (L3) connectivity to manage VxLAN fabric switches. This approach involves configuring the Nexus Dashboard to establish IP reachability between its nodes and the switch management interfaces, which can be accessed through either the management (mgmt0) interface or via in-band (IB) interfaces on the data network. This setup allows for efficient and centralized management of the network infrastructure, leveraging the advanced features of NDFC.

In Core42 infrastructure the NDFC will access and configure the switches using the out-of-band method - will connect to mgmt0 interface of the switches.

11.4.2.1  Nexus Dashboard Interfaces are Layer-3 Adjacent to Switch Mgmt0 Interface
The Switch mgmt0, IP address is not part of the subnets associated with either Nexus Dashboard interface. For this typical layer-3 reachability scenario, the switch mgmt0 interface is reachable via the Nexus Dashboard Management Interface.

In most deployments the Nexus Dashboard Management Interface is used for switch discovery, monitoring, configuration deployments, image management, and out-of-band POAP. The Nexus Dashboard Data Interface is used for the Endpoint Locator.

List of configuration tasks required:
Add static route(s) associated with the ND Management interface pointing to the switch mgmt0 subnet(s).
Define the persistent IP addresses used for SCP-POAP and SNMP-trap services in the Nexus Dashboard Management subnet.

In case of Core42 ND cluster members will be Layer-3 adjacent - will have interfaces in different IP subnets. This deployment model requires ND Data Interface to be used for the connectivity towards the switches, including switch discovery, configuration deployment, image management etc.
11.4.3  Persistent IP Addresses
Nexus Dashboard requires some services (such as SNMP trap, syslog, and others) to send data from the switches to the service. An IP address is configured on the switches for this purpose. Typically, if the IP address changes when the service is relocated, the service will reconfigure the new IP address on the switches.

In order to avoid this IP reconfiguration impact on the fabric switches, persistent IP addresses can be provided for services that require to retain the same IP address even if they are relocated to a different Nexus Dashboard node.

In case of Layer 2 adjacency between the ND nodes - the Persistent IP addresses belong to the same subnet as relevant ND interface (management of data).

The approach described above is not possible in case of Layer 3 adjacency between the ND nodes. In that scenario, persistent IPs are advertised out of each node’s data interface via BGP session established with external neighbor. The IPs must not overlap with any of the nodes' management or data subnets.

Only a Nexus Dashboard node hosting particular service (eg SNMP trap destination) advertises the Persistent IP assigned to that service to the external neighbor. In case of the Node failure, a service is moved/re-located to another available node. The node to which a service was moved starts advertising Persistent IP to the external neighbor - thus ensuring that the traffic is directed to a node on which particular service is operational at any given point in time.

Table below summarizes the persistent IP addresses to be used in for Nexus Dashboard cluster used in Private Cloud environment. The specified persistent IPs are assigned automatically by NDFC to the respective feature/service, there is no manual way to assign specific IP to a given feature.

Table 97 Persistent IP addresses
Persistent IP address
10.95.255.1/32
10.95.255.2/32
10.95.255.3/32
10.95.255.4/32
10.95.255.5/32
10.95.255.6/32
10.95.255.7/32

11.4.4  BGP On All Nexus Dashboard Nodes
To operate in Layer 3 mode, Border Gateway Protocol (BGP) must be enabled and properly configured on all nodes within the cluster. Each cluster node requires configuration of at least one BGP peer to effectively advertise the persistent IPv4 or IPv6 addresses. This ensures that the network can dynamically exchange routing information and maintain connectivity across the Layer 3 network infrastructure.

BGP configuration on ND allows to specify only a limited amount of parameters, which include:
•    local AS number (AS number of ND node),
•    IP address of the BGP neighbor,
•    AS number of the BGP neighbor.

BGP session between Nexus Dashboard Nodes and external network is only used to advertise Persistent IP addresses and attract traffic destined for those IPs to a correct Node. The BGP sessions between ND nodes and external routers are not used for any other route advertisement/manipulation.

A table below summarizes the details of BGP sessions configured on ND nodes.

Table 98 BGP sessions configured on the Nexus Dashboard nodes
ND node Name    ND AS number    BGP Peer IP address    BGP peer AS number
aead3b-a1-ndfc-01    65001    10.92.215.41    65103
aead5b-a1-ndfc-01    65001    10.93.215.41    65203
aead7b-a1-ndfc-01    65001    10.94.215.41    65303

11.5  NDFC Fabric Types
NDFC has pre-defined workflows defined to implement the network environments. Below snapshot shows few important workflows.
Figure 148 NDFC Different Types of Fabrics

11.5.1  Multi-Site Domain for VXLAN BGP EVPN Fabrics
A Multi-Site Domain (MSD) is a multi-fabric container that is created to manage multiple member fabrics. An MSD is a single point of control for definition of overlay networks and VRFs that are shared across member fabrics. When you move fabrics (that are designated to be part of the multi-fabric overlay network domain) under the MSD as member fabrics, the member fabrics share the networks and VRFs created at the MSD-level. This way, you can consistently provision network and VRFs for different fabrics, at one go. It significantly reduces the time and complexity involving multiple fabric provisioning.

As server networks and VRFs are shared across the member fabrics (as one stretched network), the new networks and VRFs provisioning function is provided at the MSD fabric level. Any new network and VRF creation is only allowed for the MSD. All member fabrics inherit any new network and VRF created for the MSD.

The topology view for the MSD fabric displays all member fabrics, and how they are connected to each other, in one view. You can deploy overlay networks (and VRFs) on member fabrics from a single topology deployment screen, instead of visiting each member fabric deployment screen separately and deploying.

11.7  NDFC Endpoint Locator
The Endpoint Locator (EPL) feature allows real-time tracking of endpoints within a data center. The tracking includes tracing the network life history of an endpoint and getting insights into the trends that are associated with endpoint additions, removals, moves, and so on. An endpoint is anything with at least one IP address (IPv4 and\or IPv6) and MAC address. EPL feature is also capable of displaying MAC-Only endpoints. By default, MAC-Only endpoints are not displayed. An endpoint can be a virtual machine (VM), container, bare-metal server, service appliance and so on.

EPL displays endpoints that have at least one IP address (IPv4 and/or IPv6). EPL is also capable of displaying MAC-Only endpoints. Select the Process MAC-Only Advertisements checkbox while configuring EPL to enable processing of EVPN Route-type 2 advertisements having a MAC address only. L2VNI:MAC is the unique endpoint identifier for all such endpoints. EPL can now track endpoints in Layer-2 only network deployments where the Layer-3 gateway is on a firewall, load-balancer, or other such nodes.

EPL relies on BGP updates to track endpoint information. Hence, typically the Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller must peer with the BGP Route-Reflector (RR) to get these updates. For this purpose, IP reachability from the Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller to the RR is required. This can be achieved over in-band network connection to the Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller Data Network interface. There is no option to configure static routes for pods on ND, so the selected RRs must be reachable through the default data network gateway.

From NDFC Release 12.0.1a, Persistent or External IP addresses are required to enable EPL. For each VXLAN fabric, a specific container is spawned running a BGP instance to peer with the spines of the fabric. This container must have a persistent IP associated that is then configured as a iBGP neighbour on the spines. A different container is used for each fabric, so the number of fabrics that are managed by NDFC where EPL is enabled decides how many persistent IP addresses must be distributed for EPL. Also, the EPL establishes iBGP sessions only over the Cisco Nexus Dashboard Data interface.
Figure 157 Endpoint Locator


Figure 158 Endpoint Locator Information


Endpoint locator (EPL) feature will be enabled for following 4 Fabrics at Core42.
1.    aead3-a1-fab-01
2.    aead5-a1-fab-02
3.    aead7-a1-fab-03
4.    aead3-a1-nws-01

11.8  Switch software upgrade using NDFC
An NDFC feature named "Fabric Software" allows to deploy Cisco software images to switches. This supports network stability and feature consistency. The benefits of Fabric Software workflows include the following:
•    Comprehensive image management policies allow you to specify a version and patch level for a set of switches
•    Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller (NDFC) validates compliance for the image policy associated with each switch
•    Image staging, validation, and in-service software upgrade (ISSU) operations are independent, allowing mass upgrades and downgrades, and the ability to perform staging and validation in a single step

You can perform the following operations before the maintenance window:
•    Stage image files
•    This copies the image files to the bootflash
•    Validate Network Operating System (NOS) and EPLD compatibility where possible
•    This checks if the image is complete, if the image is valid for the individual hardware, and if the upgrade can be non-disruptive.
•    Run pre-upgrade reports

Following capabilities provided:
•    ability to run reports pre/post-upgrade and compare the results
•    ability to generate snapshots of the configuration pre/post-upgrade
•    View Details column provides Live log status to monitor each operation
•    Allows you to make use of maintenance mode to minimize the impact of disruptive upgrades, especially for multi-reload upgrade situations
•    Allows you to perform multiple operations on the devices simultaneously. However, you cannot perform multiple operations on the same set of devices at the same time.
•    Upgrade groups allow bulk upgrades and downgrades. Upgrade groups have checks to avoid unnecessary downtime in redundant fabrics in the following cases:
•    All switches of a given role for a fabric will be in the same group
•    Switches that are grouped in odd or even groups:
•    Switches that have even numbers or VPC role of primary
•    Switches that have odd numbers or VPC role of secondary
•    Provides visibility into previous and current upgrade details as well as high level summarization
•    Visibility into current NOS, EPLD and patch consistency at a switch, fabric, and group level

11.8.4.1  Preparing a Fabric Image Policy
Table 103 Preparing a Fabric Image Policy
Step#    Description

None: No fabric-level image policy has been configured for this fabric.
In-Sync: The current image versions on each switch in the fabric is in-sync with the expected image versions. No further configurations are needed for the fabric-level image policy for this fabric.
Out-of-Sync: The current image versions on one or more switches in the fabric is out-of-sync with the expected image versions. Click the Out-of-Sync text in the Status column to bring up a slide-in pane that shows which switches in the fabric are out-of-sync with the expected image versions.
Prepare: Click to prepare a software image policy that can be used at the fabric level, as described in this topic.
Update plan: The system is ready to apply software updates to the appropriate switches in the fabric. Click Update plan to bring up the Software Update Plan for the fabric.

For each of the actions listed below, you can click the box next to single switch in the update group or click the box next to Switch to select all the switches in the update group. The following options are then displayed above the update group:
Remove from Update Group: Click to remove the selected switch(es) from the update group.
Create New Update Group: Click to create a new update group with the selected switch(es).
Move to Update Group: Click to move the selected switch(es) to a different update group.
Step 9    To upgrade the switches in an update group, perform the following steps in that group's area in the Software Update Plan window:
First click Stage/Distribute to stage and validate the update.
Click View Logs to view the actions that take place on the NX-OS switches as they relate to NDFC.
Then click Install Update to perform the software update on the switches in this update group.
Install Update allows you to trigger a software upgrade for all the switches in that update group.
A confirmation dialog appears that provides an estimated timeframe for the installation process based on the number of switches in the update group.
(Optional) In the Install Update confirmation dialog, click the Show Advanced Options arrow to make the advanced selections for the installation, if necessary.
In the Execution Paradigm field, select a Serial or Parallel execution:
•    Serial: Switches are updated one at a time, serially, where the update process must fully complete on one switch before it begins on the next switch in the update group.
•    Parallel: Switches are updated all at one time, depending on the number of switches that can be updated concurrently. For example, if you have 40 switches in the update group but 20 switches are updated at a time concurrently, then a Parallel setting will result in the first set of 20 switches going through the update process, all at one time, and then the second set of 20 switches going through the same concurrent update process after the update process has completed for the first set.
In the On Failure field, determine if you want the installation to Continue or to Pause if there is a failure that occurs during the update. A failure might occur for a number of reasons, such as a switch failing to come back online or a failure to successfully establish an ssh session between NDFC and the switch.
In the Snapshot field, choose either the Snapshot or No Snapshot option.
Check the box next to Maintenance Mode if you want to enable this option.
Make the proper selection in the Force Disruptive field, if necessary.

11.9  Replacing faulty Physical Primary ND node without standby node in place
This procedure describes steps required to replace a single primary node in the cluster. It's applicable to hardware related issues, which mandate physical replacement. If the node would reach an unexpected/undesired software state, the "acs reboot clean" command can be used to wipe the node and re-add it to the cluster.

Before the replacement procedure is conducted, the following steps should be performed:
Ensure that at least 2 primary nodes are healthy.
Ensure that the primary node you want to replace is powered off.
Prepare and deploy the new node.
Ensure that you have the same CIMC IP address and login information on the new node as you configured for the failed node.
The remaining primary nodes will use the CIMC information to restore configuration to the new node.
Ensure that the new node is powered on and note down its serial number.

To replace a single failed primary node:
1.    Log in to your Nexus Dashboard GUI using the management IP of one of the other primary nodes.
2.    From the main navigation menu, select Manage > Nodes.
3.    In the nodes list, find the Serial number of the node you want to replace and ensure that the node’s Status shows Inactive.
4.    In the Nexus Dashboard’s Nodes screen, select the inactive node by clicking the checkbox next to it.
5.    From the available Actions menu, select Replace.
6.    Provide the CIMC IP Address, CIMC login Username and Password for the new node, then click Verify.
7.    The cluster will connect to the new node’s CIMC IP address to verify connectivity and populate the Serial Number field.
8.    Click Replace to finish replacing the node.
9.    In the New Serial Number field, provide the serial number of the new node and click Replace.
After the process is completed, you will see the serial number of the old node updated to the new node’s serial number and the status will change to Active once the new primary has successfully joined the cluster.

Figure 161 Nexus Dashboard node replacement


Figure 162 Nexus Dashboard node replacement


11.10  Adding a standby node to the ND Cluster
Below short procedure depicts how to add a standby Nexus Dashboard node to an existing cluster.
Log in to the Cisco Nexus Dashboard GUI.
From the main navigation menu, select Manage > Nodes.
In the main pane, click Add Node.
The Add Node screen opens.
In the Add Node screen, provide the node information.
f)    Provide the Credentials information for the node, then click Verify.
g)    For physical nodes, this is the IP address, username, and password of the server’s CIMC. The CIMC will be used to configure the rest of the information on the node.
h)    For virtual nodes, this is the IP address and rescue-user password you defined for the node when deploying it.
i)    Provide the Name of the node.
j)    From the Type dropdown, select Standby.
k)    Provide the Management Network information.
l)    For virtual nodes, the management network information will be pre-populated with the information pulled from the node based on the IP address and credentials you provided in the previous sub-step.
m)    For physical nodes, you must provide the management network IP address, netmask, and gateway now.
n)    Provide the Data Network information.
o)    You must provide the data network IP address, netmask, and gateway. Optionally, you can also provide the VLAN ID for the network. For most deployments, you can leave the VLAN ID field blank.
p)    (Optional) Provide IPv6 information for the management and data networks.
q)    Starting with release 2.1.1, Nexus Dashboard supports dual stack IPv4/IPv6 for the management and data networks.
r)    If you want to provide IPv6 information, you must do it when adding the node.
s)    All nodes in the cluster must be configured with either only IPv4 or dual IPv4/IPv6 stack.
t)    Click Save to add the node.
The configuration will be pushed to the node and the node will be added to the list in the GUI.
