11.11  Zero-Touch Provisioning of VxLAN switches
The provisioning process of Cisco Nexus switches within a VXLAN fabric can be automated using the Power On Auto Provisioning (POAP) feature available on Cisco Nexus switches. This feature streamlines the initial configuration by automatically downloading a predefined configuration script when the switch is powered on, thereby reducing manual intervention.

In addition, the Nexus Dashboard Fabric Controller (NDFC) further enhances automation by fully managing the deployment of new data centers. NDFC reduces the overall time and effort required for deployment, while also minimizing the risk of human errors.

POAP
Power On Auto Provisioning (POAP) enables zero-touch provisioning of devices by automating their initial configuration. When a POAP-enabled device boots without a startup configuration, it enters POAP mode, locates a DHCP server, and retrieves its interface IP address, gateway, and DNS server IPs. The device then obtains a TFTP server IP to download a configuration script, which facilitates the installation of the appropriate software image and configuration file

Cisco NDFC supports POAP for VXLAN fabrics in two modes:
•    NDFC as Layer-2 adjacent
•    NDFC over Layer-3

In the Core42 network design, the switch management IPs will be manually configured for Private Cloud Fabrics. POAP over a BMC Layer-3 network can be considered for bringing up other fabrics. In this scenario NDFC is connected to the fabric over routed infrastructure. This offers design flexibility for NDFC. This requires some routing configuration along with DCHP relay that is configured on router’s interface connected to the seed switch.
11.11.1.1  Seed Switch
Seed switch acts as the entry point to the fabric for NDFC and like any other device, seed switch is also part of the fabric. Seed switch can be of any role Spine, Border, or Leaf and a fabric can have more than one seed switches.
A seed switch has two types of interfaces:
•    Seed (bootstrap) interface
•    Core-facing (fabric-facing) interface
The interface on seed switch facing NDFC is referred as seed (bootstrap) interface which is configured as Layer-3 interface and assigned an IP address over DHCP.
In case of multiple seed switches in a fabric, the fabric facing interfaces must be consistent across all the seed switches.

While the concept of a Seed Switch is more relevant in in-band management scenarios where it provides initial connectivity and management within the fabric, an out-of-band setup generally does not rely on Seed Switches in the same way. Instead, it leverages the dedicated management network for device discovery and provisioning.
11.11.1.2  DHCP Relay
The DHCP relay feature is crucial in networking environments where the DHCP server is not located on the same local network segment as the client devices. In the context of Cisco's Power On Auto Provisioning (POAP) and Plug and Play (PnP) features, DHCP relay is used to direct DHCP requests from newly connected devices to the appropriate DHCP server. This is especially important in scenarios where the devices need to obtain configuration scripts or software images from a server that is not on the same local network.

All interfaces of edge router connected to fabric switches should have DHCP Relay configured with Nexus Dashboard Data interface IP address.
Configuration Template 22 An example DHCP Relay configuration
interface Ethernet1/1
  description ** connected to Seed switch BGW1 **
  no switchport
  ip address 172.16.10.1/24
  ip dhcp relay address 192.168.1.245
  no shutdown

11.11.1.3  DHCP Scope
For POAP, DHCP server manages the temporary IP addresses to the devices during bootstrap process. Also, details of Script and Software/Image repository servers are also provided along with DHCP. For BMC POAP, DHCP scope must be defined for management subnet. The IP addresses to all the devices are assigned from this scope over DHCP.

To enable local DHCP, in the Fabric Settings, check Enable Local DHCP Server and define DHCP scope parameters in Start Address, End Address and Default Gateway, Subnet Prefix fields.

Figure 165 Configuring DHCP Scope


11.11.1.4  Adding Switches
1)    On the Fabric Overview window, choose Actions > Add Switches.
2)    In the Switch Addition Mechanism area, click the Bootstrap radio button.
3)    Enter the Admin password in the Admin Password field.
The Switches to Bootstrap table lists all the discovered switches.
4)    Select the switches you want to add to the fabric and click Import Selected Switches.

Figure 166 Adding Switches


11.12  Backup and Restore
Starting with Nexus Dashboard 3.2.1 release, backup and restore is no longer available at NDFC service level. Instead, a unified backup and restore is available at ND level. The ND level backs up not only the configuration information for ND, but also for any services (such as NDI, NDO, or NDFC) running in that ND.

Following are the high-level guidelines on backing up and restoring ND and services configurations.
•    The ND unified backup and restore feature is supported only between the same versions of ND. Performing a backup on version X of ND and then restore on version Y of ND is not allowed.
•    After restoring a backup, the password might change to an older password where the backup was collected.
•    When you perform a backup, all services that are part of the Nexus Dashboard at that time (any services listed in the Current Deployment Mode) are backed up

The following guidelines apply when backing up and restoring NDFC configurations:
•    Check the Ignore External Service IP Configuration check box, as described in Restoring ND and Services Configurations below, whenever you take a backup on one system and restore it on a different system, with different management and/or data subnets.
•    NDFC has two backup and restore options: Config-only and Full. When performing a restore from a backup:
•    You can perform a Config-only restore on existing NDFC deployments where features are enabled, and other states exist.
•    You can perform a Full restore only on a freshly installed cluster, or you must perform an acs reboot clean on the cluster before you can perform a Full restore
11.12.1.1  Backing Up ND and Services Configurations
A Nexus Dashboard and services configuration backup can be performed in two modes: manual and scheduled.
11.12.1.1.1  Manually Backing Up ND and Services Configurations
1)    Navigate to the unified backup and restore page in the Admin Console GUI:
Admin > Backup & Restore Backups that are already configured are listed in the Backups page.
2)    Click Create Backup.
The Create Backup slider appears.
3)    Review the information provided in the Current Deployment Mode area.
4)    In the Name field, enter a name for this backup.
5)    In the Type field, determine whether you want a Config-Only or a Full backup.
a)    Config-Only: A Config-Only backup is smaller than a Full backup, which is described below. It contains the following configuration data, depending on the services that are being backed up:
i)    Fabric Controller: Schedules, templates, policies, and other configured parameters
ii)    Insights: Compliance rules, settings, and other configured parameters
iii)    Orchestrator: Templates, settings, and other configured parameters
b)    Full: A Full backup is large. In addition to everything in a Config-Only backup, a Full backup also contains operational data, such as statistics, counters, and so on. Operational data is only applicable for Fabric Controller; other services will only have configuration backed up.

ND Full backup Note
Note that you cannot perform a full restore on a cluster that has an existing configuration; the backup must be restored on a new cluster with no existing configuration in this case.
Alternatively you must perform an “acs reboot clean” on the existing cluster before you can perform a Full restore, as described at:
https://www.cisco.com/c/en/us/td/docs/dcn/nd/3x/articles-321/nexus-
dashboard-troubleshooting-321.html
6)    In the Destination field, determine whether you want a local or remote backup.
a)    Local Download: The backup data is stored on the local cluster.

Encryption Key
You must provide an encryption key In the Encryption Key field. Save the key in order to restore from the backup.

b)    Remote Location: The backup data is stored in a remote location.

12  Identity Service Engine (ISE)
The primary function of Cisco Identity Services Engine (ISE) for Core42 is to provide device administration within its data centers through TACACS+ (Terminal Access Controller Access-Control System Plus) and RADIUS. This allows Core42 to manage and enforce security policies for devices within its network environment. Devices attempting to connect to Core42's network are identified and given appropriate levels of access based on their credentials. This identification and authorization process ensures that only authorized devices can access critical network resources. While ISE comprises various personas that perform specific functions, in this setup, the focus is solely on using the device administration capabilities of ISE. These capabilities are distributed across two ISE nodes, deployed as physical appliances, to ensure robust and scalable device management in Core42's data center.

12.1  ISE Key Design Decisions
In the previous questionnaire documented we aimed to discuss and capture on Core42’s goals and objectives. This mutual agreement and understanding allows for measurable success, faster identification and remediation of issues, and final delivery results that meet business and technical objectives. The key objectives of the project are summarized in the following table.

Table 105 Key Design Decisions
KDD ID    Key Description
KDD 1    Deploy a new Cisco ISE 3.3 environment for device administration using TACACS+ and RADIUS.
KDD 2    Deploy two (2) Cisco ISE 3755 physical appliances in a primary-secondary high availability setup.
KDD 3    Implement consistent access policies for all network devices across Core42's data centres.
KDD 4    Integrate ISE with LDAP directory services for user authentication instead of Active Directory.
KDD 5    Enforce authentication and authorization controls for network administrators and operators based on defined RBAC policies.
KDD 6    Utilize TACACS+ for CLI access management and RADIUS for GUI authentication where applicable.
KDD 7    Define role-based access control (RBAC) with different roles for network device administration.
KDD 8    NIC bonding will be used on Gig0 and Gig1 interfaces for high availability, ensuring each appliance has one assigned IP.

12.3  ISE Personas and Roles Overview
There are four ISE personas that can be applied to an ISE system. When it aligns with the current and future project needs, multiple personas can generally be combined on a system. The following table summarizes the ISE personas.

Table 111 ISE Persona Summary
Persona    Description
Administration    The Administration persona provides administrative functions relating to management of the ISE deployment and configurations. An Administration persona is also referred to as a Policy Administration Node (PAN).
Monitoring    The Monitoring persona functions as a log collector. There must be at least one Monitoring node in a distributed deployment. At the time of PAN configuration, the Monitoring persona must be enabled. After a secondary Monitoring node is registered in the deployment, you can edit the PAN and disable the Monitoring persona if required.
Policy Service    Session Services: Policy Service Nodes (PSNs) with Session Services enabled control network access, posture, guest, and client provisioning services.
Profiler Service: When enabled and configured, this service provides the capabilities necessary to conduct endpoint profiling.
SXP Service: This service enables policy enforcement by incorporating Security Group Tags and TrustSec.
Device Admin Service: This service provides the capabilities to create TACACS+ policy sets, policy results, etc. to control and audit the configuration of network devices.
pxGrid    Cisco pxGrid is used to share context-sensitive information from the Cisco ISE session directory with other policy network systems such as Cisco Adaptive Security Appliance (ASA) and Cisco Firepower. The pxGrid framework can also be used to exchange policy and configuration data between nodes (e.g., sharing tags and policy objects between ISE and third-party vendors) and for non-ISE related information exchanges (e.g., threat information).

Note
PxGrid will not be enabled at deployment and requires an Advantage license to function.

12.5.3  Software Version
The Cisco ISE version chosen for this deployment plays a critical role in licensing, support, and feature availability. The table below specifies the agreed-upon ISE version for this deployment.

When selecting an ISE version, organizations typically base their decision on feature requirements or long-term platform support. Cisco follows a versioning model where:

•    Even-numbered sub-versions (e.g., 3.2, 3.4) are Long-Term Support (LTS) releases, offering 18 to 24 months of patches and development support.
•    Odd-numbered sub-versions (e.g., 3.1, 3.3) are Feature Releases, designed for early access to new features but with shorter support cycles of 12 to 18 months. After this period, customers are encouraged to migrate to the next LTS version for continued stability.

The following table outlines the ISE version selected for this deployment, ensuring alignment with Core42’s operational and support requirements.

Table 118 Deployed ISE Version

Note
The software version may change at the time of the implementation based on the software selection exercise.

12.5.4  Architectural Redundancy
ISE nodes will be deployed in a redundant manner to ensure high availability for the entirety of the deployment. All configuration changes made on the Primary PAN server will be synced to all nodes within the ISE deployment. With ISE versions 1.4 and greater, ISE supports automatic PAN failover allowing the secondary PAN to be automatically promoted to the primary PAN role. If automatic failover is not enabled and if the primary administration node goes down, then the secondary node will need to be promoted to primary while the original primary node is repaired. All authentications in the network continue to function correctly if the Administration nodes are not performing PSN roles.
From a logs and perspective, Both MnT nodes in the ISE deployment receive logging data from the entire ISE domain. If the Primary MnT instance fails, the backup instance will automatically take over monitoring functions. When the Primary instance is back online, the log data will need to be manually transferred to the Primary MnT node from the Secondary. There is no promotion or demotion action needed for MNT failover.
Finally, from a PSN or Policy Nodes perspective, all nodes are active and servicing at the same time. The redundancy is achieved and performed by configuring more than one Radius Server on the Network Access Devices such as the wired access switches or the wireless LAN controllers. In case a primary PSN goes down, the Network Access Device will failover to the secondary configured Radius Server due to timeout and retries of Radius Packets. When the Primary PSN is back online, and upon the expiry of the dead-time or back-of time that is configured on the Network Access Devices, it will be re-used again.
12.5.5  ISE 3.0 Licensing
ISE version 3.0 employs a new subscription-only based model for ISE licensing with Smart License SKUs. In the new model, three subscription-based license tiers exist, namely ISE Essentials, ISE Advantage, and ISE Premier.
This new model is referred to as a nested-doll model, which means that the higher tier license already uses all the lower-tier features. For example, the ISE Premier license includes all ISE Advantage and ISE Essentials features. Similarly, the ISE Advantage license includes all ISE Essential features. The subscription term for each tier is 1,3 and 5 years.

12.6  Identity Stores
12.6.1  Identities

Since Core42's ISE deployment is focused solely on device administration, authentication will be configured for network administrators rather than end users or endpoints.

Administrator Authentication in Cisco ISE
Cisco ISE supports administrator authentication through external identity stores, including LDAP, RSA SecureID, and local ISE identity stores. Two authentication models are available:

External Authentication and Authorization
•    Authentication and authorization are handled entirely by an external identity store (LDAP).
•    No local Cisco ISE credentials are needed for the administrator.
•    Core42 will use this model with LDAP for centralized authentication.
External Authentication with Internal Authorization
•    Authentication is performed using an external identity source (e.g., RSA SecureID).
•    Authorization and role assignment occur within Cisco ISE’s local database.
•    Not applicable to Core42’s deployment, as all authentication and authorization will be handled through LDAP
.

Figure 170 ISE Identity Management

12.6.1.1  Internal Identity Store
ISE provides its own database. It can be used to store the identity of:
•    Regular users
•    Endpoints (Mac Address)
•    Guest users (only visible via the Sponsor Portal)

Note
Details regarding user and group configuration in the ISE internal identity store can be found in the following location (ISE 3.3 UI Navigation):
Administration > Identity Management > Identities
12.6.1.2  Internal Identity Store – Endpoints
ISE has an internal database that stores and manages endpoint identities. This identity store is used to authenticate and authorize network devices such as laptops, mobile phones, printers, and IoT devices. This functionality is not used in Core42 deployment.
12.6.1.3  Internal Identity Store – Users
ISE has an internal user store that can be used for admin access to devices. Most customers opt to use External Identity Sources for the same, but the internal database is available if needed.

ISE 3.3 UI Navigation: Administrator > Identity Management > Identity

12.6.2  Identity Groups
ISE Identity Groups are used to group individual identities to allow conditions in policies to be applied to groups and make the management of conditions logical and efficient. ISE creates a set of endpoint Identity Groups by default and allows the creation of custom Identity Groups to which endpoints can be assigned dynamically or statically.
12.7  External Authentication Identity Sources
ISE supports a number of External Authentication Services that can be integrated with ISE. ISE can call External Identity Sources to authenticate users against. Organizations commonly integrate ISE into an existing environment that already handles network-based authentication to resources, such as Microsoft Active Directory.

External Authentication Identity Sources can be called in Identity Sequences that are configured and leveraged in rules to allow granular authentication configurations based on specified criteria for the rule.
The following table identifies the External Identity Sources configured for this project.

Table 122 External Authentication Identity Sources
Enabled    Identity Source Type    Details
Yes    LDAP    LDAP will be used to validate accounts of users who access the network devices.
12.7.1  LDAP External Identity Source
Cisco ISE supports integration with Lightweight Directory Access Protocol (LDAP) as an external identity store for user authentication and authorization. LDAP does not require domain membership. Instead, ISE connects to an LDAP directory service using a bind DN and queries the directory for user authentication, group membership retrieval, and attribute-based authorization.
At Core42, Cisco ISE will integrate with an LDAP directory service, allowing ISE to function as an external authentication source where users and groups are stored in a hierarchical directory structure.
ISE will:
•    Authenticate users by searching for their distinguished name (DN) in the LDAP directory and validating their credentials.
•    Retrieve group memberships for authorization policies.
•    Query LDAP attributes to enforce role-based access control (RBAC) and other security policies.

Cisco ISE performs authentication against LDAP using plain password authentication. The authentication process follows these steps:

User Authentication:
•    ISE searches the LDAP directory for an entry that matches the username in the authentication request.
•    If found, ISE performs an LDAP bind request using the user's DN and password.
•    If the credentials match, authentication succeeds.

Group Membership Retrieval:
•    After successful authentication, ISE retrieves the user’s group memberships to enforce role-based access control (RBAC).

Attribute-Based Authorization:
•    ISE queries LDAP for specific user attributes that can be used in authorization policies.

LDAP integration does not require ISE to "join" the directory. Instead, a service account (bind DN) is used to perform user lookups and group membership queries.
The LDAP bind DN service account must have:

•    Read access to user and group objects.
•    The ability to search and retrieve user attributes required for authentication.
•    Permissions to query group membership information.

Note
The LDAP service account must have sufficient read permissions to retrieve user attributes and group memberships.

Important Considerations:
•    ISE sends LDAP bind requests in clear text unless LDAPS (port 636) is enabled for encryption.
•    Time synchronization between ISE servers and LDAP servers is critical for authentication to work correctly. NTP should be properly configured.
•    DNS resolution (forward and reverse) must be correctly configured for LDAP servers.

ISE 3.3 UI Navigation:
Administration > Identity Management > External Identity Sources > LDAP
The following table identifies the settings for the LDAPs External Authentication Identity Source.
Table 123 LDAP Identity Source Settings
Setting    Value
LDAP Server IP/Hostname    az1-idm1.private.core42.systems
Identity Store Name    C42_PC_LDAP
Port    636 (LDAPS - Secure)
Base DN (Search Scope)    cn=users,cn=accounts,dc=private,dc=core42,dc=systems
cn=groups,cn=accounts,dc=private,dc=core42,dc=systems
Bind DN (Service Account)    uid=service_ise_user,cn=users,cn=accounts,dc=private,dc=core42,dc=systems
Bind Password    *********
Authentication Method    Simple Bind
SSL/TLS Encryption    Enabled (LDAPS Recommended)
Password Change Support    Yes
Enable Group Retrieval    Yes
Group Membership Attribute    memberOf
Search Timeout    10 seconds (default)

LDAP Authentication Flow
•    ISE will search for the user in the Base DN scope.
•    If found, ISE will perform an LDAP bind with the user’s credentials.
•    If successful, ISE will retrieve group memberships and apply authorization policies.
•    Attributes from LDAP will be used to determine access permissions.
LDAP Group and Attribute Retrieval for Authorization Policies
After authentication, Cisco ISE retrieves group memberships and attributes from LDAP to apply authorization rules.
•    LDAP groups and attributes can be used in:
Authorization Policies – Assign different access levels based on group membership.
•    RBAC (Role-Based Access Control) – Enforce role-based access using LDAP attributes.
•    Custom Policies – Use retrieved attributes (e.g., department, location) to grant or deny access.
ISE supports two LDAP group membership retrieval models:
1.    Groups Refer to Users – Group objects contain member attributes that list users.
2.    Users Refer to Groups – User objects contain an attribute that specifies the groups they belong to.
ISE must be configured to match the LDAP directory structure used at Core42.
12.8  Certificates
12.8.1  Certificate Types
The most common certificate types in ISE deployments are:
•    Single Name: These are the simplest certificates. They have a single FQDN in the CN field. Because the FQDN name on the certificate must match the FQDN name of the ISE node, a separate certificate must be installed on each ISE node.
•    Subject Alternative Name (SAN): In addition to the FQDN in the CN field of the certificate, SAN certificates contain an extension field with additional FQDNs. If the SAN certificate is provisioned with FQDNs of all ISE nodes in the deployment, the same certificate can be installed on all ISE nodes, and only a single certificate request is necessary. If the SAN certificate is used for ISE web portals, the SAN field must include friendly FQDNs of those portals.
•    Wildcard: Wildcard server certificates can be used in ISE for Admin (web-based service) and EAP protocols that use SSL/TLS tunnelling. With the use of wildcard certificates, a unique certificate for each ISE node is not required, and the SAN field does not need to be populated with multiple FQDNs as long as the “wildcard” in the certificate matches. The use of wildcard certificates is considered less secure than assigning a unique server certificate for each ISE node. When used for EAP, not all endpoint supplicants support the wildcard character in the Certificate Subject.
Client Certificates are a strong alternative to username and password as an authentication method to authorize clients to the network. When used with ISE, the following points should be considered:
•    Certificates must have an attribute in Subject or Subject Alternative Name fields identifying the owner of the certificate (e.g., user certificates should contain a short username in the CN field or User Principal Name in the SAN field).
•    The Key Usage field must contain the Digital Signature and Key Encipherment.
•    The Extended Key Usage field must contain Client Authentication.

ISE 3.3 UI Navigation: Administration > System > Certificates

Cisco recommends creating individual certificate for each service per node for the highest level of security.

12.8.2  ISE Deployment Certificates

To ensure the certificate is issued by trusted authority, the root CA certificate must be installed in ISE. Both the internal and public roots will be loaded in ISE.
12.8.3  Internal Certificate Authority
•    Since Core42's ISE deployment is focused solely on device administration, the ISE Internal Certificate Authority (CA) will be used only for securing inter-node communication and TACACS+/RADIUS authentication.
•    Key Points for Device Administration:
•    Certificates are not required for endpoint authentication since network access control (NAC) is not in scope.
•    System certificates will be used to secure communication between ISE nodes and external servers (syslog, authentication services).
•    Trusted certificates will be installed on ISE for TACACS+ and RADIUS authentication to ensure secure admin logins.
•    The Primary Administration Node (PAN) will manage certificate trust across ISE nodes.
•    The ISE CA will be configured to support secure authentication for device administration, but features related to endpoint certificate issuance will not be used.

ISE 3.3 UI Navigation: Administration > Certificates

12.10  Device Administration – TACACS+
Network and Security administrators typically own the task of administering and monitoring network and security devices in an enterprise.  Administrating management for a limited number of devices, can be straight forward and doesn't require specialized processes or workflows. But for large enterprise networks the requirements are different, and managing thousands of devices with multiple admin users with varying levels of privilege can become a nightmare if there are no processes or workflows in place. ISE provides the capability to automate device administration tasks with clean workflows and monitoring capabilities within a controlled space in the UI.
Figure 171 TACACS+ Flow

12.10.1  Design Administration models
There are 3 different types of ISE deployments when both device administration and user authentication is required. Each scenario has its own pros and cons detailed in the following table.
•    In a "Dedicated Deployment", different clusters are dedicated to RADIUS and to TACACS+. This includes a separate Administration Node (PAN) for managing policies, users/groups, network devices etc., a separate Monitoring Node (MnT) for managing TACACS+ logs and dedicated Policy Services Node for supporting TACACS+ or RADIUS service.
•    A "Dedicated PSN" deployment would define a single ISE cluster where the Administration Node and Monitoring Node are shared for managing Identities, policies, policy related configurations, logging for both TACACS+ and RADIUS. This would include dedicated PSNs node for servicing respectively incoming TACACS+ or RADIUS requests.
•    A "Shared Deployment" would define a cluster where all the node types (Administration Node, Monitoring Node, and Policy Services Node) are shared between both RADIUS and TACACS+ services.
Table 126 ISE Deployment Models
Deployment    Shared Deployment
(Shared PAN/ MnT and PSNs)
Pros/Cons    Pros:
•    Centralized policy &monitoring for all AAA needs.
•    Same configuration in all PSNs.
•    Scale all AAA services incrementally by adding a PSN when or where needed.
Cons:
•    Load from Network Access may impact Device Administration services and vice versa.
Use case    Medium/Small sized network where device administration and network access service managed by same group.
Only human admin users.
Where separation of Network access and Device admin users are not critical.

Note
In Core42 we’ll be using the shared deployment model.
12.10.2  Policy Elements
Besides the different generic elements highlighted in the previous chapters, some components are specific to Device administration and would only be in the corresponding ISE work centre.
12.10.2.1  TACACS+ Profiles
TACACS+ profiles help to exercise control over the privileges and commands that are granted to a device administrator. ISE provides a list of templates helping to generate profiles for different types of network devices.
This selection can be found in "Common tasks type". The drop-down menu allows you to choose between:
•    Shell for IOS or IOS-XE device
•    WLC for Wireless Controllers
•    Nexus for Nexus devices.
•    Generic can be used to specify custom attributes that are not available in the common tasks. It provides a list of attributes that are not recognized by the Common Tasks section. Each definition consists of an attribute name, an indication of whether the attribute is mandatory or optional, and the value for the attribute.
Figure 172 TACACS+ Profile


In the Raw View, it is possible to enter the mandatory attributes using a equal to (=) sign between the attribute name and its value and optional attributes are entered using an asterisk (*) between the attribute name and its value. The attributes entered in the Raw View are reflected in the "Custom Attributes" section in the "Task Attribute View" and vice versa.

12.10.2.2  TACACS+ Command Sets
Command sets enforce the specified list of commands that can be executed by a device administrator. When a device administrator issues operational commands on a network device, ISE is queried to determine whether the administrator is authorized to issue these commands. Command set allows to define a list of commands in the form of Commands and Arguments. Permissions can be assigned to each command in the list to either permit or deny that specific command along with its arguments.
Figure 174 Command Set


In the ISE UI, a checkbox is also present to ‘Permit any command not listed below’. This is meant, to create a list of commands that will automatically be denied, to simplify creation of Command Sets based on blacklist of commands that are not allowed for that admin role.
Remember each command set can be written specific to the admin role, so use an intuitive naming convention, so that it’s easy to understand the role and permissions associated.
12.10.2.3  Command set syntax
In a command set, a "Command" is the first word of any command supported by the targeted network device, "Argument" covers the remaining part. ISE supports wild cards in the commands and standard regular expression for arguments.
u)    GRANT permits, denies or denies always a certain command and argument.
v)    COMMAND allows you to type the command and it accepts wild cards. They are case insensitive.
w)    ARGUMENTS can be one or more parameters that follows the command. Argument takes regular expressions.
The order of precedence is Deny Always > Permit > Deny i.e. a match of command and arguments that has ‘Deny Always’ has higher precedence over a match that has ‘Permit’, rest all are denied. This is across all the command sets that is added in the authorization policy.

In the following command set used as an example, the command "interface GigabitEthernet 1" or "interface GigabitEthernet 0-999/0" are always denied  "show",  "config" and "no shut" commands are permitted. Commands to configure other interfaces are permitted as well.

Table 133 Command Set example
Grant    Command    Argument
PERMIT    config*
DENY_ALWAYS    interface    GigabitEthernet 1
DENY_ALWAYS    interface    GigabitEthernet [0-9]{1,3}/0
PERMIT    interface
PERMIT    show
PERMIT    no    shut

When multiple command sets are used in a policy, the order of operation for command authorization when ISE receives an incoming command and arguments is as follows:
•    First ISE looks at the commands and arguments across all command sets for a match.
•    Then, ISE searches for a command set that has ‘Deny Always’ for that command match. A command set that has ‘Deny Always’ takes precedence over everything except when the check box that says "Permit any command that is not listed below" is enabled.
•    After ISE analyzes all the command sets, it authorizes the command in the following way:
•    If ISE finds a command set with "Deny Always", it denies the command
•    If not, ISE permits the command based on the first match that was permitted
•    Otherwise, ISE denies the command

Best practices
For small command sets:
•    Deny unmatched commands.
•    Use PERMIT option to specify whitelist.
For large command sets:
•    Permit unmatched commands.
•    Send DENY option to specify blacklist.

Note
Core42 has not set a specific command set for users, this will be finalized during implementation.
12.10.3  Device Admin policy sets
Device admin policy sets are similar to the policy sets used for network access. They are also a logical group of authentication and authorization policies.
The first step in the processing of a TACACS requests is the selection of a policy set. Each policy set must have an associated condition. The condition for the policy set determines which policy set is selected when processing a given request. Once the policy set is matched and selected, its authentication and authorization policies are evaluated. There is always one policy set defined, which is called the default policy set.
Policy sets are first-match policies. Each policy has a condition that can be simple or compound.

Note
For the sake of clarity and ease of troubleshooting, it is usually recommended to have one policy set per device type. Different device types will use different methods to assign privileges. The command sets will also differ between devices.
This can eventually be revisited in the future but, currently, considering the very limited number of policies, there is no real need for that.
12.10.3.1  Authentication Protocol Settings
Allowed protocols define the set of protocols that Cisco ISE can use to communicate with the device that requests access to the network resources. Allowed Protocols let you create a list of protocols allowed within TACACS+ to authenticate users. ISE supports PAP, CHAP and MSCHAPv1 protocol to receive user credentials.

Note
The "Default Device Admin" preset in Cisco ISE allows all supported TACACS+ protocols by default.
The provided image is from a lab environment for reference.


In theory, for RADIUS allowed protocols, it is best practice to restrict them to only the protocols actively in use to minimize the attack surface. However, since TACACS+ encrypts communication natively, this concern is less significant in TACACS+ environments.
12.10.3.2  Authentication policies
Once an incoming request matches a policy set criteria, the request is sent to the relevant authentication policy to authenticates the users against a user database store.
Authentication conditions are optional, however they can help differentiating different type of requests, for example to authenticate users based on certain period of time, location, IP address, port etc.

Note
The default authentication policy in Cisco ISE is configured to grant access as long as the user exists in any ISE identity store, without applying additional criteria.
The provided image is from a lab environment for reference.



The identity store could be a ISE internal database or an external identity store.
12.10.3.3  Authorization Policies
Typically, the authorization policy is used to evaluate some conditions to determine the role of a user. If there is a match, then ISE will send the corresponding privileges and permissions in the form of TACACS profile only or a combination of TACACS profile and Command set for more granular control.
ISE administrators can use the TACACS+ command sets and TACACS+ profiles (policy results) to exercise control over the privileges and commands that are granted to a device administrator. The policy works in conjunction with the network devices and thereby prevents accidental or malicious configuration changes that may be done.
12.10.4  Performance considerations
12.10.4.1  Single Connect Mode
Every time an admin logs in to a switch for example, it would establish a TCP connection. When command authorization is enabled, every action a logged in admin performs on the switch will create a new TACACS+ request, and in extension a new TCP connection.
ISE has a TACACS+ feature called “single connect mode” that retains the TCP connection instead of tearing it immediately.
It is recommended to have this option enabled at least for the most active NADs. This would provide a performance boost to these devices, but the number of open sessions should also be monitored to make sure the connection table is not growing over the limits.
Figure 175 Single Connect Mode


12.10.4.2  Authorization Cache
To improve the performance of TACACS+ authorization, a cache mechanism has been introduced since ISEv2.3
At the first authorization request, the following attributes are added to the cache:
•    Username
•    User Specific Attributes (such as User Group or any other user specific attributes)
For the following authorization requests, and for the configured cache timeout (up to 5 minutes), the information in the cache is checked first.
12.10.5  Annex
12.10.5.1  General pre-requisites
12.10.5.1.1  TACACS+ configuration for IOS
To be able to use tacacs+, aaa needs to be globally enabled on the device.

TACACS server ise-1
 address ip4 1.2.3.4
 key ISEisC00L

aaa group server TACACS+ ISE-TACACS
 server name ise-1

Authentication

The following command specifies that tacacs+ be used for all interfaces, except line con 0, with the default method list. The local keyword ensures that the NAD will fall back to the local user database if connectivity is lost to the tacacs+ servers.

Cisco strongly recommends that the local keyword is specified within the command.

aaa authentication login VTY group ISE-ISE-TACACS local
aaa authentication enable default group ISE-TACACS enable

line vty 0 4
 login authentication VTY
end

Authorization

TACACS+ Command authorization allows the network device to send a request to ISE when a command is executed. ISE tries to match the request from the network device to list of command sets and allow the command to be executed if there is a match. This is needed to monitor administrator’s actions as well as for audit.
The above steps will merely authenticate a user and provide privilege level 1. For higher levels of privilege, it needs to be explicitly defined:

aaa authorization exec CON none
aaa authorization console
aaa authorization exec VTY group ISE-TACACS local if-authenticated

line con 0
 authorization exec CON
line vty 0 4
 authorization exec VTY
end

aaa authorization config-commands
aaa authorization commands 1 VTY group ISE-TACACS local if-authenticated
aaa authorization commands 15 VTY group ISE-TACACS local if-authenticated

line vty 0 4
 authorization commands 1 VTY
 authorization commands 15 VTY
Accounting

Command accounting sends info about each command executed, which includes the command, the date, and the username. This can be used for audit.

aaa accounting exec default start-stop group ISE-TACACS
aaa accounting commands 1 default start-stop group ISE-TACACS
aaa accounting commands 15 default start-stop group ISE-TACACS

Note
For monitoring and auditing purposes, Cisco recommends that accounting is activated on the NADs

12.11  Administration and Management
Role-Based Access Control (RBAC) allows the admin to define specific roles and groups for users who need access to the ISE admin interface. The default RBAC configuration often suits most customers with only minor changes. The following information details the changes from the default RBAC configuration.
12.11.1  Admin Access Over Command Line Interface (CLI)
A CLI administrator can start and stop the Cisco ISE application, apply software patches and upgrades, reload or shut down the Cisco ISE appliance, and view all system and application logs. Because of the special privileges that are granted to a CLI administrator, we recommend that you protect the CLI administrator credentials and create web-based administrators for configuring and managing Cisco ISE deployments.

Currently, the local admin is being used.

12.12.2  Operational Data Purging
The purging process allows the ability to manage the size of the Monitoring database by specifying how long to retain data. The default is three months. This value is used when the disk space usage threshold for purging (percentage of disk space) is met. For this option, each month consists of 30 days. A default of three months equals 90 days.
Following are the configured Operational Data Purging settings.

ISE 3.3UI Navigation:
Administration > System > Maintenance > Operational Data Purging

Table 143 Data Purging
Attribute    Value
Purge operations data older than X days    90

Figure 177 ISE v3.3 Operational Data Purging

12.12.3  Auditing and Logging Settings
In ISE, system logs are collected at locations called logging targets. Targets refer to the IP addresses of the servers that collect and store logs. You can generate and store logs locally, or you can use the FTP facility to transfer them to an external server. ISE has the following default targets dynamically configured in the loopback addresses of the local system:
•    LogCollector: Default syslog target for the Log Collector
•    ProfilerRADIUSProbe: Default syslog target for the Profiler RADIUS Probe

ISE 3.3UI Navigation: Administration > System > Logging > Local Log Settings

The following table describes syslog message fields.
Table 144 Local Store Syslog Message Format
Field    Description
timestamp    Date of the message generation according to the local clock of the originating Cisco ISE node. It appears in the following format:
YYYY- MM-DD hh:mm:ss:xxx +/-zh:zm.

Possible values include:
•    YYYY = Numeric representation of the year.
•    MM = Numeric representation of the month. For single-digit months (1 to 9), a zero precedes the number.
•    DD = Numeric representation of the day of the month. For single-digit days (1 to 9), a zero precedes the number.
•    hh = The hour of the day (00 to 23).
•    mm = The minute of the hour (00 to 59).
•    ss = The second of the minute (00 to 59).
•    xxx = The millisecond of the second (000 to 999).
•    +/-zh:zm = The time zone offset from the Cisco ISE server’s time zone. In this format, zh is the number of offset hours, zm is the number of minutes of the offset hour, and it is all preceded by a minus or plus sign to indicate the direction of the offset (e.g., +02:00 indicates that the message occurred at the time indicated by the time stamp, and it occurred on a Cisco ISE node that is two hours ahead of the Cisco ISE server’s time zone).
sequence_num    Global counter of each message. If one message is sent to the local store, and the next is sent to the syslog server target, the counter increments by 2. Possible values are 0000000001 to 999999999.
msg_ode    Message code as defined in the logging categories.
msg_sev    Message severity level of a log message.
Navigate to: Administration > System > Logging > Logging Categories
msg_class    Message class. This identifies groups of messages with the same context.
msg_text    English language descriptive text message.
attr=value    Set of attribute-value pairs that provides details regarding the logged event. A comma (,) separates each pair.
•    Attribute names are as defined in the Cisco ISE dictionaries.
•    Values of the Response direction AttributesSet are bundled to one attribute called Response and are enclosed in curly brackets { }. The attribute-value pairs within the Response are separated by semicolons.
•    Example: Response={RADIUSPacketType=AccessAccept; AuthenticationResult=UnknownUser; cisco-av-pair=sga:security-group-tag=0000-00;}

13  Network Management
13.1  Out-of-Band (OOB) Management
13.1.1  Overview
Core42 Private Cloud environment devices will use the Out of Band (BMC) network which is a dedicated environment physically isolated from the Fabric network and provides port-density for all devices (Servers, Switches, Firewalls, etc) which are part of the solution that can be managed out-of-band using dedicated management ports.

Private Cloud environment BMC Network follows a three-tier architecture comprising of BMC Access, Spine Switches and Core Switches. BMC switches are deployed in the data center where the management interfaces of all network devices will connect to.

BMC Spine Switches are deployed for Compute, Object Storage, Block Storage and GPU blocks. For Block Storage and Object Storage blocks, access switches are connected to same Spine Switches. BMC Spine switches have uplink connected to BMC Core switch. Network and Management block access switches are directly connected to Core Switch.

Below are the locations where BMC switches will be deployed.

Table 151 BMC Switch Distribution
Block    Role    Device    Qty (AZ1)    Qty (AZ2)    Qty (AZ3)
BMC    Core    C9500-48Y4C-A    2    2    2
    Spine    C9500-48Y4C-A    6    6    6
    Access    C9300L-48T-4X-E    56    56    56

Below diagram shows the overall architecture of the Private Cloud BMC network.

Figure 180 Overall Architecture of Private Cloud BMC Network

13.1.2  BMC Layer 2 Design
This section captures the layer 2 design details for Core42 BMC Network

Below table lists the individual BMC VLANs that will be used by Core42 respective network components in the BMC Data Center Network.

Table 152 BMC VLANs
VLAN ID    VLAN Name    Purpose
901    net-fab-oob     Network OOB- Fab01
902-905        MGS OOB

999    native-vlan

Trunks
For BMC blocks, the uplinks from BMC Leaf switches to BMC Spine will be configured as trunk ports to carry multiple VLANs over the same link. 802.1Q trunking mode will be used for all the trunk links. Only necessary VLANs will be allowed on any given trunk port, for example, on a trunk link from a leaf switch to the spine switch, only the VLAN’s used on the access switches will be allowed on both sides. The native VLAN will be set to the Quarantine VLAN on both side of the trunks.

Configuration Template 23 Trunk OOB
!
interface <Interface Name>
  switchport mode trunk
  switchport trunk native vlan 999
  switchport trunk allowed <VLAN-List>
!
VTP
In BMC network, VTP is configured in ‘off’ mode. A switch in VTP off mode does not participate in VTP. It does not advertise its VLAN configuration and does not synchronize its VLAN configuration based on received advertisements. A switch configured in VTP off mode does not forward VTP advertisements that they receive out their trunk interfaces also.

Configuration Template 24 VTP BMC
!
vtp mode off
!
Port-Channels
The BMC Spine switches as well as BMC Core switches are configured in StackWise Virtual which allows clustering of both the physical switches together into a single logical entity. This will allow both the leaf switch uplinks connecting to StackWise Virtual core to be configured in a port-channel. Link Aggregation Control Protocol (LACP) is used on all uplinks to negotiate the building of these bundled links.

Configuration Template 25 Port-Channel BMC
!
interface <Physical Interface Name>
  channel-group <Port-Channel ID> mode active
!

Below table shows the port-channel numbers that will be used on BMC Core/Spine switches for connectivity to leaf switches. Port-channel number 10 will be used on all access/spine switches for the core uplink.

Table 153 Port-Channel Numbers
Port-Channel    Purpose
10    Access Switch Uplink / BMC Spine Switch Uplink
11-89    BMC Core/Spine Downlinks
101-109    DCI Uplinks
115-119    Firewall

Spanning Tree
In Core42 BMC network design, StackWise Virtual is used in the BMC Core/Spines with Multi-Chassis Ether-Channel (MEC) port-channels from BMC Spine/Access switches. The setup is aimed at maximizing the use of all available links. This will be achieved by avoiding blocking of ports by spanning-tree, but the use of a spanning tree instance is desired simply to prevent unanticipated loops and potential bridge table corruption in the event of a loop. With the port-channel technologies, we can however count on convergence being non-existent due to individual port channel member link failures being transparent to the STP.

In Core42 BMC network design, Spanning Tree will run purely as a failsafe mechanism. Due to the presence of Stack Wise Virtual MEC Technology, no loops will be built into the topology; however, Rapid Per VLAN Spanning Tree (RPVST+) will run purely as a fail-safe mechanism to avoid loops in the event of misconfiguration, incorrect cabling etc. The default STP mode is Rapid-PVST+ on Catalyst 9000 series switches, hence so no additional configuration is required to set the mode.

The BMC Core switch stack is configured with STP Priority of 8192, so that it becomes the root for the STP. Spine switch stack is configured with priority of 16384 and the Access switches will be configured with priority of 24576.

PortFast feature immediately brings an interface configured as an access or trunk port to the forwarding state from a blocking state, bypassing the listening and learning states. PortFast will be enabled on all the BMC access ports that connects to management port of network devices.

In a valid configuration, PortFast edge-enabled ports do not receive BPDUs. Receiving a BPDU on a Port Fast edge-enabled port means an invalid configuration. BPDU guard can be enabled at the global level on PortFast edge-enabled ports so that spanning tree shuts down ports that are in a PortFast edge-operational state if any BPDU is received on them. In Core42 BMC design, BPDU Guard is enabled globally on all the access switches, so that it will be automatically enabled for all the PortFast configured ports.
Uplink Interface from BMC Core Switches (5/6) towards DCI PE Routers will also be configured for spanning-tree PortFast command.
Root Guard function helps to enforce the root bridge placement in the STP domain. Root Guard is an interface level configuration. When Root Guard is enabled on a port it does not allow that port to become a root port. BPDUs received on a root guard enabled port triggers an STP convergence that makes the designated port to become a root port, that port is put into a root-inconsistent (blocked) state. In Core42 BMC design, Root Guard is configured on the Core/Spine switch downlink ports.

The default STP reference cost for 10Gbps links is considered inadequate as bandwidths which are a multiple of 10Gbps will appear to have the same cost. To avoid this, the Spanning Tree Protocol can use an alternative method for determining path cost, known as the “long” path cost method. The long method uses 32-bit values for port path costs. Long path cost method is configured in on all BMC switches.
Configuration Template 26 Spanning Tree BMC

!
! BMC Core Switch Stack
!
spanning-tree vlan <VLAN Range> priority 8192
spanning-tree portfast edge bpduguard default
spanning-tree pathcost method long
!
interface Port-Channel 11
 spanning-tree guard root
!
! BMC Spine Switch Stack
!
spanning-tree vlan <VLAN Range> priority 16384
spanning-tree portfast edge bpduguard default
spanning-tree pathcost method long
!
interface Port-Channel 11
 spanning-tree guard root
!
!  BMC Access Switches
!
spanning-tree vlan <VLAN Range> priority 24576
spanning-tree portfast edge bpduguard default
spanning-tree pathcost method long
!
interface range GigabitEthernet 1/0/1-48
 spanning-tree portfast
!
!

13.1.3  Overall BMC Layer 3 Design
The following diagram shows the overall Layer 3 logical connectivity design for BMC environment.

As depicted in the diagram below
•    BMC AZ Core switches host the default gateway for the local BMC segments. For NDFC data and management VLANs, the gateway is configured on MGS firewall.
•    BMC Core switches peer with MGS Firewalls over Layer 3 using eBGP routing protocol.
•     These switches also act as L2 path, for the eBGP peering between DCI PE routers and MGS firewalls.
Figure 181 Out-Of-Band Logical L3 Connectivity


13.1.3.1  BMC eBGP Peering
For BMC Network, BMC Spine 5/6 are used as the Core layer. The gateway for all the BMC subnets, except for NDFC VLANs, is configured on BMC Core which are Cisco Catalyst C9500-48Y4C switches in Stack Wise Virtual. The BMC Core is connected to MGS firewalls and are peering using eBGP. The BMC segments will be advertised to the MGS firewall.

MGS firewall has eBGP peering with DCI PE routers, via BMC Core Switches. PC_OOB VRF is used in DCI network, for this peering. Inter-AZ reachability between BMC segments follow this path.

Only local BMC subnets will be advertised from BMC core to MGS Firewall and a default route is received from MGS firewall.

Below is the Layer 3 Design for Core42 Private Cloud BMC Network.

Figure 182 Layer 3 Design of BMC Network


Table below lists the BGP details used for this connectivity.

13.1.4  Management Ports
The management interfaces of network devices in Core42 Data Center will be connected to BMC access switches. The Cisco Catalyst 9000 Series Switches has management port that is referred to as GigabitEthernet0/0 port. The interface is in its own VRF instance. This means that the routing database and protocol exchange are also separate for this interface from the other data network interfaces. By default, the Catalyst 9000 switches use VRF ‘Mgmt-vrf’ for management port.
Configuration Template 27 Management Ports BMC
!
! Catalyst 9000 Switches
!
interface GigabitEthernet 0/0
 vrf forwarding Mgmt-vrf
 ip address <ipv4_addr> <subnet>
!
!

    10.94.216.209    aead7a-a1-gpu-bmc-05
    10.94.216.211    aead7a-a1-gpu-bmc-06
    10.94.216.213    aead7a-a1-gpu-bmc-07
    10.94.216.215    aead7a-a1-gpu-bmc-08
    10.94.216.217    aead7a-a1-gpu-bmc-09
    10.94.216.219    aead7a-a1-gpu-bmc-10
    10.94.216.221    aead7a-a1-gpu-bmc-11
    10.94.216.223    aead7a-a1-gpu-bmc-12
13.1.6  Overall BMC Traffic Flows
13.1.6.1  BMC Flows
Each AZ includes a dedicated BMC management block with 2 BMC Core Switches (SPINE5/6),  6 Spines and 56 access switches. There are one entry and exit point into the BMC management block per AZ, which is through MGS firewall. Gateway for BMC VLANs for the various blocks, except NDFC VLANs, are hosted on BMC Core (Spine 5/6).

Following are the expected flows in this block. Most of the flows expected are north-south (in and out of this block), however for illustration purpose, both intra and inter-vlan flows are included.
•    Intra VLAN traffic flows within access layer if both source and destination are connected on same access or via Spine if different.
•    Inter VLAN traffic flows is routed on BMC Core switch
•    Inter-Block traffic flows towards MGS for specific use-cases like Server IPMI to RHOSO control plane networks takes the MGS F/W exit.
•    External traffic flows from take MGS firewall exit towards DCI PE Routers to reach external networks.

Figure 183 BMC Flows


13.1.6.2  Failure Scenarios
Normal Operations
•    Ops Users from external networks enter BMC networks via MGS firewall to reach the BMC networks.
•    Redundant devices (BMC Core Switch, MGS firewall and DCI PE) provide resiliency during various failure scenario as discussed next.
Node/Link Failures
•    BMC Block can withstand any link failures between Access to Spine, spine to core spine, and to DCI PE routers. Redundant links exist between the layers.
•    Access to BMC Spines is part of port-channel bundle and any link failure will cause the remaining link to carry the traffic
•    BMC Spine/Core, MGS Firewalls and DCI PE layers can withstand node failures. Node Failure at this layer, will cause the redundant devices to take over the traffic flows.
Access Node Failures
•    Any failure at the access layer switches will cause impact to those devices IPMI/mgmt ports connected to the failed switch
•    This is expected as the management ports are typically not redundant except for few devices
AZ Failure
•    AZ level failure could mean multiple component or device failures such as both DCI PE Routers fail, both MGS firewall fail or both BMC Core Switch fail.
•    This is a worst-case scenario. If caused could impact all BMC flows to the affected AZ.
•    The failure only impacts the management plane traffic which is less critical compared to the control or data plane traffic flows.

Figure 184 BMC Flows Failure Scenarios


 
13.2  Switch Operations and Management
13.2.1  Banner and Hostname
Every device on the network needs to be identified using a hostname. Hostnames will follow Core42’s current device naming standard. The hostname will be used as default for the device’s prompt when logged into it.

The configuration of the login banner provides a way to display a message to anyone who accesses the switch. Login banners can be useful to re-emphasize the potential law infringement represented by an unauthorized access to the device in question. It can also be used to provide information about the location of the device, the contact details of the administrator, or a message of the day.

Below banner will be used on the switches used in Private Cloud environment.
Configuration Template 28 Banner
WARNING! Authorized Access Only. All actions will be monitored and recorded. Disconnect immediately if you are not an authorized user!
13.2.2  SSH/Telnet
Cisco NX-OS allows the use of SSH server to enable an SSH client to make a secure, encrypted connection to a Cisco NX-OS device. SSH uses strong encryption for authentication. The SSH server in the Cisco NX-OS software can interoperate with publicly and commercially available SSH clients.

Telnet
It is recommended to not enable telnet service (via feature telnet) as unsecure connection service for network management.

Configuration Template 29 SSH
!
crypto key generate rsa label <hostname> modulus 2048
!
ip access-list acl-vty-access
  10 remark *** c42-vpn-subnet ***
  20 permit tcp 192.168.246.0/24 any eq 22
  30 remark *** cisco-vpn-subnet ***
  40 permit tcp 192.168.245.0/24 any eq 22
  50 remark *** signature-ssl-vpn-subnet ***
  60 permit tcp 192.168.232.0/22 any eq 22
  70 remark *** az1-ndfc-subnet ***
  80 permit tcp 10.92.215.40/29 any eq 22
  90 remark *** az2-ndfc-subnet***
  100 permit tcp 10.93.215.40/29 any eq 22
  110 remark *** az3-ndfc-subnet ***
  120 permit tcp 10.94.215.40/29 any eq 22
  200 deny ip any any
!
line vty
access-class acl-vty-access in
!
13.2.3  User Account password encryption
When username is configured with password, by default sha256 (type5) hash is generated.

Beginning with Cisco NX-OS Release 10.3(1)F, the type 8 (PBKDF2) and type 9 (SCRYPT) password hash is supported on Cisco Nexus 9000 Series switches. Type 9  will be implemented as part security hardening.

In order to generate password hash for type 8 or 9. we can use the following syntax:

Configuration Template 30 Username
username <user> password <password> [pbkdf2 | scrypt] role [role name]
Type 8 password is displayed in configuration as "$nx-pbkdf2" followed by salt and hash. Type 9 password is displayed as "$nx-scrypt" followed by salt and hash.

Clear text passwords cannot include these special characters at the beginning of the password: quotation marks (" or '), vertical bars (|), or right-angle brackets (>). If a password is trivial (such as a short, easy-to-decipher password), the Cisco NX-OS software will reject your password configuration if password-strength checking is enabled. Be sure to configure a strong password. Passwords are case-sensitive.
13.2.4  SNMP
Simple Network Management Protocol (SNMP) is an application-layer protocol that provides a message format for communication between SNMP managers and agents. SNMP provides a standardized framework, and a common language used for the monitoring and management of devices in a network.

It is recommended to use SNMPv3 to provide information to Private Cloud environment network management systems. Networking devices in each fabric will communicates with Private Cloud environment’s network management systems out-of-band via management Ethernet ports on switches.

There are access lists, associated with network management systems to provide the required SNMP access restrictions. Management plane protection configuration allows in-band SNMP access only via management Ethernet ports on switches.

Configuration Template 31 SNMP
!
ip access-list SNMP-ACCESS_IN
 10 remark *** ACL for SNMP Access Control ***
 20 permit udp X.X.X.X/32 any eq snmp
 30 permit udp any eq snmptrap X.X.X.X/32
 40 permit udp X.X.X.X/32 any eq snmp
 50 permit udp udp any eq snmptrap X.X.X.X/32
 100 deny ip any any
!
!
ip access-list extended MANAGEMENT_ACL
  permit udp any any eq bootpc           ! Allow DHCP
  permit icmp any any echo-request       ! Allow ICMP Echo Request (ping inbound)
  permit icmp any any echo-reply         ! Allow ICMP Echo Reply (ping outbound)
  permit tcp any any eq ssh              ! Allow SSH
  permit udp any any eq 161              ! Allow SNMP (UDP 161 inbound)
  permit udp any any eq 162              ! Allow SNMP (UDP 162 outbound)
  permit udp any any eq domain           ! Allow DNS
  permit tcp any any eq 1812             ! Allow AAA (RADIUS)
  permit tcp any any eq 514              ! Allow SYSLOG (UDP 514)
  permit tcp any any eq 6514             ! Allow Secure SYSLOG (TCP 6514)
  permit tcp any any eq 443              ! Allow Southbound API ports (HTTPS)
  permit tcp any any eq 80               ! Allow Southbound API ports (HTTP)
  permit udp any any eq 1812             ! Allow RADIUS flow (UDP 1812)
  permit udp any any eq 1813             ! Allow RADIUS flow (UDP 1813)
  permit udp any any eq 123              ! Allow NTP (UDP 123) inbound
  permit udp any any eq 123 reverse      ! Allow NTP (UDP 123) outbound
  permit ip 192.168.1.0 0.0.0.255 any     Adjust the MGMT subnet
!
snmp-server globalEnforcePriv
!
!--- SNMP Configuration: Ensure the strongest authentication algorithm is used (SHA)
snmp-server community <community-string> ro sha
snmp-server user <username> <role> auth [sha] <password> priv as- 128 <priv-pwd>
!--
!
snmp-server location <location>
snmp-server contact <contact>
!
snmp-server host <SNMP-Server> version 3 priv <username>
snmp-server host <SNMP-Server> use-vrf management
!
snmp-server source-interface trap mgmt0
snmp-server enable traps
!
snmp-server community <community> group network-admin
snmp-server community <community> use-ipv4acl SNMP-ACCESS_IN
!
interface mgmt0
  ip access-group MANAGEMENT_ACL in
  ip access-group MANAGEMENT_ACL out
  vrf management
!

In Fabric, devices will use Authentication and accounting pre-defined group with RADIUS servers in it.

!
ip tacacs source-interface mgmt0
tacacs-server host X.X.X.X key 0 XXXXXXXXXXXXXX timeout 5
tacacs-server host X.X.X.X key 0 XXXXXXXXXXXXXX timeout 5
aaa group server tacacs+ ISE-CLUSTER
    server X.X.X.X
    server X.X.X.X
    source-interface mgmt0
!
tacacs-server directed-request
!
aaa authentication login default group ISE-CLUSTER local
aaa authentication login console local
aaa authentication login error-enable
aaa accounting default group ISE-CLUSTER
aaa authorization config-commands default group ISE-CLUSTER  local
aaa authorization commands default group ISE-CLUSTER  local
aaa accounting default group ISE-CLUSTER
!
13.2.6  Logging
All devices in Fabric send logging massages to logging servers to record changes of network states. Logging level 5 (Normal but significant condition) will used to log network event. Management IP addresses will used as source interface for logging messages

Configuration Template 33 SYSLOG
!
logging logfile LOGFILE.TXT 7 size 4096000
logging server x.x.x.x 5 use-vrf management
logging source-interface mgmt0
logging timestamp milliseconds
!
13.2.7  NTP
Private Cloud infrastructure will use network time protocol (NTP) servers as preferred source for time synchronization. Management IP addresses will be used as source interface for NTP queries. Access-control list will be applied to protect NTP sessions (queries and responses).

Configuring NTP authentication provides assurance that NTP messages are exchanged between trusted NTP peers. Enable authentication for NTP if possible.

NTP access can be controlled by using access groups. Specifically, the types of requests can be specified that the device allows and the servers from which it accepts responses. Without any access groups, NTP access is granted to all devices. With access groups, NTP access is granted only to the remote device whose source IP address passes the access list criteria.
Configuration Template 34 NTP
feature ntp
!
clock timezone GST 4 0
logging level ntp 6
!
ip access-list acl-ntp-peer
  10 remark *** acl for ntp access control ***
  20 permit ip 10.92.168.5/32 any
  30 permit ip 10.93.168.5/32 any
  40 permit ip 10.94.168.5/32 any
!
ntp server 10.92.168.5 use-vrf management
ntp server 10.93.168.5 use-vrf management
ntp server 10.94.168.5 use-vrf management
ntp source-interface mgmt0
ntp logging
ntp access-group peer acl-ntp-peer
!

Network devices should be configured to use accepted name servers in line with C42 Domain Name Service (DNS). This protocol uses DNS servers to resolve assigned names to IP addresses.

ip domain-lookup
ip domain-name <aead.au>
ip name-server X.X.X.X Y.Y.Y.Y
ip domain-list <list>

The configuration below can be implemented to no resolve ‘names’ via DNS from the command line.

no ip domain-lookup

N9K switches use smart licensing using policy starting 10.2(1)F version.

Smart Licensing Using Policy (SLP) is an enhanced version of Smart Licensing, the objective of which is to provide a licensing solution that does not interrupt the operations of the network and to enable a compliance relationship to account for the hardware and software licenses you purchase and use. The process is described as depicted in below Figure.

Figure 185 Smart Licensing Policy


This mode requires switch to push its licensing information report to a licensing server that can be on-prem (CSLU) or on cloud (CSSM) with the possibility to do it online or offline

13.2.10  Control-Plane Policing
While many security policies are aimed at protecting unauthorized access and configuration of network devices, there is a facet of network security that is often overlooked—unintended Denial of Service (DoS) situations caused by the network traffic.

In cases where the device processor must become an active part of the network data plane traffic flow, excessive data could fully consume the available resources, resulting in a situation where the device is unable to perform all necessary Control Plane functions to keep the network operational

The Control Plane Policing (COPP) is a protection mechanism protecting the CPU of the Nexus 9000 platform. the CoPP is declined in 4 pre-defined Flavors:
•    dense
•    strict (default)
•    moderate
•    lenient

NX-OS default strict CoPP will be deployed to the switches in the fabrics as below:

Figure 187 Control Plane Policing


There is a policer for every class with CIR (= throughput max) and BC (= Burst committed). It will define rules of policing and burst limit to let the packet reach the CPU limiting the impact in case of flooding, loop or DDOS attack.

For Private Cloud Fabric, the default class "strict" is the recommended one and will be used.
Configuration Template 36 CoPP
copp profile strict

Changing the CoPP will drop all the flow trying to access the CPU (ISIS, OSPF, ARP etc.) causing an impact of seconds, it is strongly recommended to use GIR and isolate the device before proceeding this change.

13.2.11  Storm-Control
Traffic storm control (also called traffic suppression) allows you to monitor the levels of the incoming broadcast, multicast, and unicast traffic over a 3.9-millisecond interval. During this interval, the traffic level, which is a percentage of the total available bandwidth of the port, is compared with the traffic storm control level that you configured. When the ingress traffic reaches the traffic storm control level that is configured on the port, traffic storm control drops the traffic until the interval ends.

When the traffic exceeds the configured level, you can configure traffic storm control to perform the following optional corrective actions:

•    Shut down—When ingress traffic exceeds the traffic storm control level that is configured on a port, traffic storm control puts the port into the error-disabled state.
•    Trap—You can configure traffic storm control to generate an SNMP trap when ingress traffic exceeds the configured traffic storm control level. The SNMP trap action is enabled by default. However, storm control traps are not rate-limited by default. You can control the number of traps generated per minute by using the snmp-server enable traps storm-control trap-rate Using trap as less disruptive for the active links.

By default, Cisco NX-OS takes no corrective action when traffic exceeds the configured level.
Configuration Template 37 Storm-Control
Interface ethernet x/y | port-channel x
storm-control broadcast level 10
storm-control multicast level 10
storm-control unicast level 10
! Configure storm control action
storm-control action trap

There is not a recommended threshold level because that will depend on the normal BUM traffic on the network. A way to determine that could be to observe counters after a normal day (common/usual traffic flow and amount patterns). As agreed with Core42 team, 10% will be considered for the deployment with action policing and notification.

13.2.12  Loop Detection – IP/MAC move Mechanism
When an endpoint moves, two host route prefixes for the same endpoint are present in the BGP EVPN control plane. The initial prefix is identified by the original VTEP location, and after the move, the prefix is identified by the new VTEP location.

With two prefixes in the control plane present for the same endpoint, a tiebreaker determines the winner. For this purpose, a BGP extended community called the MAC mobility sequence number is added to the endpoint host route advertisement (specifically Route type 2). The sequence number is updated with every endpoint move. In the event the sequence number reaches its maximum possible value, the sequence number wraps around and starts over.

An endpoint may move multiple times over the course of its lifetime within a fabric. Every time the endpoint moves, the VTEP that detects its new location increments the sequence number by 1 and then advertises the host prefix for that endpoint into the BGP EVPN control plane. Because the BGP EVPN information is synced across all the VTEPs, every VTEP is aware of whether an endpoint is coming up for the first time or whether it is an endpoint move event based on the previous endpoint reachability information.
13.2.13  Duplicate MAC Detection
MAC duplication detection is enabled by default in a Nexus 9000 VXLAN fabric on a per-Leaf basis. The feature was implemented per RFC 7432 to control the unlimited incrementing of the BGP EVPN sequence number which would occur if duplicate MAC addresses existed within the VXLAN fabric. A duplicated MAC address host is allowed 5 duplications or moves within 180 seconds; these are the default values; those values can be changed/configured via the cli.

When a host is initially learned in the VXLAN fabric the BGP EVPN mobility sequence number is set to zero (0). The original Leaf this host is connected advertises the host route to other remote VTEPs via BGP EVPN. If another Leaf/VTEP has a host connected with the same MAC address, then this triggers the 5 allowed duplications check. The sequence number will bounce back and forth between the two VTEPs, incrementing and being re-advertised within BGP EVPN.

After the 5th move within 180 seconds, the switch starts a 30 second lock (hold down timer) before checking to see if the duplication still exists (an effort to prevent an increment of the sequence number). This 30 second lock can occur 3 times within 24 hours (this means 5 moves in 180 seconds for 3 times within 24 hours) before the switch permanently locks/freezes the duplicate entry.

Whenever a MAC address is frozen or permanently frozen, a syslog message is written by L2RIB.
The frozen entry can be removed using the “clear mac address-table dynamic address xxxx.xxxx.xxxx” command on the given frozen MAC entry.

Detailed example with mac 0001.0000.1111 and logging level for l2fm set to 5 (instead of 2 default) to see all mac-moves:
! Raise L2FM logging level from defauult value 2 to 5 on N9K
LEAF4(config)#logging level l2fm 5

LEAF4# sh logg | i 0001.0000.1111
LEAF4 %L2FM-4-L2FM_MAC_MOVE2: Mac 0001.0000.1111 in vlan 11 has moved from nve1(1.1.1.60) to Eth1/4
LEAF4 %L2FM-4-L2FM_MAC_MOVE2: Mac 0001.0000.1111 in vlan 11 has moved from Eth1/4 to nve1(1.1.1.60)
LEAF4 %L2FM-4-L2FM_MAC_MOVE2: Mac 0001.0000.1111 in vlan 11 has moved from nve1(1.1.1.60) to Eth1/4

The following command can be run multiple times during the problem to observe the sequence number incrementing for a specific mac-address xxxx.xxxx.xxxx: “show l2route evpn mac all detail | i Flags:|Topology|xxxx.xxxx.xxxx”

LEAF4# show l2route evpn mac all detail | i Flags:|Topology|0001.0000.1111
Topology     Mac Address         Prod     Flags     Seq No  Next-Hops
11         0001.0000.1111     Local  L,     3      Eth1/4 << 3 mac moves observed

To see and clear frozen macs
LEAF4# show l2rib internal permanently-frozen-list
Topology     Mac Address         Frozen time
----------- -------------- ---------------------------
11         0001.0000.1111     Sat Nov 06 09:49:28.307 UTC

LEAF4# clear mac address-table dynamic address 0001.0000.1111

LEAF4# show l2rib internal permanently-frozen-list
Topology Mac Address Frozen time
----------- -------------- ---------------------------

LEAF4#

The following are example commands to help the configuration of the number of VM moves in a specific time interval (seconds) for duplicate MAC-detection:

Configuration Template 38 Example Commands for Duplicate MAC-Detection


13.2.14  Duplicate IP Detection
The concept is very similar to the one explained earlier regarding mac duplicate detection, to control the incrementing of the BGP EVPN sequence number, after the 5th move within 180 seconds, the switch starts a 30 second lock (hold down timer) before checking to see if the duplication still exists (an effort to prevent an increment of the sequence number). This 30 second lock can occur 5 times within 24 hours (this means 5 moves in 180 seconds for 5 times in 24 hours) before the switch permanently freezes the duplicate IP entry.

Whenever an IP address is duplication/freeze is detected, a syslog message is written by HMM.
Below an example; After the sixth HMM duplicate messages: IP address 11.0.0.13 is flagged with DF-Duplicate:

LEAF3# sh logging | i 11.0.0.13
2024 Nov 22 08:49:56 LEAF3 hmm: (CUSTOMER1) [IPv4] Detected a potential duplicate host 11.0.0.13/32
2024 Nov 22 08:50:55 LEAF3 hmm: (CUSTOMER1) [IPv4] Detected a potential duplicate host 11.0.0.13/32
2024 Nov 22 08:51:55 LEAF3 hmm: (CUSTOMER1) [IPv4] Detected a potential duplicate host 11.0.0.13/32

LEAF3# show fabric forwarding ip local-host-db vrf CUSTOMER1
HMM host IPv4 routing table information for VRF CUSTOMER1
Status: *-valid, x-deleted, D-Duplicate, DF-Duplicate and frozen,
c-cleaned in 00:06:06
    Host         MAC Address       SVI     Flags     Physical Interface
DF* 11.0.0.13/32     0001.0000.1313  Vlan11 0x1420201  Ethernet1/1

LEAF3# clear ip arp 11.0.0.13 vrf CUSTOMER1 force-delete
LEAF3# show fabric forwarding ip local-host-db vrf CUSTOMER1

For the initial fabric deployment at Core42 the default duplicate detection parameters/timers will be used. Those can be tuned later if necessary.

The following are example commands to help the configuration of the number of VM moves in a specific time interval (seconds) for duplicate IP-detection:

Configuration Template 39 Example Commands for Duplicate IP-Detection
